Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
The Maxim 'What Has Fallen Cannot Return' – Scope and Criterion
Legal maxim with furuat and a derived criterion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'الساقط لا يعود' to a given right, identify whether it is subject to isqāṭ, and evaluate using the four-condition criterion.

Core Idea
A right that truly falls cannot be resurrected, but some rights never fall.

This passage introduces a key legal maxim in muʿāmalāt: once a right is dropped, it is gone forever. However, not all rights can be dropped. The text provides examples and then a precise criterion to distinguish droppable from non-droppable rights, a tool every mufti needs.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its irrevocability
The maxim 'الساقط لا يعود' means that when a right falls due to a cause, it becomes non-existent and cannot be revived. This is the default rule for rights in muʿāmalāt.
The maxim is from the Majalla (Article 51).
2
Study the examples: debt, detention, easement
The text gives three clear examples: creditor releasing debtor (ibrāʾ), seller delivering goods before payment (dropping detention right), and dropping an easement right. In each case, the right falls irrevocably.
These illustrate the maxim in action.
3
Identify the exceptions
The text clarifies that some rights are not subject to isqāṭ: ownership of physical objects (milk al-ʿayn) and the endowment beneficiary's right to income. These rights do not fall even if the owner attempts to drop them.
They are not exceptions to the maxim; they never fall under its scope.
4
Analyze the four-condition criterion
Shaykh Aḥmad al-Zarqā derived a criterion: a right is droppable if (1) existing at time of dropping, (2) not related to ownership of a ʿayn, (3) owner's interest is exclusive or predominant, (4) not linked to another's right.
This criterion is a product of Hanafi taʿlīl.
5
Apply the criterion to new scenarios
Use the four conditions to test any right: if all are satisfied, the right can be dropped irrevocably; if any condition fails, the dropping is ineffective.
This is a practical tool for a mufti.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷٤/٨١ - القاعدة السابعة عشرة: الساقط لا يعود» (م/٥١). مسقط أي أن ما يسقط من الحقوق بسبب يصبح بسقوطه معدوماً فلا يعود، كما لا يعود المعدوم. فلو أبرأ الدائنُ مدينهُ سَقَط الدين، فلا تمكن استعادته إذا ندم الدائن. - ولو سَلَّم البائعُ المبيعَ للمشتري قبل قبض الثمن سقط حقه في احتباس المبيع لأجل استيفاء الثمن، فلا يعود له حق استرداد المبيع لأجل احتباسه بعد ذلك، وإنما له ملاحقة المشتري بالثمن كما تقدم (ر: ف ٥١ ه و ۱۲/۸۱) - وكذا حق الارتفاق بالمرور أو المسيل لعقار في أرض الغير أو داره لو أسقطه صاحب العقار المرتفق سقط وليس له الرجوع فيه .

الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
تنبيه هام:
هناك حقوق لا تسقط بالإسقاط، فإذا أسقطها صاحبها وتركها كان له أن يطالب بها بعد ذلك، لا لأنها تعود بعد السقوط، بل لأنها لم تسقط، فلا تنافي هذه القاعدة ولا تعتبر مستثناة منها، لأنها لا تدخل تحتها. - فمن ذلك ملكية الأعيان: فقد قدمنا في بحث خصائص الملكية أنها لا تسقط بالإسقاط ؛ وإنما تنتقل بالأسباب الناقلة (ر : ف ٦/٢٥). ومن ذلك حق الموقوف عليه في غلة الوقف فلو أسقطه وتركه - مدة دون مدة التقادم فله أن يعود إلى طلبه لأنه لم يسقط (ر: قواعد الحمزاوي ص / (٢٢٩ .
هذا، ولم يضع فقهاؤنا ضابطاً لما يسقط ومالا يسقط من الحقوق بالإسقاط .
ولكن والدي رحمه الله في شرحه لهذه القاعدة قد استنبط ضابطاً فقهياً لتمييز ما يسقط من الحقوق بالإسقاط وما لا يسقط في الاجتهاد الحنفي، استمده من تعليلات الفقهاء عندما يعللون السقوط وعدمه. فجاء جامعاً مانعاً تنطبق عليه جميع أنواع الحقوق التي تسقط والتي لا تسقط. وخلاصة الضابط أنَّ الحق الذي يسقط بالإسقاط هو ما توافرت فيه صفات أربع :
۱ أن يكون الحق قائماً الإسقاط. فلا حين يصح الإبراء عن دين مثلاً قبل نشوئه.
۲ - أن يكون غير متعلق بتملك عين كما أسلفنا .
٣- أن تكون مصلحة صاحبه فيه خالصة أو غالبة.
۴ - أن لا يتعلق به حق الغير. فلا يسقط حق الزوجة في المطالبة بنفقتها بإسقاطها له لأنه للبنين . وكما أن حق متولي الوقف ووصي اليتيم في التصرف بإدارة الوقف وأموال لا يسقط بالإسقاط، لأنهما لا يعملان لأنفسهما .
74/81 - The seventeenth rule: What has fallen cannot return (M/51). That is, when a right falls due to a cause, it becomes non-existent by its fall and does not return, just as the non-existent does not return. So if the creditor releases the debtor, the debt falls; it cannot be reclaimed if the creditor regrets. And if the seller delivers the sold item to the buyer before receiving the price, his right to detain the sold item for the price falls; he cannot thereafter reclaim the sold item for detention, but he can pursue the buyer for the price as previously stated (see: F 51 and 12/81). Similarly, an easement right of passage or drainage for a property in another's land or house: if the owner of the benefiting property drops it, it falls and he cannot retract it. [PAGE_964] Chapter (81) Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification. Important Notice: There are rights that are not extinguished by dropping. If their owner drops them and abandons them, he can later demand them again, not because they return after falling, but because they never fell. So this does not contradict this rule and is not considered an exception from it, because it does not fall under it. Among these: ownership of corporeal property: we have previously stated in the discussion of the characteristics of ownership that it is not extinguished by dropping; it is only transferred by transferring causes (see: F 6/25). And among them: the right of the endowment beneficiary (mawqūf ʿalayh) in the income of the endowment; if he drops it and abandons it for a period less than the statute of limitations, he can return to claim it because it did not fall (see: Qawāʿid al-Ḥamzāwī, p. 229). Now, our jurists did not formulate a precise criterion (ḍābiṭ) for what falls and what does not fall by dropping. But my father, may Allah have mercy on him, in his commentary on this rule, derived a juristic criterion for distinguishing what falls by dropping from what does not fall in the Hanafi school, drawing from the jurists' rationales when they explain falling or non-falling. It came comprehensive and exclusive, applying to all types of rights that fall and those that do not. The summary of the criterion is that a right that falls by dropping is one that possesses four characteristics: 1. The right must be existing at the time of dropping. So, for example, release (ibrāʾ) of a debt before its creation is not valid. 2. It must not be related to ownership of a corporeal thing, as we noted. 3. The benefit of its owner in it must be exclusive or predominant. 4. It must not be linked to the right of another. So a wife's right to claim maintenance does not fall by her dropping it because it is for the children. And similarly, the right of the endowment trustee and the guardian of an orphan to manage the endowment and the orphan's property does not fall by dropping because they do not act for themselves.
Source pages p. 963

Commentary
This passage presents the maxim 'What has fallen cannot return' (al-sāqiṭ lā yaʿūd), a fundamental rule in Hanafi fiqh, especially in transactions. The text provides examples: remission of debt (ibrāʾ) is irrevocable; delivery of sold item before payment extinguishes the seller's right of retention (ḥaqq al-ḥabs); dropping an easement right is final. Then comes an important note (tanbīh) clarifying that some rights are inherently unsusceptible to being dropped, such as ownership of physical objects (milk al-ʿayn) and the right of an endowment beneficiary (mawqūf ʿalayh) to income. These rights are not revived after dropping; they simply never fell because they are not subject to isqāṭ. The passage concludes with a remarkable juristic criterion (ḍābiṭ) derived by the author's father (Shaykh Aḥmad al-Zarqā) that specifies four conditions for a right to be droppable: (1) existence of the right at the time of dropping, (2) not pertaining to ownership of a physical thing, (3) the owner's interest is exclusive or predominant, and (4) not connected to a third party's right. This criterion is a good example of Hanafi taʿlīl (causal reasoning) and serves as a tool for muftis in ijtihād.

Key Terms
إسقاط (isqāṭ): The act of a right-holder voluntarily extinguishing his own right

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following rights can be dropped according to the criterion?
A) Ownership of a car
B) Right to walk across a neighbor's land (easement)
C) Right of a wife to maintenance
D) Right of a guardian to manage an orphan's property

Key Takeaway
The maxim الساقط لا يعود governs most rights in muʿāmalāt, but the four-condition criterion is essential to determine which rights are truly subject to isqāṭ.

The Criterion for Renunciation of Rights
Four-condition criterion for renunciation of rights

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the four-condition Hanafi criterion to determine whether a given right can be dropped by renunciation (isqat).

Core Idea
A right drops by renunciation only if it exists, is not tied to physical property, the holder's interest is pure or dominant, and no unlawful result follows.

This passage presents a refined Hanafi criterion for distinguishing rights that can be dropped by renunciation from those that cannot. It was derived by Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa from the reasoning of earlier jurists. Understanding this criterion is essential for muftis when assessing the validity of renunciation in various legal contexts.

Guided Path
1
Check existence of the right
The right must exist at the time of renunciation. A future right (e.g., a debt not yet created) cannot be dropped.
2
Check connection to physical ownership
Rights connected to ownership of a physical object (milk al-'ayn) cannot be dropped. Only rights in abstract obligations or actions are eligible.
3
Assess the holder's interest
The interest of the right-holder must be pure (for themselves) or predominant. If the right is held primarily for others or for public interest, it cannot be dropped.
4
Check for unlawful result
If dropping the right leads to a legally prohibited outcome (like pure harm or excessive uncertainty), the renunciation is ineffective.
The author distinguishes between mortgage (gharar) and lease (continuous renewal) in the example of buyer's option.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
تنبيه هام:
هناك حقوق لا تسقط بالإسقاط، فإذا أسقطها صاحبها وتركها كان له أن يطالب بها بعد ذلك، لا لأنها تعود بعد السقوط، بل لأنها لم تسقط، فلا تنافي هذه القاعدة ولا تعتبر مستثناة منها، لأنها لا تدخل تحتها. - فمن ذلك ملكية الأعيان: فقد قدمنا في بحث خصائص الملكية أنها لا تسقط بالإسقاط ؛ وإنما تنتقل بالأسباب الناقلة (ر : ف ٦/٢٥). ومن ذلك حق الموقوف عليه في غلة الوقف فلو أسقطه وتركه - مدة دون مدة التقادم فله أن يعود إلى طلبه لأنه لم يسقط (ر: قواعد الحمزاوي ص / (٢٢٩).
هذا، ولم يضع فقهاؤنا ضابطاً لما يسقط ومالا يسقط من الحقوق بالإسقاط .
ولكن والدي رحمه الله في شرحه لهذه القاعدة قد استنبط ضابطاً فقهياً لتمييز ما يسقط من الحقوق بالإسقاط وما لا يسقط في الاجتهاد الحنفي، استمده من تعليلات الفقهاء عندما يعللون السقوط وعدمه. فجاء جامعاً مانعاً تنطبق عليه جميع أنواع الحقوق التي تسقط والتي لا تسقط. وخلاصة الضابط أنَّ الحق الذي يسقط بالإسقاط هو ما توافرت فيه صفات أربع :
۱ أن يكون الحق قائماً حين الإسقاط. فلا يصح الإبراء عن دين مثلاً قبل نشوئه.
۲- أن يكون غير متعلق بتملك عين كما أسلفنا .
٣- أن تكون مصلحة صاحبه فيه خالصة أو غالبة. فحق متولي الوقف ووصي اليتيم في التصرف بإدارة الوقف وأموال لا يسقط بالإسقاط، لأنهما لا يعملان لأنفسهما . وعقوبات الحدود لا تسقط بإسقاط المجني عليه أو ولي الأمر، لأنها حق الله الذي يسمى : الحق العام. بخلاف القصاص فإنه يسقط بالإسقاط، لأنَّ حق الشخص فيه هو الغالب أي حقه في نوع العقوبة، أما أصل العقوبة فهي حق عام يمارسه السلطان لمصلحة الجماعة. فلا يسقط بإسقاط المجني عليه أو وليه) . فبتنازل صاحب حق القصاص عن حقه تنتقل عقوبة الجاني إلى التعزير كما تقدم في نظرية المؤيدات (ر: ف ١٥/٤٩).
٤- أن لا يترتب على إسقاط الحق نتيجة غير مشروعة. فلو باع شخص شيئاً كان قد آجره أو رهنه قبل البيع، فللمشتري الخيار في أن يفسخ البيع ويسترد الثمن أو ينتظر انقضاء مدة الإجارة أو فكاك الرهن ليتسلم المبيع. فلو أسقط حقه في هذا الخيار لا يسقط على الراجح من الآراء (ر ) رد المحتار باب التصرف في الرهن) وذلك لأنه لو سقط خياره لبقي حقه في تسلم المبيع معلقاً، وذلك ضرر محض غير مشروع. فمن المبادىء الشرعية أن الإنسان لا يجبر على تحمل الضرر المحض الذي لا يقتضيه العقد ولو رضي به (ر : الدرر باب البيع الفاسد (۱) (170/2)
(۱) الذي أرى أن التعليل بالنتيجة غير المشروعة إنما ينطبق على حالة المبيع المرهون لا المأجور. أ - ففي الرهن قد يباع المرهون قضائياً لاستيفاء الدين، فإذا صح إسقاط المشتري السابق حقه في الفسخ يصبح حقه في تسلم المبيع حقاً احتمالياً محضاً، وهذا غرر فاحش غير مشروع . ب- أما الإجارة فإن لها مدة محددة تنتهي فيها فلا غرر فالتعليل الصحيح عندي لعدم صحة إسقاط المشتري حقه في فسخ شرائه إذا تبين أن المبيع مأجور هو الإجارة عند فقهائنا من العقود الزمنية، أي المستمرة ( ر : ف ١٤/٤٧) ويصرحون بأنها بمثابة عقود متجددة آناً فاناً. فإذا أسقط المشتري حقه في فسخ الشراء تجدّد له حق جديد في الفسخ بالتجدد المفترض في عقد الإجارة.
Chapter (81): Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification. Important note: There are rights that cannot be dropped by renunciation (isqat). So if their owner renounces and abandons them, he may demand them afterwards, not because they return after falling, but because they did not fall; thus this does not conflict with this rule and they are not considered exceptions to it, because they do not fall under it. - Among these is ownership of real property: we have previously stated in the discussion on the characteristics of ownership that it cannot be dropped by renunciation; rather it is transferred only by transferring causes (cf. F 6/25). Also among these is the right of the beneficiary (mawquf 'alayh) to the revenue of the waqf; if he renounces and abandons it for a period less than the statute of limitations, he may return to claim it because it did not drop (cf. Qawa'id al-Hamzawi p. 229). Now, our jurists did not set a criterion for what drops and what does not drop from rights by renunciation. But my father, may God have mercy on him, in his commentary on this rule, extracted a fiqh criterion to distinguish what drops from what does not drop in the Hanafi ijtihad, deriving it from the reasoning (ta'lilat) of the jurists when they explain dropping and non-dropping. So it came comprehensive and preventive, applying to all types of rights that drop and those that do not. The summary of the criterion is that the right that drops by renunciation is the one that possesses four characteristics: 1) The right must exist at the time of renunciation. So it is not valid to forgive a debt, for example, before its creation. 2) It must not be connected to ownership of a physical object ('ayn), as we have mentioned. 3) The interest of its owner in it must be pure or predominant. So the right of the administrator (mutawalli) of a waqf and the guardian (wasi) of an orphan to act in managing the waqf and funds does not drop by renunciation, because they do not act for themselves. And prescribed penalties (hudud punishments) do not drop by renunciation of the victim or the ruler, because they are the right of Allah, called 'public right'. Contrary to retaliation (qisas), it does drop by renunciation, because the personal right in it is predominant—i.e., his right regarding the type of punishment, while the original punishment is a public right exercised by the ruler for the interest of the community. It does not drop by renunciation of the victim or his guardian. So when the owner of the right of qisas relinquishes his right, the criminal's punishment transfers to discretionary punishment (ta'zir), as previously discussed in the theory of sanctions (cf. F 15/49). 4) No unlawful result should follow from the renunciation of the right. So if a person sells something that he had previously leased or mortgaged before the sale, the buyer has the option to rescind the sale and reclaim the price, or to wait until the lease period ends or the mortgage is released to take possession of the sold item. If he renounces his right in this option, it does not drop according to the preponderant opinion (cf. Radd al-Muhtar, chapter on transactions in mortgage), because if his option dropped, his right to take possession of the sold item would remain suspended, which is pure unlawful harm. It is a Sharia principle that a person cannot be forced to bear pure harm not required by the contract, even if he consents (cf. al-Durar, chapter on invalid sale (1) 170/2). (1) In my view, the reasoning based on unlawful result applies only to the case of the sold item that is mortgaged, not the leased one. A) In mortgage, the mortgaged item may be sold judicially to satisfy the debt; so if the prior buyer's option to rescind is validly dropped, his right to take possession becomes merely probable, which is excessive, unlawful uncertainty (gharar). B) As for lease, it has a fixed period that ends, so there is no gharar. The correct reasoning in my view for the invalidity of the buyer renouncing his option to rescind the purchase when it becomes clear that the sold item is leased is that lease, in our jurists' view, is a temporal contract (i.e., continuous) (cf. F 14/47), and they state that it is like contracts renewed moment by moment. So if the buyer renounces his right to rescind the purchase, a new right to rescind is renewed for him with the presumed renewal in the lease contract.
Source pages p. 964

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the rule that some rights cannot be dropped by renunciation (isqat). The author begins by affirming that certain rights, such as ownership of physical property and the beneficiary's right to waqf revenue, cannot be dropped even if the holder renounces them; they simply do not fall under the general rule. He then notes that Hanafi jurists had not formulated a general criterion for distinguishing between rights that drop and those that do not. However, the author's father, Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa, extracted such a criterion from the reasoning (ta'lilat) of the jurists. The criterion states that a right drops if it meets four conditions: (1) it must exist at the time of renunciation, (2) it must not be connected to ownership of a physical object, (3) the holder's interest must be pure or predominant, and (4) no unlawful result must follow its renunciation. The author illustrates each condition with examples: condition 1 excludes forgiveness of a future debt; condition 2 excludes ownership rights; condition 3 excludes the rights of waqf administrators and guardians (because they act for others) and hudud punishments (public right), while qisas can be dropped because the personal interest is predominant; condition 4 explains why a buyer's option to rescind a sale of a previously leased or mortgaged item cannot be dropped—because it would lead to pure harm or uncertainty. The author then adds his own observation that the reasoning of condition 4 applies differently to mortgage (due to gharar) and lease (due to the continuous nature of the ijarah, causing the option to renew). Thus, this passage provides a nuanced, practical criterion for muftis and jurists assessing the effect of renunciation on various rights.

Key Terms
الإسقاط (al-isqat): The act of voluntarily renouncing or dropping a right by its owner.
الحق (al-haqq): A legally recognized entitlement of a person or the community, including rights of Allah (public rights) and personal rights.
الضابط (al-dabit): A fiqh criterion that encapsulates the conditions under which a ruling applies; here, for determining which rights can be dropped.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the criterion, why cannot a waqf administrator (mutawalli) drop his right to manage the waqf?
A) Because the right is connected to ownership of physical property.
B) Because the administrator's interest in the right is not pure or predominant; he acts for the benefit of others.
C) Because the right does not exist at the time of renunciation.
D) Because dropping it would result in an unlawful outcome.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school uses a four-condition criterion to determine if a right can be dropped by renunciation, ensuring that only truly personal, harmless rights are extinguished.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
beginner
6 min
Maxim: Donations Are Not Complete Without Delivery
Presents the maxim that donation contracts require delivery for completion, listing examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim that donation contracts require delivery for completion and apply it to identify when a gift, loan for use, or deposit becomes binding.

Core Idea
A donation is not binding until the item is delivered—delivery completes the contract.

In Hanafi law, not all contracts become binding upon offer and acceptance. Contracts of donation (tabarru'āt) such as gifts, loans for use, and deposits require physical delivery of the item to be considered complete. This lesson explains the 18th maxim of the Majalla, which codifies this principle, and illustrates it with practical examples.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Read the maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' and its English translation. Understand that it means a donation is not completed except by delivery.
2
Identify the scope
Recognize that this maxim applies to contracts of tabarru' (gratuitous transfers) such as gift (hibah), loan for use (i'arah), and deposit (wadi'ah).
These are called 'real contracts' (عقود عينية) in contrast to consensual contracts.
3
Examine each furu'
For each contract type, note how delivery functions: in hibah, the gift is not binding until the item is handed over; in i'arah, the borrower's right to use arises upon delivery; in wadi'ah, the deposit is not complete until the item is received by the depositee.
The common thread: without qabd, the contract is not fully concluded.
4
Determine legal effect
Understand that until delivery, the donor or lender may revoke the offer. The maxim ensures that ownership or usufruct does not transfer without actual possession.
This protects the donor's right to change their mind before parting with the item.

Source Text

الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها ٧٥/٨١ - القاعدة الثامنة عشرة» : «لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض . فالهبة وسائر العقود العينية الأخرى كالإعارة والإيداع، يعتبر تسليم العين فيها عنصراً متمماً لانعقاد العقد، لا مجرد تنفيذ له. وقد تقدم إيضاح ذلك في مناسبته من نظرية العقود (ر: ف ۱۳/۳۰). (م/ ۹۸)
Chapter (81) Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification. 75/81 - The Eighteenth Rule: 'Donation is not completed except by delivery.' Thus, gift and all other real contracts such as loan for use (i'arah) and deposit (wadi'ah), the handing over of the item is considered a completing element for the conclusion of the contract, not merely its execution. This has been explained previously in its proper place in the theory of contracts (see: F 13/30). (Majalla Art. 98)
Source pages p. 966

Commentary
This is the 18th of the 99 general maxims of the Majalla. It states that contracts of donation (tabarru'at) such as gift (hibah), loan for use (i'arah), and deposit (wadi'ah) are not perfected until the item is physically delivered to the recipient. Delivery is not merely the execution of an already binding contract; rather, it is a condition for the contract to be concluded ('anqad). This is a distinctive feature of 'real contracts' in Hanafi law, as opposed to consensual contracts where the contract becomes binding upon offer and acceptance alone. The author refers to an earlier discussion on the theory of contracts.

Key Terms
التبرع (al-tabarru'): A gratuitous contract where one party gives something without compensation, e.g., gift.
القبض (al-qabd): Physical taking possession or delivery of the item.

Key Takeaway
Delivery (qabd) is an essential element for the completion of donation contracts in Hanafi law.

Maxim: Change of Title Cause Stands for Change of Substance
Presents a maxim with two applications and a comparative law note.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'change of cause of ownership stands in place of change of substance' to determine when a prior legal right is extinguished upon reacquisition.

Core Idea
Reacquiring property through a new cause severs old rights as if the property itself changed.

This lesson introduces a crucial Hanafi maxim that addresses a common legal scenario: when a person reacquires an item they previously owned through a new transaction, the law treats the item as if it has been transformed, thereby eliminating certain prior claims. We will examine two concrete applications from sales and gifts, and see how this principle mirrors modern legal concepts.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its core idea
Read the maxim: 'تبدل سبب الملك كتبدل الذات' (Change of the cause of ownership stands in place of change of substance). Understand that it means a new legal cause (e.g., a new purchase) effectively resets the status of the owned item.
The maxim is from article 76 of the Ottoman Majalla.
2
Apply to the first furu' (sale with defect)
Consider the scenario: A buys an item, sells it to C, then buys it back from C. Later, A discovers an old defect from his first purchase. The maxim prevents A from returning the item to the original seller because A's current ownership is based on the second purchase, not the first. The item is considered 'new' in legal terms.
The option of defect (khiyar al-ayb) is lost due to the change in cause.
3
Apply to the second furu' (gift revocation)
Consider: A gives a gift to B. B sells or gives it to C, then later reacquires it through a new cause (e.g., purchase). The original donor A's right to revoke the gift is cut off because B's current ownership is via a new cause.
The right of revocation (ruju') in hibah is extinguished.
4
Compare to the French theory of purge
Note the parallel: French law holds that a good-faith purchase from a public auction or commercial establishment clears the item of prior real rights. This is conceptually similar to the Hanafi maxim.
This comparison illustrates the universality of the principle.
5
Formulate a general rule for application
The maxim applies whenever ownership returns to a person through a new legal cause, regardless of whether the item physically changed. The key is the novelty of the cause.
If the person reacquired without a new cause (e.g., by nullification of the prior transfer), the maxim may not apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۷۶/۸۱ - القاعدة التاسعة عشرة: تبدل سبب الملك كتبدل الذات هذه القاعدة صيغتها في المجلة هكذا تبدل سبب الملك قائم مقام تبدل الذات أي كتبدل الشيء المملوك. وعلى هذا فَرَّع الفقهاء: - أن المشتري إذا باع المبيع، ثم اشتراه ثانيةً من مشتريه، ثم ظهر فيه عيب قديم كان موجوداً فيه عند بائعه الأول، فليس له أن يرده عليه بحكم خيار العيب، لأن المبيع قد عاد إلى ملكه بسبب جديد هو شراؤه الثاني. فليس ملكه الحاضر مستنداً إلى شرائه الأول الذي يريد فسخه الآن فيعتبر هذا الشيء الآن كأنه غير الشيء الذي كان اشتراه من البائع الأول، لتبدل سبب الملك ر ف ٢/٤٥) . - وكذا لو باع الموهوب له الشيء الموهوب، أو وهبه لغيره، ثم عاد إلى ملكه بشراء أو هبة أو أي سبب جديد آخر، انقطع حق الواهب الأول من الرجوع في الهبة، كأنما الشيء الحاضر غير الموهوب السابق. وفي الفقه الأجنبي الحديث ما يشبه هذه القاعدة. فنظرية التطهير : purge في القانون الفرنسي تقاربها في الفكرة. وبمقتضاها لديهم يعتبر تملك العين بسبب فيه علنية وحسن نية - كشرائها بالمزاد العلني مثلاً، أو من محل تجاري معروضة فيه - مطهراً لها، أي يحررها من كل حق عيني كان لأحدٍ عليها فيبقى لصاحب الحق العيني ملاحقة المسؤول عنه بالتعويض ( ر ) موجز الحقوق الفرنسية لدالوزج ف ۱۱۰۷ - ١١١٤).
76/81 - The Nineteenth Rule: Change of the cause of ownership stands in place of change of substance. This rule is phrased in the Majalla as: 'Change of the cause of ownership takes the place of change of substance,' i.e., like a change in the owned thing. Upon this, the jurists derived: - That if a buyer sells the sold item, then buys it again from his buyer, and then an old defect appears that existed at the first seller, he cannot return it to the first seller by virtue of the option of defect, because the item has returned to his ownership by a new cause, his second purchase. Thus, his current ownership is not based on his first purchase which he now wants to rescind, so this thing now is considered as if it is not the same thing he bought from the first seller, due to change of the cause of ownership (see: F 2/45). - Similarly, if the donee sells the gifted item, or gives it as a gift to someone else, then it returns to his ownership by purchase, gift, or any other new cause, the right of the first donor to revoke the gift is cut off, as if the present thing is not the previous gifted item. In modern foreign fiqh, there is something similar to this rule. The theory of purge in French law approximates it in idea. According to it, acquiring an item by a cause that involves publicity and good faith—such as buying it at a public auction, or from a commercial establishment where it is displayed—is considered a purification of it, i.e., it releases it from any real right that someone had on it, and the holder of the real right is left to pursue the responsible party for compensation (see: Mukhtasar al-Huquq al-Faransiyyah li-Daloz, no. 1107-1114).
Source pages p. 966

Commentary
This maxim is the 19th of the Majalla's general maxims. It addresses the situation where a person reacquires an item through a new legal cause (e.g., a second purchase) after having previously owned it. The principle is that the change in the cause of ownership effectively treats the item as if it has been transformed into a different object, thereby severing previous legal rights that were attached to the earlier ownership. Two applications are given: (1) In sales, if a buyer sells an item, then buys it back, and then discovers a latent defect from the first sale, he cannot return it to the original seller because the item returned to his ownership via a new cause (the second purchase). (2) In gifts, if a donee disposes of the gift and later reacquires it through a new cause, the original donor's right to revoke the gift is extinguished. The author also notes a parallel in modern French law, where the theory of 'purge' (purification) states that a good-faith purchase from a public auction or a commercial establishment clears the item of any prior real rights, shifting the right to compensation.

Key Terms
سبب الملك (sabab al-milk): The legal cause or transaction through which ownership of an item is acquired (e.g., sale, gift, inheritance).
خيار العيب (khiyar al-'ayb): The option to rescind a sale due to a latent defect that existed before the sale.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'change of title cause stands for change of substance', if a buyer sells a car and then buys it back from the same buyer, and then discovers a defect that existed when he first owned it, what is the ruling?
A) He can return it to the first seller because the defect predates his first purchase.
B) He cannot return it to the first seller because the car is now owned via a new purchase, treated as a different car.
C) He can only return it if he obtains the second seller's consent.
D) He must keep the car because the defect is his own fault.

Key Takeaway
A change in the legal cause of ownership functions like a transformation of the object itself, severing old rights.

Qa'ida 18: Charitable Contracts Require Delivery
Explaining the maxim that donation contracts are not complete without delivery

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض', apply it to gift, loan, and deposit contracts, and identify when possession is required for completion.

Core Idea
A donation is not binding until possession is taken; the giver may revoke before delivery.

This lesson explores a fundamental maxim of Hanafi contract law: gratuitous (tabarru') contracts such as gifts (hibah), loans for use (i'arah), and deposits (wadi'ah) are not fully concluded until the recipient takes physical possession. Unlike sales, where offer and acceptance transfer ownership, these contracts are considered weak (da'if) and require delivery for perfection. Understanding this rule is essential for muftis when advising on disputes over promised donations.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
The maxim is: 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' (A donation is not completed except by taking possession). Identify that it applies to all gratuitous contracts (hibah, i'arah, wadi'ah) and not to commutative contracts like sale.
The term 'tabarru'' includes any transfer without compensation.
2
Explain the rationale for the rule
Hanafi jurists classify tabarru'at as 'weak' contracts because the donor receives no consideration. To prevent disputes and encourage finality, delivery (qabd) is elevated to a condition for completion (tamam), not just execution.
Contrast with sale (bay') where the contract is binding upon offer and acceptance.
3
Apply the maxim to the listed furuat
For hibah: the donor may revoke before the donee takes possession. For i'arah: the lender may withdraw before delivery. For wadi'ah: the deposit is not concluded until the depositee receives the item.
Each case highlights the common element: possession perfects the contract.
4
Identify practical consequences for fatwa
If a person promises a gift but does not deliver, no legal claim arises. If the donor dies before delivery, the gift is void. Similarly, a lending arrangement can be cancelled before the borrower takes the item.
These implications guide muftis in resolving real-life disputes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
٧٥/٨١ - القاعدة الثامنة عشرة» : «لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض . فالهبة وسائر العقود العينية الأخرى كالإعارة والإيداع، يعتبر تسليم العين فيها عنصراً متمماً لانعقاد العقد، لا مجرد تنفيذ له. وقد تقدم إيضاح ذلك في مناسبته من نظرية العقود (ر: ف ۱۳/۳۰). (م/ ۹۸)
75/81 - The Eighteenth Rule: 'A donation is not completed except by taking possession.' So hibah (gift) and other real contracts such as i'arah (lending for use) and wadi'ah (deposit) – delivery of the item is considered a completing element for the contract's formation, not mere execution. This has already been explained in its appropriate place in the theory of contracts (ref: part 13/30). (Majalla article 98).
Source pages p. 966

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental principle of Hanafi contract law: unilateral beneficial contracts (tabarru'at) require delivery (qabd) for their perfection. Unlike commutative contracts, where offer and acceptance suffice to transfer ownership, in hibah (gift), i'arah (loan for use), and wadi'ah (deposit) the contract is not fully concluded until the object is physically handed over. This is because these contracts, being gratuitous, are considered weak (da'if) and need additional formality to prevent disputes. The rule is from the Majalla (article 98) and is essential for understanding the Hanafi distinction between 'aqd (contract) and tamlik (transfer).

Key Terms
تبرع (tabarru'): A gratuitous act or donation, including hibah (gift) and any contract done without compensation.
قبض (qabd): Physical or constructive taking possession of the object of the contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'لا يتم التبرع إلا بالقبض' means that gratuitous contracts in Hanafi fiqh are not binding until the recipient takes possession.

Conditional Promises Are Binding
Explaining that promises made in conditional form become enforceable

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim that conditional promises are binding to determine the enforceability of a promise in a given scenario.

Core Idea
A bare promise is not court-enforceable, but a conditional promise becomes a binding commitment.

The Hanafi school distinguishes between a bare promise (wa'd) and a conditional commitment (ta'liq). While fulfilling a bare promise is recommended (diyanatan), it is not legally enforceable (qada'an). However, when a promise is made conditional, it becomes binding. This rule protects the promisee from deception and upholds the sanctity of conditional undertakings. The derived rule from the twentieth maxim is elaborated by Ibn Nujaym and the Majalla.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its wording
First, locate the maxim: 'المواعيد بصورة التعاليق تكون لازمة' (Promises in the form of conditions are binding). This is the core legal rule we will analyse.
The maxim is derived from the twentieth maxim on conditions.
2
Understand the default rule regarding promises
Recognise the default Hanafi position: a bare promise (wa'd) is not legally binding (qada'an) but its fulfilment is recommended (diyanatan). No right arises for the promisee, and he cannot compel execution by judicial force.
This default applies to promises of loans, sales, gifts, rescission, release, etc.
3
Recognise the transformation caused by ta'liq
Notice that when a promise is made conditional (mu'allaq 'ala shart), it exits the meaning of a bare promise and acquires the garment of commitment and undertaking. The promise becomes binding to avoid deceiving the promisee.
Ibn Nujaym states: 'A promise is not binding unless it is conditional.'
4
Apply the maxim to a concrete scenario
Consider a promise: 'If you come to me today, I will give you a loan.' The promisee comes, but the promisor refuses. Apply the maxim: because the promise was conditional, it is binding; the promisee can compel execution.
This is a furu' application of the maxim.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد
۷۸/۸۱ - المواعيد بصورة التعاليق تكون لازمة» (م/ ٨٤) . الأصل في الوعد أنه لا يلزم صاحبه قضاء وإن كان الوفاء به مطلوباً
فلو وعد شخص آخر بقرض أو ببيع أو بهبة أو بفسخ أو بإبراء أو بأي عمل حقوقي آخر لا ينشأ بذلك حق للموعود، فليس له أن يجبره على تنفيذه بقوة القضاء
غير أن فقهاء الحنفية لحظوا أنَّ الوعد إذا صَدَر معلقاً على شرط فإنه يخرج عن معنى الوعد المجرد ويكتسي ثوب الالتزام والتعهد فيصبح عندئذ ملزماً لصاحبه (ز) شرح العلامة علي حيدر على المجلة). وذلك فيما يظهر اجتناباً لتغرير الموعود بعدما خرج الوعد مخرج التعهد . وقد قال ابن نجيم في الحظر والإباحة من «الأشباه» ۲/ ۱۱۰ : «لا يلزم الوعد إلا إذا كان معلقاً».
What branches off from it among the rules: 78/81 - 'Promises in the form of conditions are binding' (Majalla article 84). The principle regarding a promise is that it is not binding upon its giver legally (qada'an), though fulfilling it is required (diyanatan). So if a person promises another a loan, sale, gift, rescission, release, or any other legal act, no right arises for the promisee, and he cannot compel its execution by judicial force. However, Hanafi jurists observed that if the promise is made conditional on a condition, it exits the meaning of a bare promise and acquires the garment of commitment and undertaking, thus becoming binding on its giver (ref: Commentary of Allama Ali Haydar on the Majalla). This appears to be to avoid deceiving the promisee after the promise has been made in the form of an undertaking. Ibn Nujaym said in 'Al-Ashbah' in the section on permissibility and prohibition (2/110): 'A promise is not binding unless it is conditional.'
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage introduces a derived rule that branches from the twentieth maxim on conditions. The general rule is that a promise (wa'd) is not legally enforceable (qada'an) in Hanafi law, though its fulfillment is commendable (diyanatan). However, if the promise is expressed in conditional form (e.g., 'If you do X, I will give you Y'), it transforms into a binding commitment. The Hanafi jurists, including Ibn Nujaym, state that such a conditional promise is binding because it has the appearance of a ta'liq (suspension), which creates legal consequences. This prevents the promisee from being deceived. The author references Ali Haydar's commentary and Ibn Nujaym's Al-Ashbah. This rule is significant in modern contexts where promises are often made conditional.

Key Terms
وعد (wa'd): A promise to do something in the future.
تعليق (ta'liq): Suspension of a legal effect on a future condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Ali promises Zaid: 'If you return my book tomorrow, I will give you $100.' Zaid returns the book, but Ali refuses to pay. According to Hanafi fiqh, what is the ruling?
A) Ali is not obligated because a promise is never binding.
B) Ali is obligated because the promise was conditional.
C) Zaid can only seek compensation if he suffered loss.
D) The promise is invalid because it involves a future condition.

Key Takeaway
Conditional promises are binding in Hanafi law to prevent deception and uphold commitments made in the form of conditions.

Conditional Obligations and Binding Promises in Hanafi Jurisprudence
Explains the maxim on conditional obligations and its application to binding promises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi maxim on conditional obligations to identify which promises become legally binding (lazim qada'an) and distinguish the Hanafi position from the Maliki view.

Core Idea
A conditional promise becomes a binding commitment; a simple promise does not.

This passage from the Mecelle introduces a key supplementary maxim: any matter conditioned upon a future condition becomes established when that condition occurs. This principle transforms ordinary promises into legally enforceable obligations when they are expressed in conditional form. The passage also contrasts the Hanafi position with the Maliki school's broader enforcement based on reliance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the main maxim and its rationale
Read the first maxim: 'A matter conditioned upon a condition must become established when the condition is established.' Understand that the condition becomes a constructive cause (sabab ja'li).
This is a universal principle in Hanafi usul.
2
Analyze the derivative maxim on promises
The derived maxim states: 'Promises in the form of conditions are binding.' Note the default rule that simple promises are not legally enforceable, and why the conditional form changes that.
Ibn Nujaym's statement: 'A promise is not binding unless it is conditional.'
3
Examine the illustrative cases
Two examples are given: (1) A guarantees payment if a buyer fails to pay; (2) A promise to rescind a sale if the price is returned in case of gross fraud. Both show when a conditional promise becomes binding.
The second example transforms the sale into bay' al-wafa (like a mortgage).
4
Compare Hanafi and Maliki approaches
The author notes that Maliki school has a broader enforcement: a promise becomes binding if a reason (sabab) is mentioned and the promisee relies on it to their detriment. Evaluate which approach better prevents harm.
The author finds the Maliki view 'very sound' but does not change the established Hanafi position.

ملاحظة :
إنَّ الفقهاء والشراح في المذهب الحنفي لا يتوسعون في التمثيل لهذه القاعدة في نطاق المعاملات بأكثر من ذلك والظاهر أنها لا تطبق على نطاق واسع في جميع المواعيد المعلقة.
وفي الاجتهاد المالكي أربعة آراء فقهية حول لزوم الوعد بالعقد وعدم لزومه قضاء. والمشهور من هذه الآراء أنه يع تبر الوعد بالعقد ملزماً للواعد قضاء إذا ذكر فيه سبب، ودخل الموعود تحت التزام مالي بمباشرة ذلك السبب بناء على الوعد وذلك كما لو وعد شخص آخر بأن يقرضه مبلغاً
·
بسبب عزمه على الزواج ليدفعه مهراً أو ليشتري به بضاعة، فتزوج الموعود، أو اشترى البضاعة، ثم نكل الواعد عن القرض، فإنه يُجبر قضاء على تنفيذ وعده (ر: الفروق للقرافي ٢٤/٤ - ٢٥، ورسالة الالتزام للحطاب وهي منشورة في الجزء الأول من فتاوى فتح العلي الملك للشيخ عليش بحث مسائل الالتزام) (۱) .
وهذا وجيه جداً، فإنه يبني الإلزام بالوعد على فكرة دفع الضرر الحاصل فعلاً للموعود من تغرير الواعد فهو أوجه من الاجتهاد الحنفي
(۱) وقال أصبغ من فقهاء المالكية يكفي للإلزام بالوعد ذكر السبب من زواج أو بناء أو غيرهما، ولو لم يباشره الموعود.
Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Fiqh
77/81 - The twentieth supplementary maxim: 'A matter conditioned upon a condition must become established when the condition is established' (M/86). Because the condition upon which the matter is suspended becomes, by the will of the speaker and the consideration of the Shari'ah, a constructive cause (sabab ja'li), so the conditional ruling is linked, in existence and non-existence, to this condition, like the connection of the effect to its legal cause. On this basis, the matter conditioned upon a condition is considered non-existent before the occurrence of the condition, and when the suspension is valid and recognized, the conditioned matter comes into existence with the occurrence of the condition upon which it is suspended and is not delayed beyond it. The explanation of this, with its examples, has preceded in the theory of contracts (see: f 6/28 and 3/43-6). Diyanah (as a heading).
What branches from it of the maxims:
78/81 - 'Promises in the form of conditions are binding' (M/84). The origin is that a promise is not binding on its maker in court (qada'an), even though fulfilling it is required. So if a person promises another a loan, a sale, a gift, a rescission, a release, or any other legal act, no right arises thereby for the promisee; he cannot compel the promisor to execute it by force of court. However, the Hanafi jurists observed that if a promise is issued suspended on a condition, it departs from the meaning of a bare promise and dons the garment of obligation and commitment, thereby becoming binding on its maker (see the commentary of the scholar 'Ali Haydar on the Mecelle). And that is, as appears, to avoid deceiving the promisee after the promise has been issued in the form of a commitment. Ibn Nujaym said in al-Hazr wa al-Ibahah from al-Ashbah 2/110: 'A promise is not binding unless it is conditional.'
Based on this, the jurists decided that if a person says to another: 'Sell to so-and-so, and if he does not give you its price, I will give it to you,' and the buyer does not give the price after demand, the speaker is bound by it. Likewise, if someone sells something with gross fraud (ghubn fahish), and the buyer says to the defrauded seller: 'If you return the price to me, I rescind the sale for you,' this promise is binding, and the sale becomes like bay' al-wafa (sale of trust), which is in the meaning of a mortgage (rahn) (see f 9/16 in the margin and 46/8).
Note: The jurists and commentators in the Hanafi school do not expand in giving examples for this maxim within the scope of transactions beyond this, and it appears that it is not applied widely to all conditional promises.
In the Maliki ijtihad, there are four juristic opinions regarding the binding nature of a promise to contract and its non-bindingness in court. The most famous of these opinions is that a promise to a contract is considered binding on the promisor in court if a reason (sabab) is mentioned in it, and the promisee has entered into a financial obligation by undertaking that reason based on the promise. That is like if a person promises another to lend him an amount because of his intention to marry, to pay a dowry, or to buy goods, and the promisee marries or buys the goods, then the promisor reneges on the loan: he is compelled in court to fulfill his promise (see: al-Furuq for al-Qarafi 4/24-25, and Risalah al-Iltizam by al-Hattab, published in the first part of Fatawa Fath al-'Ali al-Malik by Shaykh 'Ilaysh, section on matters of commitment). This is very sound, because it bases enforcement of the promise on the idea of preventing the actual harm occurring to the promisee from the deception of the promisor. It is stronger than the Hanafi ijtihad. (1) Asbagh, from the Maliki jurists, said that for enforcement of the promise, mentioning the reason (of marriage, building, or other) suffices, even if the promisee has not undertaken it.
Source pages p. 967

Commentary
This passage presents two interconnected legal maxims from the Hanafi usul al-fiqh framework, specifically from the Mecelle's list of universal rules. The first maxim is the 20th supplementary maxim, which states that any matter conditioned upon a future condition becomes obligatory when that condition occurs. The reasoning is that the condition becomes a legal cause (sabab ja'li) created by the speaker's will and recognized by the Shari'ah, linking the conditioned effect to its cause like effect to cause. Before the condition, the matter is considered non-existent; when the condition occurs, the matter comes into existence without delay. The second maxim is derived from the first: promises made in the form of conditions become legally binding. The default rule is that a promise is not enforceable in court (qada'an), though fulfilling it is recommended. However, when a promise is made conditional (mu'allaq) on a future event, it takes on the character of a binding commitment (iltizam) to avoid deceiving the promisee. Ibn Nujaym explicitly states that a promise is not binding unless conditional. Two examples illustrate the rule: (1) If A says to B, 'Sell to so-and-so, and if he doesn't pay you, I will pay you,' and the third party fails to pay, A is bound to pay. (2) If a buyer says to a seller who sold with gross fraud, 'If you return the price, I will rescind the sale,' this promise is binding, and the transaction becomes like a bay' al-wafa (a sale where the seller can repurchase, treated as a mortgage). The note mentions that Hanafi jurists do not extend this rule widely; it applies only to specific cases. The author then compares the Maliki school, which has four opinions; the most famous is that a promise to contract is binding if a reason (sabab) is mentioned and the promisee has incurred a financial obligation based on it (e.g., promising a loan for marriage or purchase, and the promisee acts on it). The author finds this Maliki approach very sound ('wajih jiddan') because it bases enforcement on preventing actual harm from deception, and considers it stronger than the Hanafi view. An additional note from Asbagh states that mentioning the reason alone suffices for enforcement, even if the promisee has not yet acted.

Key Terms
الوعد (al-wa'd): A promise to do something in the future; not legally binding in court unless made conditional on a future event (ta'liq).
التعليق (al-ta'liq): Attaching a legal obligation to a future condition (shart).
الغبن الفاحش (al-ghubn al-fāhish): Gross fraud or excessive overpayment in a sale; one of the conditions that allows rescission and creates a conditional promise.
بيع الوفاء (bay' al-wafā'): A sale with the understanding that the seller can repurchase when able; legally treated as a mortgage (rahn) in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi fiqh, when does a promise become legally enforceable in court?
A) When it is made with sincere intention.
B) When it is witnessed by two witnesses.
C) When it is expressed in the form of a condition (ta'liq).
D) When it is in writing.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, only promises expressed as conditions (ta'liq) are enforceable in court; simple promises are not.

Binding Conditional Promises: Analysis and Critique
Analysis and critique of the Majalla's rule on conditional promises

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the refined qawida on binding conditional promises to distinguish between binding and non-binding promises in the Hanafi fatwa context.

Core Idea
Promises that deceive and imply commitment are binding; bare promises are not.

This passage critiques the twentieth Majalla rule and proposes a more precise formulation based on harm and deception. We will examine the Hanafi examples, the Maliki alternative, and the juristic reasoning behind the reformulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Majalla rule and its apparent scope
The rule as stated seems to make all conditional promises binding. Note the Hanafi default of non-bindingness for bare promises.
The rule is from Ibn Nujaym and taken verbatim by the Majalla.
2
Examine the Hanafi examples
Two cases: guarantee of payment if buyer fails, and promise to cancel a defective sale upon return of price. Both are binding.
Hanafi jurists did not extend beyond such cases.
3
Analyze the critique
The wording is misleading; the true basis is not mere conditionality but that the promise implies commitment and deceives the promisee.
Allamah Ali Haydar's reasoning is cited.
4
Compare with the Maliki school
Maliki enforces promises when a reason is mentioned and the promisee acts, focusing on actual harm.
The author finds this more sound than the Hanafi focus on verbal form.
5
Understand the proposed new formulation
The author proposes: 'Promises, if issued in a form that deceives the promisee and implies commitment, are binding on the promisor.'
This is more precise and aligns with preventing harm.

Chapter (81): Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification. And upon this, the jurists decided that if a person says to another: 'Sell to so-and-so, and if he does not give you its price, then I will give it to you,' and the buyer did not give the price after demand, the speaker is bound by it. - Likewise, if someone sold something with a gross disadvantage, and the buyer said to the deceived seller: 'If you return the price to me, I cancel the sale for you,' this promise is binding, and the sale becomes like a sale with right of redemption, which is in the nature of a pledge. Note: The Hanafi jurists and commentators do not expand much on examples for this rule in the scope of transactions beyond that, and it appears that it is not applied widely to all conditional promises. In Maliki ijtihad, there are four juristic opinions regarding the binding nature of a promise to contract and its non-bindingness in court. The well-known among these opinions is that the promise to contract is considered binding on the promisor in court if a reason is mentioned in it, and the promisee entered into a financial commitment by undertaking that reason based on the promise. For example, if someone promised another to lend him an amount due to his intention to marry to pay the dowry or to buy goods, and the promisee married or bought the goods, then the promisor reneged on the loan, he is compelled in court to fulfill his promise. This is very sound, because it bases enforcement of the promise on the idea of removing actual harm suffered by the promisee from the promisor's deception, and it is more sound than the Hanafi ijtihad, which bases enforcement on the verbal forms of the promise, whether conditional or not. For conditionality or its absence does not change anything of the reality of the promise. My father, Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa, may Allah have mercy on him, said in his commentary on the rules of the Majalla under this rule (p. 358): 'The apparent meaning of this rule is that it is general and absolute regarding every promise that comes in the form of a condition, while the reality is contrary...' And Allamah Ali Haydar mentioned in his commentary under this rule that the Majalla took it verbatim from Ibn Nujaym. And this seems, and since it is established in the Hanafi school that the basic principle regarding a bare promise is non-bindingness, the wording of this rule is misleading as contrary to the intended meaning. The juristic foundation of this rule is that the promise is issued in a form that implies commitment and undertaking (as Allamah Ali Haydar indicated in his reasoning for the rule) because at that time it deceives the promisee, and issuing the promise in a conditional form suggests the meaning of commitment, undertaking, or guarantee. In light of the foregoing, I propose that this rule be formulated in a new wording as follows: 'Promises, if issued in a form that deceives the promisee and implies commitment, are binding on the promisor.'
Source pages p. 968

Commentary
This passage is a critical commentary on the twentieth rule of the Majalla, which concerns promises made in a conditional form. The author begins by presenting two Hanafi examples: first, a person guarantees payment if the buyer fails; second, a buyer promises to rescind a defective sale if the price is returned. Both are considered binding. However, the author notes that Hanafi scholars rarely extend this rule beyond such examples, indicating limited application. The author then contrasts the Hanafi approach with the Maliki school, which, according to the well-known view, enforces a promise if a reason (like marriage) was mentioned and the promisee acted upon it, incurring financial loss. The author finds the Maliki approach more sound because it focuses on actual harm from deception rather than mere verbal form. Citing his father, the author criticizes the Majalla's rule as overly broad and misleading, since the Hanafi default is non-bindingness of bare promises. He suggests the rule should be reformulated to only bind promises that are issued in a deceiving and commitment-implying form. The passage thus refines the qawida by limiting its scope to promises that cause harm.

Key Terms
وعد معلق (wa'd mu'allaq): A promise made in a conditional form (e.g., 'if you do X, I will do Y').
إلزام (ilzam): Legal enforcement of a promise or obligation in court.
غرور (ghurur): Deception that leads the promisee to incur loss by relying on the promise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The mere fact of the conditional wording
B) The promisee's reliance and resulting loss from deception
C) The promisee's request for the promise
D) The presence of witnesses

Key Takeaway
A conditional promise is binding only if it deceives the promisee and implies commitment; otherwise, it remains unenforceable like a bare promise.

The Binding of Promises: A Critical Refinement of the Majalla's Qawida
Critique of a qawida on promises and proposal for reformulation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi criterion for binding promises (ighrar) and apply the refined qawida to distinguish binding from non-binding promises.

Core Idea
A promise becomes binding when it deludes the promisee into reliance, not when it is merely conditional.

In this lesson, we examine a qawida from the Majalla concerning promises made with conditional phrasing. Mustafa al-Zarqa critiques the original maxim, pointing out that it misrepresents the Hanafi position. He proposes a refined formulation focusing on whether the promise deludes the promisee (ighrar). Understanding this refinement will prevent misapplication of the maxim in fatwa.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Original Qawida
Read the passage to understand the original Majalla maxim: 'المواعيد المعلقة لا تلزم' (conditional promises are not binding). Note its apparent absolute scope.
This maxim is from Ibn Nujaym, as noted by Ali Haydar.
2
Recall the Hanafi Principle
Remember that in Hanafi fiqh, a bare promise (وعد مجرد) is not legally binding. Binding occurs only if the promise induces reliance (ighrar). The key is the promisee's delusion, not the presence of a condition.
The principle is rooted in istihsan and preventing harm.
3
Analyse the Critique
Al-Zarqa's father observed that the original maxim seems to imply that any conditional promise is non-binding, which is misleading. The author clarifies that the conditionality does not change the essence; the real issue is whether the promise 'deludes the promisee' (يغر الموعود).
This critique is methodological: the wording of the maxim is too broad.
4
Study the Proposed Reformulation
The author suggests a new wording: 'المواعيد إذا صدرت بصورة تغر الموعود وتشعر بالالتزام فإنها تكون مُلزِمَةً للواعد.' This shifts focus from conditionality to inducement.
The reformulation aligns with the Hanafi concept of ighrar.
5
Apply the Refined Qawida
Test the refined maxim on cases: e.g., a promise that leads the promisee to take a costly action becomes binding; a vague promise without reliance does not.
The application protects against injustice and follows the school's objectives.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية الفقه الذي يبني الإلزام على الصور اللفظية للوعد هل هي تعليقية أو غير تعليقية، (1). (۱) فإن التعليق وعدمه لا يغير شيئاً من حقيقة الوعد (۱. قال والدي الشيخ أحمد الزرقا رحمه الله في شرحه لقواعد المجلة تحت هذه القاعدة ( ص ٣٥٨) : «ظاهر هذه القاعدة أنها مطلقة عامة في كل وعد أتى بصورة التعليق والحال خلافه . . . » . وقد ذكر العلامة علي حيدر في شرحه تحت هذه القاعدة أن المجلة قد أخذتها بنصها من ابن نجيم. ويبدو هذا، ونظراً لأنَّ من المقرر في المذهب الحنفي أن الأصل في الوعد المجرَّد عدم الإلزام، فإن صيغة هذه القاعدة تعتبر موهمة خلاف المراد. أنَّ المرتكز الفقهي لهذه القاعدة أن هو يصدر الوعد بصورة تُشعر بالالتزام والتعهد (كما أشار إليه العلامة علي حيدر في تعليله للقاعدة لأنه عندئذ يغرُّ الموعود، وإصدار الوعد بصيغة التعليق على الشرط يوحي بمعنى الالتزام والتعهد أو الكفالة. هو مما يلي: وفي ضوء ما تقدم أقترح أن تُصاغ هذه القاعدة بصيغة جديدة كما يلي: المواعيد إذا صدرت بصورة تغر الموعود وتشعر بالالتزام فإنها تكون مُلزِمَةً للواعد».
Chapter Ten: Universal Legal Maxims. The fiqh that bases obligation on the verbal forms of promise: are they conditional or non-conditional? (1). (1) For conditionality or its absence does not change anything of the reality of the promise (1. My father Sheikh Ahmad al-Zarqa, may Allah have mercy on him, said in his commentary on the Majalla rules under this maxim (p. 358): 'The apparent meaning of this maxim is that it is absolute and general in every promise that comes in the form of conditionality, while the reality is otherwise…' And the scholar Ali Haydar mentioned in his commentary under this maxim that the Majalla took it verbatim from Ibn Nujaym. And this is apparent. And since it is established in the Hanafi school that the original principle regarding a bare promise is non-binding, the wording of this maxim is misleading contrary to the intended meaning. The fiqh basis of this maxim is that when a promise is issued in a form that gives a sense of commitment and undertaking (as indicated by the scholar Ali Haydar in his reasoning for the maxim, because then it deludes the promisee), and issuing a promise in a conditional form suggests a meaning of commitment, undertaking, or guarantee. Then it follows: In light of the above, I propose that this maxim be formulated in a new wording as follows: 'Promises, if issued in a manner that deludes the promisee and gives a sense of commitment, then they are binding on the promisor.'
Source pages p. 969

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of a qawida (legal maxim) from the Majalla concerning promises made with conditional phrasing. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, begins by quoting his father's observation that the apparent meaning of the existing maxim is too absolute, implying that any conditional promise is automatically non-binding—which contradicts the Hanafi position. In Hanafi fiqh, a bare promise (without inducement) is not binding, but if the promise is worded in a way that leads the promisee to rely on it (yaghurru al-maw'ood), it becomes binding. Ali Haydar confirms the maxim's origin from Ibn Nujaym. The author then proposes a refined wording: promises that delude the promisee and convey commitment are binding. This refinement aligns the maxim with Hanafi principles, emphasizing that the key factor is not conditionality per se but the inducement of reliance. The passage demonstrates how later Hanafi scholars refine and clarify inherited formulations to prevent misunderstanding.

Key Terms
وعد (wa'd): A promise; an undertaking to do something in the future.
تعليق (ta'liq): Conditional phrasing; making a promise contingent upon a condition.
إلزام (ilzam): Binding; making a promise legally enforceable.
يغر الموعود (yaghurru al-maw'ood): To delude the promisee; to cause reliance through the form of the promise.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Whenever it is conditional on a future event
B) When it is made in a manner that deludes the promisee and conveys commitment
C) Only if it is a written contract
D) Always, even if it is a bare promise

Key Takeaway
The binding nature of a promise in Hanafi fiqh hinges on whether it deludes the promisee into reliance, not on its conditional phrasing.

The Qawida: 'Conditions Must Be Observed as Far as Possible'
Definition and explanation of a qawida on contractual conditions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qawida 'يلزم مراعاة الشرط بقدر الإمكان' to evaluate whether a contractual condition is enforceable, distinguishing between restrictive and suspensive conditions and identifying the Shariah limitation.

Core Idea
A condition agreed upon by Muslims must be enforced unless it violates Shariah contract rules.

This lesson examines one of the foundational qawaid from the Majalla, which balances contractual freedom with Shariah boundaries. The rule is based on the prophetic hadith that Muslims are bound by their conditions, but it is qualified by the phrase 'as far as possible,' meaning that conditions contrary to Shariah are not enforceable. Understanding this rule is essential for a mufti when assessing contractual stipulations in various transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Qawida Text and Its Source
Read the Arabic phrase 'يلزم مراعاة الشرط بقدر الإمكان' and note that it is rule 21 from the Majalla. Recognize it as a universal principle governing contractual conditions.
The Majalla codified Hanafi fiqh for Ottoman courts.
2
Distinguish the Type of Condition
The passage clarifies that this qawida applies specifically to restrictive conditions (shart taqyidi), which modify an existing contract, not suspensive conditions (shart ta'liqi) that delay the contract's existence. Identify which type each condition in a given scenario is.
Suspensive conditions are governed by different rules.
3
Recall the Dalil (Proof)
The qawida is based on the hadith 'المسلمون على شروطهم' (Muslims are bound by their conditions). Understand that the hadith establishes the general principle, but the qawida adds the limitation.
The hadith is found in multiple collections; its chain is ahad but accepted by the Hanafi school.
4
Understand the Limitation 'بقدر الإمكان'
Analyze the phrase 'as far as possible' as meaning the condition must not contravene the rules of Shariah in the contract system. This is the key qualification: if a condition violates a clear prohibition (e.g., riba, gharar), it is not enforceable.
The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools have different thresholds for what contradicts Shariah.
5
Apply the Qawida to a Scenario
Take a practical case, e.g., a condition in a sale that the buyer will not resell the item. Determine if it is a restrictive condition, then check if it violates any Shariah prohibition. If not, it is enforceable; if yes, it is void.
This step is crucial for fatwa drafting.

Source Text

79/81 - The twenty-first rule: Observance of a condition must be observed as far as possible (Majalla art. 83). Because it is narrated from the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) that he said: 'Muslims are bound by their conditions' as previously discussed (see f 2/10 b and 7/41). And the condition intended in this rule is the restrictive condition, not the suspensive condition (see f 2/43). So it must be respected and enforced as far as possible. And what is meant by 'as far as possible' is that it should not violate the rules of Shariah in the system of contracts. Source pages p. 969

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-first qawida from the Majalla: 'يلزم مراعاة الشرط بقدر الإمكان' (Observance of a condition must be observed as far as possible). The author explains that the condition referred to is a restrictive condition (shart taqyidi), which modifies an existing contract, not a suspensive condition (shart ta'liqi) that suspends the contract's existence. The rule is based on the well-known hadith: 'المسلمون على شروطهم' (Muslims are bound by their conditions). However, the condition must be observed only 'as far as possible,' meaning it must not contradict the Shariah rules governing contracts. For example, a condition that violates a clear prohibition would not be enforceable. The author notes that the various madhhabs differ in how strictly they interpret this limit. The Hanbali school is the most accommodating, while the Hanafi school has its own framework summarized elsewhere. This rule ensures contractual integrity while upholding Shariah boundaries.

Key Terms
الشرط التقييدي (al-shart al-taqyidi): A restrictive condition that modifies an existing contract without suspending its existence.
الشرط التعليقي (al-shart al-ta'liqi): A suspensive condition; a condition that delays the existence or effect of a contract.
بقدر الإمكان (biqadr al-imkan): As far as possible; limited by Shariah compatibility.
المشارطات العقدية (al-musharitat al-'aqdiyya): Contractual stipulations; conditions agreed upon in a contract.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) Suspensive conditions (ta'liqi)
B) Restrictive conditions (taqyidi)
C) Void conditions
D) Repealing conditions

Key Takeaway
The qawida 'يلزم مراعاة الشرط بقدر الإمكان' balances contractual freedom with Shariah compliance: conditions must be fulfilled unless they violate Shariah.

Rule 22: Legal Permissibility Negates Liability
Qawida with furuat explaining when permissibility removes liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'الجواز الشرعي ينافي الضمان' to distinguish between permissible and impermissible acts and determine liability accordingly.

Core Idea
If an act is permissible in Shariah, no financial liability arises from its harmful consequences.

This lesson introduces the twenty-second qawida from the Majalla: 'Legal permissibility negates liability.' This maxim is a cornerstone in Hanafi tort and contract law, clarifying that permission from the Lawgiver removes responsibility. The passage explains the principle and provides three practical applications that illustrate how permissibility shields from liability, while transgression (ta'addi) imposes it.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Identify the Arabic text: 'الجواز الشرعي ينافي الضمان'. Understand its plain meaning: when an act is legally permissible, the doer is not liable for any resulting harm. Note the definition of daman as financial compensation.
2
Identify the scope of the maxim
Determine which fiqh chapters this maxim governs. It applies to torts (jinayat), contracts (ijarah), lost property (luqata), and generally any chapter involving permissible vs. impermissible acts that cause harm.
3
Work through the furuat
Apply the maxim to each of the three examples in the passage. For each, identify the act, whether it is permissible, and why liability is or is not imposed. Example 1: digging a hole in one's own property — permissible, no liability. Example 2: loading a rented animal with usual load — permissible, no liability. Example 3: finder detaining lost property for expenses — permissible, so no liability for delay.
The third furu' is cut off in the source but the principle is clear.
4
Identify the exception
Note that permissibility is lost when the act involves transgression (ta'addi), such as digging in a public road without permission. In that case, liability attaches. The exception is governed by the maxim 'الضرر يزال' (Harm must be removed).
5
Relate to other qawaid
Understand how this maxim interacts with other legal maxims. 'الضرر يزال' parallels it but applies when permissibility is absent. 'ما ثبت على خلاف القياس فغيره عليه لا يقاس' restricts its extension to cases founded on contrary analogy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
80/81 - القاعدة الثانية والعشرون: الجواز الشرعي ينافي الضمان (91/p). الضمان: هو التزام بتعويض مالي عن ضرر للغير. ومعنى هذه القاعدة أنه لا يترتب على شخص ضمان بسبب فعله أو عدم فعله شيئاً إذا كان ذلك منه جائزاً شرعاً، لأن تسويغ الشارع ذلك الفعل أو الترك يقتضي رفع المسؤولية عنه؛ وإلا لم يكن جائزاً. وعلى هذا فروع كثيرة : - منها ما لو حفر إنسان في ملكه الخاص حفرة، فتردى فيها حيوان لغيره، فلا يضمنه لأن حفره هذا جائز . بخلاف ما لو حَفَرَ في الطريق العام لأنه يكون عندئذ معتدياً على حق العامة، فيضمن الضرر. - وكذا لو حَمّل المستأجر الدابةَ المأجورة قدر المعتاد فهلكت، فإنه لا يضمنها لأن فعله جائز. أما لو حملها أكثر من المعتاد فإنه يضمنها. - ولو امتنع الملتقط عن تسليم اللقطة لصاحبها حتى يأخذ النفقة التي
80/81 - The twenty-second rule: Legal permissibility negates liability (91/p). Liability: is an obligation to pay financial compensation for harm caused to another. The meaning of this rule is that no liability is incurred by a person for his act or omission if that act or omission is legally permissible, because the Lawgiver's permitting that act or omission implies removal of responsibility for it; otherwise it would not be permissible. Upon this there are many branches: Among them, if a person digs a hole in his private property, and an animal belonging to someone else falls into it, he is not liable because his digging is permissible. Contrary to if he digs in the public road, for then he is transgressing the right of the public, so he is liable for the harm. Likewise, if a lessee loads a rented animal with the usual load and it perishes, he is not liable because his action is permissible. But if he loads it with more than the usual, he is liable. And if the finder of a lost object refuses to hand it over to its owner until he takes the expenses that...
Source pages p. 970

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-second qawida from the Majalla: 'الجواز الشرعي ينافي الضمان' (Legal permissibility negates liability). The author defines ضمان as financial compensation for harm. The core principle is that if an action or omission is permissible in Shariah, no liability arises from it, because permission implies that the doer is not responsible. However, if the action becomes impermissible (e.g., digging in a public road), liability attaches. The passage then provides three furuat (applications): (1) Digging a hole in one's own property: permissible, no liability for subsequent harm. If in a public road, it is transgression (ta'addi), so liability. (2) Loading a rented animal with the usual load: permissible, no liability if it dies. If overloading, liable. (3) A finder of lost property refusing to return it until expenses are paid: the text cuts off, but the implication is that detaining the found property for expenses is permissible, so no liability for delay. These examples illustrate the rule's scope: permissibility (ibaha) shields one from liability, but transgression removes that shield.

Key Terms
الضمان (al-daman): Financial compensation for harm caused to another.
الجواز الشرعي (al-jawaz al-shar'i): Legal permissibility; an act not prohibited by Shariah.
الملتقط (al-multaqit): Finder of a lost item (luqata).
اللقطة (al-luqata): Lost property found by someone.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because the animal owner was negligent
B) Because the hole was dug with permission from authorities
C) Because digging on one's own property is legally permissible
D) Because the animal was a trespasser

Key Takeaway
Legal permissibility (jawaz shar'i) is a complete defense against liability for harm caused, unless the act involves transgression (ta'addi).
