The Distinction Between Restrictive and Suspensory Conditions
Clarifying the type of condition intended in a key qawida

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between taqyeedi and ta'leeqi conditions and apply the qawida 'conditions must be respected as far as possible' to determine enforceability of contractual stipulations.

Core Idea
Only restrictive (taqyeedi) conditions are enforceable, and only if they do not violate Sharia rules.

This passage from the Majalla clarifies a fundamental rule regarding contractual conditions. It distinguishes between two types: restrictive (taqyeedi) and suspensory (ta'leeqi). The rule that conditions must be respected applies only to the former. Even then, enforcement is limited to what is possible without violating Sharia. Different schools have varying thresholds for what constitutes a violation, with the Hanbali school being most expansive and the Hanafi school more restrictive.

Guided Path
1
Identify the key distinction
The passage explicitly distinguishes between taqyeedi (restrictive) and ta'leeqi (suspensory) conditions. Identify which type is meant by 'al-shart al-maqsud' (the intended condition).
Taqyeedi conditions are integral to the contract; ta'leeqi conditions suspend the contract on a future event.
2
Understand the scope of the qawida
The rule 'conditions must be respected as far as possible' applies only to taqyeedi conditions. Ta'leeqi conditions are generally not enforceable in Hanafi law, except in limited cases.
3
Determine the limit of enforcement
'As far as possible' means the condition must not violate the fundamental rules of Sharia in the system of contracts. If it contradicts Sharia, it cannot be enforced.
Example: A condition to sell alcohol in a contract is invalid because it violates Sharia.
4
Note madhhab differences
Different schools have different thresholds for what constitutes a Sharia violation. The Hanbali school is the most expansive in accepting conditions; the Hanafi is more restrictive.
The passage references earlier sections for the Hanafi theory.
5
Apply the framework
Given a contractual condition, classify it as taqyeedi or ta'leeqi, then determine if its enforcement would violate Sharia. If taqyeedi and Sharia-compliant, it must be enforced; otherwise, it is invalid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها على شروطهم كما تقدم (ر ) ف ٢/١٠ ب و ٧/٤١). والشرط المقصود في هذه القاعدة هو الشرط التقييدي لا التعليقي (ر: ف (٢/٤٣) فيجب احترامه وإنفاذه بقدر الإمكان. والمراد بقدر الإمكان أن لا يخالف قواعد الشريعة في نظام العقود. وقدمنا أن الاجتهادات المذهبية مختلفة جداً توسيعاً وتضييقاً في حدود هذه المخالفة والموافقة وبالنتيجة فيما هو مقبول أو مرفوض من المشارطات العقدية (ف (١/٤٢ - ٥ وإن أوسعها الاجتهاد الحنبلي، كما أو جزنا نظرية الاجتهاد الحنفي في ذلك، فليرجع إليها هناك (ف: ٤٣/ ١٢ .(١٤ -
Chapter (81): Explanation of the Rules of the Majalla and their Classification. On their conditions as previously mentioned (see vol. 2, p. 10b and 7/41). The intended condition in this rule is the restrictive (taqyeedi) condition, not the suspensory (ta'leeqi) (see vol. 2, p. 43). So it must be respected and enforced as far as possible. By 'as far as possible' it means that it should not violate the rules of Sharia in the system of contracts. We have already mentioned that madhhab-based ijtihads differ greatly in widening or narrowing the limits of this violation and compliance, and consequently in what is accepted or rejected of contractual stipulations (see vol. 1, pp. 42-5; and the most expansive is the Hanbali ijtihad, as we have summarized the theory of Hanafi ijtihad in that regard, so refer to it there (vol. 12, pp. 43-14).
Source pages p. 970

Commentary
This passage is a methodological note on the rule regarding conditions (shuroot) in contracts. It distinguishes between two types of conditions: taqyeedi (restrictive/integrative) and ta'leeqi (suspensory). The rule being discussed applies to the former. The principle is that such conditions must be honored and enforced, but only to the extent possible ('bi qadr al-imkan'), which means they must not contradict the fundamental rules of Sharia regarding contracts. The author notes that different schools (madhahib) have varying degrees of strictness in determining what constitutes a violation, with the Hanbali school being the most lenient. The reader is referred to earlier sections for the Hanafi position on this issue.

Key Terms
الشرط التقييدي (ash-shart at-taqyeedi): A restrictive condition that is integral to the contract, as opposed to a suspensory condition.
الشرط التعليقي (ash-shart at-ta'leeqi): A suspensory condition that makes the contract contingent on a future event.
الشرط المقصود (ash-shart al-maqsud): The intended condition in the context of the qawida, i.e., the taqyeedi condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
Only restrictive (taqyeedi) conditions that do not violate Sharia are enforceable under the Hanafi qawida.

Critique of the Hanafi Qawida: Hire and Guarantee Do Not Coexist
Criticizing the Hanafi rule that hire and guarantee cannot coexist

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi qawida 'al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an', apply it to cases of hirer transgression, evaluate its critique, and compare the Hanafi view with the majority position.

Core Idea
In Hanafi law, once a hirer becomes a usurper, hire cancels even if the property is safe.

This passage critically examines the Hanafi legal maxim that hire and guarantee cannot coexist. Mustafa al-Zarqa demonstrates how the rule is applied when a hirer deviates from agreed terms, becoming a transgressor (ghasib). He raises objections based on legal theory and practical consequences, contrasting the Hanafi view with the majority of other schools.

Guided Path
1
State the qawida and its scope
Recite the qawida 'الأجر والضمان لا يجتمعان' and note that it governs cases of hire where the hirer becomes liable for the object.
2
Examine the furuat
Review the derived cases: (1) hirer deviates, animal perishes → no hire, guarantee value; (2) animal returned safely → still no hire because potential guarantee.
Note the use of 'potential guarantee' (عرضة للضمان) rather than actual destruction.
3
Identify the Hanafi interpretation of daman
Contrast 'actual guarantee' (ضمان متحقق) with 'potential guarantee' (عرضة للضمان). The Hanafi position relies on the latter.
This is key to understanding the critique.
4
Analyze the author's critique
Al-Zarqa argues the rule is 'bizarre' (غرابة) and opens loopholes for people to benefit from others' property without compensation. He suggests restricting it to actual destruction.
This is a critical refinement, not an outright rejection.
5
Compare with the majority view
Other schools (Maliki, Shafi'i, Hanbali) allow hire and guarantee to coexist: the hirer owes fair hire for wrongfully used benefits plus value if the object perishes.
Thamarah al-khilaf: practical difference in liability.
6
Apply the istithna'
Exception: if the hire had already been settled before transgression, then if the object does not perish, hire is still owed. If it perishes, no hire (guarantee merges).
This exception limits the absolute application of the qawida.

Source Text

الغاصب منافع المغصوب تلك النظرية التي تقدمت الإشارة إليها وإلى انتقادها (ر: ف ۳/۷۷)
- وعلى هذه القاعدة يفرّع الحنفية أنه : لو استأجر شخص دابة مثلاً ليركبها إلى مكان معين، فذهب بها رأساً إلى مكان آخر يعتبر متعدياً في حكم الغاصب، ويخرج عن صفة الأمين التي هي الصفة الأصلية شرعاً للمستأجر، فإذا هلكت الدابة عنده قبل ردها إلى مالكها يضمن قيمتها ولا أجر عليه لأنَّ الأجر والضمان لا يجتمعان .
-
وكذا إذا لم تهلك فردها سليمة ولو بعد شهور لا أجر عليه أيضاً !! لأنه كان في حالة مسؤولية بضمانها لو هلكت عنده (ر: أول بحث إجارة الدواب من الفتاوى الخانية وقواعد الحمزاوي مسائل الإجارة ص/ ۸۲). يتضح من هذه التفريعات وأمثالها أنه ليس المراد بالضمان في هذه القاعدة الضمان المتحقق الواقع فعلاً أي بأن تهلك العين فيلتزم الشخص بقيمتها، بل المراد به في النظر الحنفي كون الشخص عرضة لضمان العين، وذلك بأن يكون بحالة يعتبر فيها هو المسؤول بقيمة العين إن هلكت، سواء أوقع الهلاك فعلاً أم لم يقع، أي أن يكون في حالة تحمل التبعة.
وهذا في منتهى الغرابة كما ترى فهو تفريح نظري محض لم ينظر فيه إلى مصلحة التطبيق. فهو يفسح للناس مجال الاحتيال للانتفاع بأموال الغير بلا بدل فيعقدون الإجارة على غير المنفعة التي يريدون، ثم يخالفون إلى الانتفاع بما يريدون دون التزام بعوض ولا يبالون ضمان المأجور عند الهلاك لأنه نادر.
ولو أن فقهاء الحنفية خصصوا القاعدة بحالة وقوع الضمان وتحققه، كما إذا هلكت الدابة مثلاً في هذه الحال والتزم المستأجر بقيمتها لكان لها وجه، إذ يقال أن ضمان الأصل تندمج فيه المنافع مع أن ذلك أيضاً غير قوي.

Chapter (81): Commentary on the Qawaid of the Majalla and Their Classification
The usurper of benefits of the usurped object: that theory previously alluded to and its critique (see: f 3/77). – And upon this qaida, the Hanafis derive: If someone hires, for example, a beast of burden to ride to a certain place, then takes it instead to another place, he is considered a transgressor in the ruling of a usurper, and departs from the attribute of trustworthy, which is the original legal attribute for a hirer. So if the beast perishes in his possession before returning it to its owner, he is liable for its value and has no hire, because hire and guarantee do not coexist. – Similarly, if it does not perish and he returns it safe, even after months, he also has no hire!! Because he was in a state of responsibility for its guarantee had it perished with him (see: beginning of the discussion on hiring beasts from al-Fatawa al-Khaniyya and Qawaid al-Hamzawi, Masail al-Ijarah p. 82). It becomes clear from these derived rulings and their like that what is meant by guarantee in this qaida is not the guarantee that actually occurs, i.e., that the object perishes and the person becomes liable for its value, but rather what is meant in Hanafi consideration is that the person is susceptible to guarantee of the object, i.e., he is in a condition where he is deemed responsible for the value of the object if it perishes, whether the destruction actually occurs or not, i.e., he is in a state of bearing the liability. This is extremely bizarre, as you see; it is a purely theoretical reasoning that does not consider the interest of application. It opens for people a field of circumventing to benefit from others' property without compensation: they conclude a hire for a benefit they do not intend, then violate it to benefit as they wish without commitment to compensation, and they do not care about the guarantee of the hired object upon destruction because it is rare. If only the Hanafi jurists had restricted the qaida to the case of actual occurrence and realization of guarantee, as when the beast perishes in this situation and the hirer is committed to its value, it would have some justification, for it is said that the guarantee of the principal absorbs the benefits – although that also is not strong. The majority of other schools, in their view, hold that hire and guarantee coexist. So in this incident, for example, the hirer is liable for the fair hire for the benefits he obtained wrongfully according to the period, and in addition he guarantees the value of the principal on the day of destruction if it perishes. See: al-Mughni and al-Sharh al-Kabir, book of ghasp, 5/402 and (436). However, the Hanafis qualify this qaida by stipulating that the hire not have become settled on the person before he enters a state of guarantee of the object. If the named hire had become settled on him beforehand, as if he first received the contracted benefit, then became a transgressor in the ruling of a usurper, then he – although he becomes a bearer of the liability for the hired object's destruction – is obligated for the hire according to them if it does not perish. But if the hired object perishes after the transgression before return to the owner, then he guarantees it and has no hire in that case in any event, because the guarantee of the benefits is merged into the guarantee of the principal. 81/85 –
Source pages p. 974

Commentary
This passage is a critical analysis of the Hanafi qawida 'الاجر والضمان لا يجتمعان' (hire and guarantee do not coexist). The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, begins by noting that the rule was mentioned earlier with critique. He then presents the standard Hanafi application: if someone hires an animal to go to a specific place but goes elsewhere, he becomes a transgressor (like a usurper) and loses his status as amin (trustee). If the animal perishes, he must pay its value and owes no hire. Even if it does not perish and is returned safely, he owes no hire because he was in a state of potential liability. Al-Zarqa criticizes this as bizarre: it allows people to circumvent paying hire by intentionally misusing the property, since they only risk a rare destruction. He argues the rule should be restricted to actual destruction. He then notes that other schools allow both hire and guarantee to coexist. Finally, he mentions a Hanafi qualification: if the hire had already been settled before the transgression (e.g., the hirer used the contracted benefit first), then after transgression he still owes the hire if no destruction occurs, but if destruction occurs, no hire because the guarantee of benefits merges into guarantee of the principal.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability or guarantee for the value of an object if it perishes; in this context, also includes potential liability (being 'عرضة للضمان').
أجر (ajr): Hire or rental payment for use of a thing.
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who takes another's property unlawfully and is liable for it.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi rule criticized in the text, if a man hires a donkey to go to the market but instead takes it to the river and returns it safely a week later, what is the ruling on the hire?
A) He must pay the full agreed hire.
B) He must pay fair hire (ajr al-mithl).
C) He owes no hire at all.
D) He owes half the hire.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi qawida 'al-ajr wa al-daman la yajtami'an' cancels hire upon potential guarantee, but its critique and the istithna' for settled hire are essential for balanced application.

Majalla Qawida 27: Disposition Without Permission
Explaining Qawida 27 and its branches

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Qawida 27 to distinguish between tortious and permissible dispositions of another's property, including the effect of delivery.

Core Idea
Disposing of another's property without permission is tortious unless Shari'a or custom permits it.

This lesson introduces Majalla's Qawida 27, a foundational rule in Hanafi property law. The rule forbids unauthorized disposition of others' property but distinguishes between actual dispositions (which are immediately tortious) and verbal dispositions (which become tortious only when executed by delivery). Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining liability in cases of ghasp (usurpation) and invalid contracts.

Guided Path
1
State the Rule and Its Scope
Recite Qawida 27: 'It is not permissible for anyone to dispose of another's property without permission.' Note that permission can come from the owner or from the Shari'a/custom.
The Majalla text says 'without his permission' but we include Shari'a permission.
2
Distinguish Actual vs. Verbal Disposition
Identify whether the disposition is a physical act (taking, consuming, digging) or a verbal act (contractual promise). Actual dispositions without permission are automatically transgressive.
Verbal dispositions are not immediately tortious.
3
Identify When a Verbal Disposition Becomes Actual
If a verbal contract is followed by delivery (e.g., selling another's property and handing it over), it becomes an actual disposition and triggers liability as ghasp.
Without delivery, the verbal act is merely a statement.
4
Recognize Exceptions from Shari'a or Custom
Determine if the disposition is permitted by clear Shari'a evidence or established custom, such as a shepherd slaughtering a fatally injured sheep.
These exceptions negate the transgressive nature.
5
Apply to Furuat (Branches)
Practice with scenarios: taking without permission (ghasp), eating without permission (liable), digging a well (liable if damage), selling without delivery (not tortious), selling with delivery (ghasp).
Use the furuat from the passage.

Source Text

The twenty-seventh qaida: 'It is not permissible for anyone to dispose of the property of another without permission' (Majalla art. 96). The wording of the qaida in the Majalla is 'without his permission' i.e., the owner's permission. And we have preferred to omit the pronoun to include the permission of the Shari'a. Disposition of another's property is either actual or verbal by way of contract: a. Actual disposition of another's property by taking, consuming, digging in the ground, and the like, without permission, is considered a transgression. The disposer is in the ruling of a usurper, liable for the damage. The source of permission may be the Shari'a or custom; e.g., if a shepherd slaughters a sheep that has been injured and is not expected to survive, then he is not liable (see: al-Qawaid al-Fiqhiyya by Shaykh Mahmud al-Hamzawi, section on ghasp, p. 191). b. As for verbal disposition by way of contract, such as selling another's property, giving it as a gift, pawning it, leasing it, lending it, depositing it, etc., then there is detail: If it is followed by the disposer's execution through delivery, it becomes an actual disposition and takes the ruling of ghasp.
Source pages p. 975

Commentary
This passage presents Qawida 27 from the Majalla: no one may dispose of another's property without permission. The author notes that the original text says 'without his permission' (the owner's), but he recommends omitting the pronoun to also include permission from the Shari'a (e.g., legal exceptions). The rule is then divided into two types: actual dispositions and verbal dispositions. Actual dispositions (taking, consuming, digging) are automatically transgressive if done without permission, and the disposer is like a usurper, liable for damage. An exception is when permission comes from the Shari'a or custom, such as a shepherd slaughtering a dying sheep to prevent waste. Verbal dispositions (contracts like sale, gift, lease) are initially just statements; they only become actual disposition (and thus tortious) if the disposer follows through with delivery. This distinction matters for liability.

Key Terms
تصرف (tasarruf): Any act of dealing with property, whether physical (actual) or verbal (contractual).
إذن (idhn): Permission; can come from the owner (idhn al-malik) or from the Shari'a (idhn al-shar').
غاصب (ghasib): Usurper; one who takes another's property wrongfully. In this rule, a disposer without permission is treated like a ghasib.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what distinguishes a verbal disposition from an actual disposition in terms of liability?
A) Verbal dispositions are never tortious.
B) Verbal dispositions become tortious only if followed by delivery.
C) Verbal dispositions are always tortious if no permission exists.
D) Both are equally tortious regardless of delivery.

Key Takeaway
Majalla Qawida 27 prohibits unauthorized disposition of another's property, but actual and verbal acts are treated differently: only actual acts or executed verbal acts trigger liability as ghasp.

The Maxim on Disposition in Another's Property Without Permission
Explaining the maxim on unauthorized disposition and its subdivisions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'No one may dispose of another's property without permission' to classify a given disposition as actual (ghasb) or verbal (fuduli) and determine the legal consequences.

Core Idea
A disposition in another's property without permission is either actual (liability) or verbal (suspended pending ratification).

This lesson examines a foundational legal maxim that governs all dealings with property in Islamic law. You will learn how the Hanafi school distinguishes between actual acts of usurpation and verbal contracts made without authority, and how ratification and custom can alter the ruling.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Write down the maxim in Arabic and English. Identify that it governs all dispositions in another's property without permission, covering both actual acts and verbal contracts.
The maxim is general, but exceptions exist.
2
Classify the disposition as actual or verbal
Given a scenario, decide whether the disposition involves a physical act (taking, consuming, digging) or remains a verbal contract (selling, leasing).
If delivery follows a verbal contract, it becomes actual.
3
Apply the appropriate consequence
For actual dispositions, rule that it is ghasb and the disposer is liable for damage. For verbal dispositions without delivery, rule that the contract is suspended (fuduli) pending ratification.
No liability for the fuduli if the owner rejects.
4
Identify exceptions
Recognize that permission may come from custom (shepherd slaughtering a dying sheep) or from legal guardianship (wali/wasi). These are not violations.
Custom is authoritative in such cases.
5
Derive the branch rule on commanding
Apply the derived rule that commanding someone to dispose of another's property is invalid, and the direct doer bears liability unless deceived.
This stems from 'he who does not possess cannot give'.

Chapter Ten: General Legal Maxims in Fiqh
... a right according to the duration, and he guarantees beyond that the value of the principal on the day of destruction if it is destroyed. Ref: al-Mughni and al-Sharh al-Kabir, Book of Ghasb, 5/402. And (436). Exceeding: The Hanafis restrict this maxim by [the condition] that the rent has not become settled upon the person before he enters a state of guarantee for the property. If the specified rent had become settled upon him previously, such as if he first received the benefit contracted for, until he became a transgressor in the ruling of a usurper, then – although he becomes liable for the risk of destruction of the leased property – he is obliged to pay the rent according to them if it does not perish. But if the leased property perishes after the transgression before returning to the owner, then he guarantees it and there is no rent upon him in that case in any event because the guarantee of benefits merges into the guarantee of the principal. 81/85 – The Twenty-Seventh Maxim: 'No one may dispose of another's property without permission' (M/96). The wording of the maxim in the Majalla: 'without his permission' i.e. the owner's permission. We have preferred to omit the pronoun to include permission from the Law. Disposition in another's property is either actual or verbal by way of contracting: A – Actual disposition in another's property by taking, consuming, digging in the ground, and the like, without permission, is considered transgression. B – As for verbal disposition by way of contracting, such as selling another's property, gifting, mortgaging, leasing, lending, depositing, etc., it has detail: – If the disposer follows it with execution by delivery, it becomes actual disposition and takes the ruling of ghasb. – If his disposition remains in the realm of speech, it is fuduli. The contract of a fuduli is suspended pending the owner's ratification. Nothing is owed by the disposer because there is no harm from these purely verbal dispositions as long as the owner has the option to accept or reject it. Ratification applies to actions as well as words; an actual disposition without the owner's permission, if the owner later ratifies it, becomes permissible. The effectiveness of a guardian or executor's disposition over a minor is not an exception; rather it is permitted by the Law because it is based on guardianship. From the maxim it becomes clear that the 'non-permissibility' expressed in this maxim is interpreted in two different meanings depending on whether the disposition is actual or verbal: In case of actual disposition without the owner's permission, non-permissibility is interpreted as prohibition necessitating liability. – In case of verbal disposition, non-permissibility is interpreted as lack of effectiveness. Branches that derive from it: 81/86 – A – Commanding disposition in another's property is invalid (M/95). Because one who lacks a thing cannot give it; so one who does not own the disposition cannot command it. Accordingly, if a person commands another to take another's property, or throw it into the sea or burn it, or slaughter his sheep, his command is disregarded, and liability is on the doer. This is if the commanded person knows that the property belongs to someone other than the commander. But if he is not aware and the commander makes him think it is his, as if he said, 'Slaughter this sheep of mine' (with the possessive 'my' pronoun), then the owner may hold the commanded person liable, and the commanded person may recourse against the commander for what he guaranteed because of his misrepresentation. – Among the important juristic branches of this maxim is such as when a person deposits some...
Source pages p. 975

Commentary
This passage presents a complete legal maxim from the Majalla (Article 96) and its subdivisions. The author begins by noting a Hanafi qualification on a prior rule about guarantee and rent: if the rent has become settled before the person becomes a guarantor of the property, then the rent remains due unless the property perishes, in which case no rent is owed because the guarantee of benefits merges into the guarantee of the principal. This sets the stage for the main maxim. The maxim states: 'No one may dispose of another's property without permission.' The author notes that the pronoun 'his' in the Majalla wording implicitly refers to the owner, but they prefer to omit it to include permission from the Law (e.g., a guardian's authority). The maxim is then analyzed into two types: actual and verbal disposition. Actual disposition (taking, consuming, digging) without permission constitutes transgression, making the disposer a usurper liable for damages. However, permission may come from the Law or custom, as illustrated by a shepherd slaughtering a hopelessly injured sheep – this is not liability because custom implicitly permits it. Verbal disposition (selling, gifting, leasing, etc.) if followed by delivery becomes actual and is treated as usurpation; if it remains in speech, it is a 'fuduli' (unauthorized) contract, which is suspended pending the owner's ratification. No liability attaches to the fuduli because the owner can reject the contract, so no harm is done. Ratification can retroactively validate even actual dispositions. The passage clarifies that guardians (of minors) are not exceptions because they derive authority from the Law. The maxim's 'non-permissibility' thus has two meanings: for actual acts, it means prohibition with liability; for verbal acts, it means lack of enforceability (suspended). Then the passage turns to subsidiary rules derived from this maxim. The first is Article 95: 'Commanding disposition in another's property is invalid.' The reason: one who lacks ownership cannot give it. Examples: if someone commands another to take or destroy another's property, the command is void, and the doer is liable (if he knew it was not the commander's). If the commander deceived the doer into thinking it was his own, the doer may seek recourse. Further branches include scenarios of deposit (the text cuts off). This passage demonstrates how a general maxim is expanded through classification and applied to specific cases, showing the systematic nature of Hanafi legal reasoning.

Key Terms
التصرف الفضولي (al-tasarruf al-fuduli): A verbal disposition (contract) made by a person without the owner's permission, which remains suspended pending ratification.
الغصب (ghasb): Unlawful taking of another's property by force or transgression, with liability for damages and the principal.
الإجازة (ijaza): Post facto ratification by the owner that validates a fuduli contract or transforms an actual disposition into a permissible one.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The sale is void and the seller is liable for damages.
B) The sale is suspended and the seller may be liable if the owner rejects it.
C) The sale is suspended pending the owner’s ratification, and the seller is not liable.
D) The sale is valid if the seller repents.

Key Takeaway
The permissibility of disposition hinges on permission; actual dispositions without permission entail liability, while verbal ones are suspended pending ratification.

The Dual Meaning of 'Adam al-Jawaz' in the Hanafi Legal Maxim
Explaining the dual interpretation of 'adam al-jawaz'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two meanings of 'adam al-jawaz' as applied to physical and verbal dispositions without permission.

Core Idea
For physical acts, 'non-permissibility' means prohibition with liability; for verbal acts, it means lack of enforceability.

In this passage, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa clarifies a critical ambiguity in the Majalla's maxim about disposition without permission. The term 'adam al-jawaz' is not monolithic—it carries two distinct legal meanings depending on whether the disposition is physical or verbal. This distinction is foundational for applying the maxim correctly and for understanding the subsidiary rules that follow.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of dispositions
The passage distinguishes between physical (fi'li) and verbal (qawli) dispositions. The fuduli (unauthorized agent) is an example of a verbal disposition.
2
Map each type to its meaning of 'adam al-jawaz'
For physical: 'adam al-jawaz' means prohibition that triggers liability (daman). For verbal: it means lack of enforceability (adam al-nifadh) until ratified.
3
Understand the role of ratification
Ratification (ijaza) applies to both types. For verbal, ratification makes the contract enforceable. For physical, it retroactively authorizes the act, removing liability.
4
Note the exception for guardians
Guardians and executors (wali/wasi) are not fuduli because their authority is from the Law, not from the owner's permission.

والإجازة تلحق الأفعال كالأقوال، فالتصرُّف الفعلي بلا إذن المالك إذا أجازه المالك بعدئذ انقلب مأذوناً.
ونفاذ تصرف الولي أو الوصي على القاصر ليس استثناء بل هو مأذون به شرعاً لأنه مستند إلى ولاية.
يتضح من ذلك أن عدم الجواز المعبر به في هذه القاعدة، يُفسّر بمعنيين مختلفين بحسب كون التصرف فعلياً أو قولياً:
ففي حالة التصرف الفعلي دون إذن المالك يفسر عدم الجواز بمعنى المنع الموجب للضمان.
- وفي حالة التصرف القولي يفسر عدم الجواز بمعنى عدم النفاذ.
ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد:
.
٨٦/٨١ - أ - الأمر بالتصرف في ملك الغير «باطل» (م۹٥) . لأن فاقد الشيء لا يعطيه؛ فمن لا يملك التصرف لا يملك الأمر به. وعلى هذا لو أمر شخص غيره بأن يأخذ مال آخر، أو يلقيه في البحر أو يحرقه، أو أن يذبح شاته، فلا عبره لأمره، والضمان على الفاعل. هذا إذا كان المأمور عالماً بأن المال لغير الآمر . أما لو لم يكن عالماً وأوهمه الأمر أنه له كما لو قال له : اذبح لي شاتي هذه (بياء المتكلم)، فإن لصاحب المال تضمين المأمور، وللمأمور أن يرجع على الأمر بما ضمن لتغريره إياه.
- ومن الفروع الفقهية المهمة لهذه القاعدة ما لو أودع شخص بعض ملك ماله وقال للوديع : إذا أنا مت فادفعه لابني ففعل، وكان للميت المودع وارث آخر، فإن الوديع يضمن لأن الوديعة بعد موت المودع تصبح الورثة جميعاً، فأمره بالدفع إلى أحدهم باطل.
وكذا لو أمره بدفعه إلى أجنبي غير وارث (من شرح والدي رحمه الله على القواعد نقلاً عن الفصل / ۲۸/ من جامع الفصولين). ولو قال الدائن المدين: ارم ديني في البحر فرمى هذا مقدار الدين لم يبرأ، لأنَّ الدين شاغل للذمة اعتباراً، وليس متعلقاً بعين مال المدين. فقد رمى مال نفسه، والأمر باطل، وعليه دفع الدين. (ر: شرح هذه القاعدة للعلامة علي حيدر).
على أنَّ هذه القاعدة مقيَّدة بأن لا يكون الآمر مجبراً كما سنرى في القاعدة/ ۲۸
۸۷/۸۱ - ب - لا يجوز لأحد أن يأخذ مال أحد بلا سبب (م/۹۷).
لأنه إذا لم يكن للشخص حق التصرف القولي في مال الغير بلا إذنه كما أفادته القاعدتان السابقتان فبمقتضى الأولوية ليس له أخذ مال الغير بلا مسوغ شرعي. فإذا أخذه كان ضامناً حتى يرده، لقول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام: على اليد ما أخذت حتى تؤديه. رواه أحمد في مسنده وأصحاب السنن الأربعة ر ف (۲/۱۰(ب).
وعلى هذا يلزم رد بدل الصلح (۱) إذا اعترف قابضه بعد الصلح أنه لم يكن له حق .
(1) بدل الصلح هو ما يتفق المتصالحان على أن يدفعه أحدهما للآخر حسماً للنزاع في الحق المتنازع فيه (ر: ١٨/٤٦).
Chapter (81): Explanation and Classification of the Rules of the Majalla. - If his disposition remains in the realm of speech, it is acting as an unauthorized agent (fuduli). The contract of an unauthorized agent is suspended upon the owner's ratification. And nothing is upon the agent since there is no harm from these purely verbal dispositions as long as the option to accept or reject is for him or the owner. And ratification attaches to actions just as to words, so a physical disposition without the owner's permission, if the owner later ratifies it, becomes authorized. And the effectiveness of the disposition of a guardian or executor over a minor is not an exception; rather, it is authorized by the Law because it is based on guardianship. It becomes clear from this that the expression 'non-permissibility' used in this rule is interpreted in two different meanings according to whether the disposition is physical or verbal: In the case of a physical disposition without the owner's permission, non-permissibility is interpreted as prohibition that necessitates liability. And in the case of a verbal disposition, non-permissibility is interpreted as lack of enforceability. What branches from it of the rules: 86/81 - A: 'An order to dispose of the property of another is void' (Art. 95). Because one lacking a thing cannot give it; so whoever does not have the right to dispose does not have the right to order it. According to this, if a person orders another to take the property of someone else, or to throw it into the sea, or burn it, or to slaughter his sheep, then his order is of no consequence, and the liability is upon the doer. This is if the one ordered knows that the property belongs to other than the orderer. But if he does not know and the orderer makes him believe it is his own, as if he says 'Slaughter this sheep of mine' (with the possessive 'my'), then the owner has the right to hold the doer liable, and the doer has the right to recourse against the orderer for what he paid due to his deception. And among the important fiqh applications of this rule: if a person deposits some of his property and says to the depositee: 'If I die, give it to my son,' and he does so, and the deceased depositor has another heir, then the depositee is liable because the deposit after the death of the depositor becomes the property of all the heirs, so his order to pay to one of them is void. Likewise if he orders him to pay it to a non-heir stranger. (From my father's commentary on the rules, quoting from chapter 28 of Jami' al-Fusulain). And if a creditor says to the debtor: 'Throw my debt into the sea,' and he throws the amount of the debt, he is not discharged, because the debt is an obligation on the liability (dhimma) notionally, and it is not attached to the debtor's specific property. He has thrown his own property, and the order is void, and he must pay the debt. (See: explanation of this rule by Allama Ali Haydar). However, this rule is conditioned that the orderer not be a coercer, as we will see in rule/28. 87/81 - B: 'It is not permissible for anyone to take the property of another without a cause' (Art. 97). Because if a person does not have the right of verbal disposition over another's property without his permission, as the two previous rules have indicated, then by the more cogent reason (awlawiyya) he does not have the right to take another's property without a legal justification. So if he takes it, he is liable until he returns it, due to the saying of the Messenger (peace be upon him): 'Liability lies on the hand for what it has taken until it returns it.' Reported by Ahmad in his Musnad and the authors of the four Sunan. (Vol. 2, p. 10). According to this, it is obligatory to return the substitute of a compromise (badal al-sulh) if the recipient after the compromise acknowledges that he had no right to it. (1) The substitute of a compromise is what the two parties agree that one should pay to the other to settle a dispute over a contested right (see: 18/46).
Source pages p. 976

Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a commentary on the Majalla's legal maxims, specifically explaining a key maxim about disposition without permission. The author first clarifies the dual meaning of 'adam al-jawaz' (non-permissibility) as used in the maxim: for physical acts (e.g., physically taking property), it means prohibition that triggers liability (daman); for verbal acts (e.g., a contract by a fuduli), it means lack of enforceability (adam al-nifadh) until ratification. This distinction is crucial for correctly applying the maxim. The passage then derives two subsidiary maxims from this principle: (1) 'An order to dispose of another's property is void' (Majalla Art. 95) because one who does not own disposition cannot command it. The author provides three applications: ordering harm to another's property (the doer is liable unless deceived), a deposit case where the depositor's order to give to one heir after death is void if there are other heirs, and a creditor's order to the debtor to throw away the debt (the debtor remains liable because the debt is a liability, not attached to a specific asset). An exception is noted: the rule does not apply if the orderer is a coercer (mujbir). (2) 'It is not permissible to take another's property without a cause' (Majalla Art. 97) based on the hadith 'ala al-yad ma akhadhat hatta tu'addihi. An application is that if someone acknowledges after a settlement that he had no right to the compromised sum, he must return it. The passage reinforces the Hanafi emphasis on causation in liability: liability arises from unauthorized taking, even if the taker later discovers he had no right.

Key Terms
الفضولي (al-fuduli): The unauthorized agent who makes a verbal disposition (contract) in someone else's property without permission; his contract is suspended on ratification.
عدم الجواز (adam al-jawaz): In this rule, it has two meanings: for physical acts it means prohibition with liability; for verbal acts it means lack of enforceability (non-nifadh).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the meaning of 'adam al-jawaz' when applied to a verbal disposition (e.g., a fuduli contract)?
A) Prohibition with liability
B) Lack of enforceability
C) Void ab initio
D) Permissible with condition

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi legal maxims, 'adam al-jawaz' has two meanings: prohibition with liability for physical acts, and lack of enforceability for verbal acts.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
10 min
The Subsidiary Maxim: An Order to Dispose of Another's Property is Void
Derivation and application of the subsidiary maxim on ordering disposition of another's property.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'An order to dispose of another's property is void' to various scenarios and identify when it is restricted.

Core Idea
An order to dispose of another's property is void; the doer is liable unless deceived, and even a creditor cannot discharge a debt by ordering its destruction.

This passage derives a practical maxim from the principle that one cannot authorize what one does not own. The rule 'An order to dispose of another's property is void' is applied to several concrete cases: unauthorized destruction, deceit, deposit after death, and debt discharge. Each case tests the limits of the maxim. An important restriction is noted: the rule does not apply if the orderer is a coercer (mujbir), which will be explained later.

Guided Path
1
State the derived maxim and its rationale
The maxim is 'الأمر بالتصرف في ملك الغير باطل' (Majalla Art. 95). The rationale: 'فاقد الشيء لا يعطيه' (one lacking a thing cannot give it).
2
Apply to the basic case
If A orders B to take or destroy C's property knowingly, the order is void and B is liable (daman).
3
Examine the deception exception
If B is deceived by A into thinking the property is A's, B is liable to C but has recourse (ruju') against A for taghrir (deception).
4
Analyze the deposit application
A depositor's order to the depositee to give the deposit to one heir after death is void if other heirs exist; the depositee is liable to all heirs.
5
Analyze the debt application
A creditor's order to the debtor to throw the debt into the sea does not discharge the debtor, because the debt is a liability on the dhimma, not attached to specific property.

Commentary
This passage from 'Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm' by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa is a commentary on the Majalla's legal maxims, specifically explaining a key maxim about disposition without permission. Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate

The Hanafi Maxim: No Taking of Property Without Cause
Presenting a legal maxim with proof and furuat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim 'La yajuz li ahad an yakhudha mal ahad bi-la sabab', identify its textual proof from the hadith, apply it to the four provided furuat, and identify the exception for established rights.

Core Idea
Taking another's property without a legally recognized cause is impermissible and creates liability.

This lesson presents a foundational legal maxim that protects property rights in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim is supported by both rational argument and a prophetic hadith. We will explore its applications to various scenarios including settlements, mistaken payments, lost property, and prescription, as well as the important exception when a right is established.

Guided Path
1
Memorise the maxim
Learn the Arabic wording 'لا يجوز لأحد أن يأخذ مال أحد بلا سبب' and its English meaning. This is the core rule governing all property takings.
The maxim is a sughra (minor) qāʿida but applies broadly.
2
Understand the proofs
Examine the rational argument (a fortiori from the prohibition of verbal disposition without permission) and the textual proof (the hadith 'The hand is liable for what it has taken until it returns it'). Identify the type of proof and its strength.
The hadith is reported by Ahmad and the four Sunan; it is a primary textual evidence.
3
Analyse the furuat
For each of the four cases (badal al-ṣulḥ, mistaken payment, luqṭa, taqādum), explain how the maxim applies and what ruling follows. Note the specific conditions.
The furuat illustrate the scope of the maxim across different chapters.
4
Study the istithnā'
Understand the exception: taking based on an established right is permissible. Focus on the later jurists' fatwa allowing creditor self-help from a procrastinating debtor's property.
This exception highlights the distinction between ḥukm al-diyāna and ḥukm al-qaḍā'.
5
Apply to a new case
Use the maxim and its exception to derive the ruling for a novel scenario, such as a person taking a lost item intending charity vs. intending personal use.
Practice ensures mastery of applying the maxim to unfamiliar facts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
87/81 - ب - لا يجوز لأحد أن يأخذ مال أحد بلا سبب (م/97).
لأنه إذا لم يكن للشخص حق التصرف القولي في مال الغير بلا إذنه [؟] كما أفادته القاعدتان السابقتان فبمقتضى الأولوية ليس له أخذ مال الغير بلا مسوغ شرعي. فإذا أخذه كان ضامناً حتى يرده، لقول الرسول عليه الصلاة والسلام: على اليد ما أخذت حتى تؤديه. رواه أحمد في مسنده وأصحاب السنن الأربعة ر ف (2/10(ب).
وعلى هذا يلزم رد بدل الصلح (1) إذا اعترف قابضه بعد الصلح أنه لم يكن له حق .
(1) بدل الصلح هو ما يتفق المتصالحان على أن يدفعه أحدهما للآخر حسماً للنزاع في الحق المتنازع فيه (ر: 18/46).
- وكذا يجب رد ما دفعه الإنسان على ظن أنه مدين ثم تبين خلافه كما تقدم (ر: ف 16/81).
وإذا التقط الشخص اللقطة لنفسه كان غاصباً ضامناً. فيجب أن يلتقطها بقصد حفظها وتعريفها وردها لصاحبها متى ظهر. وإن لم يظهر صاحبها فسبيلها الصدقة.
- وعلى هذا أيضاً كان التقادم (أي مرور الزمان) إنما يمنع فقط سماع الدعوى بالحق أو بالعين أمام القضاء. ولكن المدعى عليه لا يسقط عنه الحق ولا يتملك الشيء بالتقادم، بل عليه رده في حكم الديانة، لأن التقادم ليس سبباً مملكاً من أسباب الملكية، وإنما هو حاجز قضائي كما تقدم بيانه (ر ف 4/23).
أما إذا كان أخذ مال الغير مستنداً إلى حق ثابت فإنه يجوز ولو دون رضى صاحب المال.
بل قد أفتى المتأخرون بأنه يجوز من جهة الديانة أخذ الدائن حقه الصحيح الثابت من مال المدين المماطل أو الجاحد إذا دخل في يد الدائن ولو بدون علم المدين ومن غير جنس الدين وليس مقضياً به، وذلك لفساد الذمم كما تقدم وإن كان نظام القضاء في الشريعة لا يقر له هذا الأخذ. وهذا مما يختلف فيه حكم الديانة عن القضاء (ر: ف ٤/٢ و ٢٣/ ١٠ الحاشية الأخيرة).
87/81 - B - It is not permissible for anyone to take the property of another without cause (M/97). Because if a person does not have the right of verbal disposition over another's property without his permission, as the two previous rules indicated, then a fortiori he does not have the right to take another's property without a legitimate justification. If he takes it, he is liable to guarantee it until he returns it, due to the saying of the Messenger, peace be upon him: 'The hand is liable for what it has taken until it returns it.' Reported by Ahmad in his Musnad and the authors of the four Sunan (2/10(b)). On this basis, the substitute of a settlement (badal al-sulh) must be returned if the recipient admits after the settlement that he had no right. (1) Badal al-sulh is what the two parties to a settlement agree that one will pay to the other to resolve a dispute over the contested right (ref: 18/46). Likewise, what a person pays thinking he is a debtor, then it becomes clear otherwise, must be returned, as previously mentioned (ref: F 16/81). And if a person picks up a lost item for himself, he is considered a usurper and is liable. He must pick it up with the intention of preserving it, announcing it, and returning it to its owner when he appears. If its owner does not appear, then it should be given as charity. Similarly, prescription (i.e., passage of time) only prevents hearing the claim of a right or a specific thing in court. But the defendant is not relieved of the right nor does he own the thing by prescription; rather, he must return it in terms of religious duty, because prescription is not a cause of ownership among the causes of ownership; it is merely a procedural barrier as previously explained (ref: F 4/23). As for taking another's property based on a established right, it is permissible even without the owner's consent. In fact, the later jurists have issued fatwa that it is permissible, from the religious duty perspective, for a creditor to take his established right from the property of a procrastinating or denying debtor if it comes into the creditor's hand, even without the debtor's knowledge, and even if it is not of the same type as the debt and not adjudicated, due to the corruption of consciences as previously mentioned, even though the judicial system in Sharia does not approve of this taking. This is one of the matters where the religious duty ruling differs from the judicial ruling (ref: F 4/2 and 23/10, last footnote).
Source pages p. 977

Commentary
This passage presents a legal maxim (qāʿida) from the Hanafi school: It is impermissible for anyone to take another's property without a legally recognized cause. The author first provides the rational proof: since one cannot verbally dispose of another's property without permission, taking it is even more prohibited. Then he cites a hadith as textual proof: 'The hand is liable for what it has taken until it returns it.' This hadith is reported by Imam Ahmad and the authors of the four Sunan, and it establishes the principle of liability for wrongful taking. The author then gives several applications: (1) If after a settlement (ṣulḥ) the recipient admits he had no right, he must return the settlement amount (badal al-ṣulḥ). (2) If someone pays money thinking he is a debtor but later discovers he was not, he is entitled to its return. (3) A person who picks up a lost item (luqṭa) for himself becomes a guarantor (ḍāmin) and must treat it properly: preserve it, announce it, and return it to the owner; if the owner never appears, it should be given as charity. (4) Prescription (taqādum) does not transfer ownership or extinguish the right; it only bars the claim in court; in religious duty (diyāna), the right remains and must be fulfilled. Then the author notes the exception: if the taking is based on an established right, it is permissible even without the owner's consent. As an example, later Hanafi jurists (mutāʾkhkhirūn) allowed a creditor to take his debt from the property of a procrastinating or denying debtor when it comes into his hand, even without the debtor's knowledge, due to moral decay, though the court would not sanction it. This highlights the distinction between the ruling in religious duty (ḥukm al-diyāna) and the ruling in judiciary (ḥukm al-qaḍāʾ).

Key Terms
بدل الصلح (badal al-ṣulḥ): The consideration paid by one party to the other to settle a dispute over a contested right.
التقادم (al-taqādum): The passage of time that bars the hearing of a claim in court but does not extinguish the right or transfer ownership.
ضمان (ḍamān): Liability to compensate or guarantee the return of property taken wrongfully.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the ruling on a person who picks up a lost item (luqṭa) with the intention of keeping it for himself?
A) He becomes an owner after one year.
B) He is a usurper (ghāṣib) and is liable (ḍāmin).
C) He can use it as his own immediately.
D) He must give it to the ruler.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'no taking without cause' is fundamental, but it is qualified by the exception for established rights and the distinction between religious duty and judicial enforceability.

Attribution of Action: Doer vs. Commander
Maxim with two furūʿ examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'يضاف الفعل إلى الفاعل لا الأمر ما لم يكن مجبراً' to determine liability in cases of command and coercion, and identify the exception.

Core Idea
The doer is liable unless the commander was coerced—then liability shifts.

When someone orders another to commit a harmful act, who bears responsibility? This lesson introduces a key Hanafi maxim that answers that question: the action is attributed to the direct doer, not the commander, unless the commander was under duress. This principle is essential for muftis dealing with cases of property damage and personal injury involving orders and coercion.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Read the maxim carefully. Note that it applies only to actions involving transgression against property or persons (taʿaddī ʿalā māl aw nafs). This distinguishes it from other areas where command may be relevant.
The maxim is a ḍābiṭ (sub-maxim) that restricts the general rule of attribution.
2
Identify the general rule and the exception
The general rule: action is attributed to the doer (fāʿil). Exception: unless the commander is coerced (mujbir). In the exception, the doer becomes a mere tool (āla) and the commander is treated as the effective actor.
Coercion (ikrāh) nullifies free will in Hanafi jurisprudence.
3
Apply the maxim to the furūʿ (examples)
Work through the two examples: (a) A orders B to destroy C's property voluntarily – B is liable. (b) A orders B to dig a hole in the road voluntarily – B is liable for harm. Consider what would happen if A were coerced.
The examples are from ghaṣb (usurpation) and jināyāt (injuries).
4
Confirm the istithnāʾ (exception)
When the commander is coerced, the ruling flips: the commander is liable because the doer is his tool. This is derived from the fiqh of ikrāh.
Exception is part of the maxim itself: 'مالم يكن مجبراً'.
5
Relate to other qawāʿid
Note how this maxim complements others, e.g., 'لا ينسب إلى الساكت قول' (no statement is attributed to one who remains silent). Both deal with attribution.
Hierarchy: this is a sughrā (minor) maxim under the kubrā 'الأمور بمقاصدها'?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
88/81 - القاعدة الثامنة والعشرون: يضاف الفعل إلى الفاعل لا الأمر، ما لم يكن مجبراً (م/ 89). وذلك لأن الأمر إذا كان مجبراً كان المأمور في حكم الآلة (ر: الدر المختار، وحاشيته في الإكراه 85/5 . المراد بالفعل هنا ما فيه تعد على مال أو نفس: فلو أمر شخص آخر بإتلاف مال الغير، أو بحفر حفرة في الطريق
88/81 - The twenty-eighth rule: The action is attributed to the doer, not the commander, unless he is coerced (M/89). This is because when the commander is coerced, the commanded person is considered a tool (ref: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its commentary on ikrah 5/85). What is meant by 'action' here is that which involves transgression against property or person: For example, if one person orders another to destroy the property of a third party, or to dig a hole in the road
Source pages p. 978

Commentary
This passage presents another legal maxim of the Hanafi school: the action (fiʿl) is attributed to the direct doer (fāʿil) and not to the commander (āmir), unless the commander is under coercion (mujbir). The reasoning is that when the commander is coerced, the person executing the command becomes like a mere tool (āla), and thus the agency is shifted to the commander. The maxim specifically applies to actions that involve aggression against property or persons (mā fīhi taʿaddī ʿalā māl aw nafs). Two examples are mentioned: (1) someone orders another to destroy a third party's property – the direct destroyer is liable unless he was coerced; (2) someone orders another to dig a hole in the public road – the digger is responsible for any harm caused. The text references Al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss on coercion (ikrāh) for further details. This maxim is a sub-principle (ḍābiṭ) that restricts the general rule that actions are attributed to the doer.

Key Terms
مجبر (mujbir): A person who is coerced (under duress) and thus commands someone else to perform an act; the command is not truly voluntary.
آلة (āla): Tool; the person executing the command under coercion is considered a mere instrument of the commander.
تعد (taʿaddī): Transgression, aggression against another's right, especially property or life.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, if A voluntarily orders B to destroy C's property and B does so, who is liable?
A) A alone
B) B alone
C) Both A and B
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The doer bears liability for harmful acts ordered by a non-coerced commander; only when the commander is coerced does liability shift.

The Twenty-Eighth Maxim: Attribution of Acts (Doer vs. Commander)
Explaining the 28th maxim with furuat and istithna'at.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the 28th maxim to determine liability in command scenarios and identify the three exceptions where the commander is liable.

Core Idea
An act is attributed to its physical doer, not the commander – unless compulsion, authority, or benefit shifts liability.

This passage from the Majalla introduces a fundamental maxim governing liability when one person commands another to act. The default rule is that the doer bears responsibility, but crucial exceptions arise when the commander compels, when the commander is a sultan, or when the act benefits the commander. Understanding these nuances is essential for a mufti dealing with torts, contracts, and coercion cases.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its core principle
Begin with the maxim: 'The act is attributed to the doer, not the commander, unless he is compelled.' This establishes who is liable by default.
The maxim is from the Majalla (Article 89).
2
Identify the default attribution: doer liable
When A commands B to do an act, B (the doer) is responsible for the consequences, not A (the commander). This applies to torts, crimes, and contracts.
Example: If A orders B to destroy C's property, B is liable.
3
Recognize the exception of compulsion (mujbir)
If the commander compels the doer (ikrāh mulji'), the commander becomes liable because the doer is like a tool. The doer may have a right to recover from the commander.
Compulsion can be physical or coercive threats.
4
Distinguish constructive compulsion (sultan, adult over minor)
A sultan's command is treated as compulsion due to his authority. Similarly, an adult commanding a minor is constructive compulsion. In both cases, the commander is ultimately liable.
The minor may be liable initially but can recover from the adult commander.
5
Apply the agency exception when the act benefits the commander
If the commanded act is for the commander's benefit (e.g., paying his debt, building his house), the relationship becomes agency (wakālah). The commander bears the cost or liability.
This is a restriction on the maxim, not an exception to the default.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸۸/۸۱ - القاعدة الثامنة والعشرون: يضاف الفعل إلى الفاعل لا الأمر، ما لم يكن مجبراً» (م/ ۸۹).
وذلك لأن الأمر إذا كان مجبراً كان المأمور في حكم الآلة (ر: الدر المختار، وحاشيته في الإكراه ٨٥/٥ .
المراد بالفعل هنا ما فيه تعد على مال أو نفس:
فلو أمر شخص آخر بإتلاف مال الغير، أو بحفر حفرة في الطريق العام فوقع فيها حيوان أو بارتكاب جريمة ففعل ذلك، كان المأمور هو المسؤول الضامن، لأنه هو الفاعل دون الآمر .
- ومن أمر غيره بعقد متعلق بالمأمور نفسه كان المأمور العاقد هو الملتزم بعقده ولو أنَّ العقد في غير مصلحته.
- بخلاف ما لو أكرهه الآمر على العقد، فإنّ له الخيار في الإمضاء أو الفسخ بعد زوال الإكراه.
ومتى أضيف الفعل إلى الآمر لكونه مجبراً (بصيغة الفاعل) ترتبت في حقه مسؤولية المكره (بصيغة الفاعل المفصَّلَة في بحث الإكراه من الكتب الفقهية، بحسب كون الإكراه مُلجئاً أو غير ملجىء. وكونه إكراهاً على تصرف قولي أو على فعل. وقد تقدمت خلاصة عنها في بحث الإكراه من عيوب الرضا في نظرية العقود، وفي شرح القواعد (ر: ف ٤/٣٤ و ٨١ .(۳۲
والفروع الفقهية تدل على أن القاعدة مقيدة بأن لا يكون الفعل المأمور به لمصلحة الآمر وإلا كان الأمر عندئذ في حكم الوكالة يقوم فيها المأمور مقام الآمر في حدود الأمر، وينفذ عليه تصرفه.
وذلك كما لو أمره بقضاء دين على الآمر، أو بالإنفاق عليه أو بناء داره مثلاً، فإنَّ المأمور عندئذ يكون كوكيل يرجع على الأمر بما دفع أو أنفق .
-
والإكراه الذي يضيف الفعل إلى الآمر قد يكون حكمياً تقديرياً كما لو كان الآمر سلطاناً فإن أمره إكراه ( ر ) قواعد الحمزاوي مسائل الغصب ص / ١٩٦).
- ومثل ذلك ما لو كان الأمر بالغاً عاقلاً والمأمور قاصراً صبياً أو مجنوناً، فإنَّ المأمور يضمن أولاً نتائج ما يفعل أو يتلف، ولكنه يرجع على الأمر، لأنَّ أمر الكبير العاقل للقاصر في حكم الإكراه.
88/81 - The twenty-eighth maxim: 'The act is attributed to the doer, not the commander, unless he is compelled' (Majalla Article 89). That is because if the commander is a compeller, the commanded person is in the position of a tool (ref: al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss on coercion 5/85). What is meant by 'act' here is any transgression against property or life. For example: if a person orders another to destroy someone's property, or to dig a hole in the public road so an animal falls into it, or to commit a crime, and he does it, the commanded person is the responsible and liable party, because he is the doer, not the commander. - And if someone orders another to enter into a contract concerning the commanded person himself, the commanded contracting party is the one bound by the contract, even if the contract is not in his interest. - This is different from if the commander compels him to the contract, for then he has the option to confirm or rescind after the coercion ceases. Whenever the act is attributed to the commander because he is a compeller (in the active participle form), the liability of the compeller (in the active participle form, detailed in the discussion of coercion in fiqh books, according to whether the coercion is compelling or non-compelling, and whether it is coercion for a verbal act or a physical act) attaches to him. A summary of this has preceded in the discussion of coercion as a defect of consent in contract theory, and in the explanation of the rules (ref: 4/34 and 81, 32). The fiqh branches indicate that the rule is restricted by [the condition] that the commanded act not be for the benefit of the commander; otherwise, the command in that case is considered an agency, in which the commanded person stands in the place of the commander within the limits of the command, and his action is binding on the commander. For example, if he orders him to pay a debt on behalf of the commander, or to spend on him, or to build his house, then the commanded person is like an agent who can recover from the commander what he paid or spent. - And the coercion that attributes the act to the commander may be notional and constructive, as when the commander is a sultan, for his command is coercion (ref: Qawāʿid al-Ḥamzāwī, chapter on usurpation, p. 196). - And similarly, if the commander is a mature, sane adult and the commanded person is a minor, child, or insane, then the commanded person is initially liable for the results of what he does or destroys, but he can recover [damages] from the commander, because the command of a mature sane adult to a minor is considered coercion.
Source pages p. 978

Commentary
This passage presents the twenty-eighth maxim from the Majalla, a fundamental legal principle in Hanafi fiqh. The maxim states that the legal responsibility for an action falls on the actual doer, not on the person who commanded it, unless the commander compelled the doer. The author explains the rationale: when the commander compels, the doer is like a mere tool. The maxim applies to any act that transgresses against property or life. Several examples are given: 1) If A orders B to destroy C's property or to dig a dangerous hole, B is liable; 2) If A orders B to enter into a contract concerning B himself (e.g., to sell his own property), B is bound by the contract; 3) If A compels B to enter a contract, B has the option to rescind after coercion ends. The author then introduces an important restriction: if the commanded act is for the benefit of the commander, the command is treated as an agency (wakālah), and the commander is ultimately liable. Examples include ordering someone to pay the commander's debt or build his house. The passage also discusses two special cases where the commander is considered to have compelled the doer constructively: when the commander is a sultan (his command is effectively compulsion) and when the commander is an adult and the doer is a minor (the minor can initially be liable but can recover from the adult commander). This maxim is crucial in tort law, contract law, and criminal liability within the Hanafi school.

Key Terms
مجبر (mujbir): A commander who compels the doer, so the action is attributed to the commander.
إكراه (ikrāh): Coercion that forces a person to act against his will; can be mulji' (overpowering) or ghayr mulji' (non-overpowering).
مأمور (ma'mūr): The person who is commanded to perform an action.
آمر (āmir): The person who issues the command.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the 28th maxim, if a person orders another to destroy someone's property without compulsion, who is liable?
A) The commander.
B) The doer.
C) Both equally.
D) Neither.

Key Takeaway
The 28th maxim establishes doer liability unless the commander compels, is a sultan, or benefits from the act, making it a cornerstone of Hanafi tort and contract law.

The General Rule: Command Does Not Transfer Liability
Explaining the baseline rule and the agency exception

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general Hanafi rule that a command does not shift liability from the commanded person to the commander, and identify the exception when the commanded act benefits the commander (agency).

Core Idea
Command alone never transfers liability; the doer is liable unless the commander coerces or deceives.

This passage opens with a foundational rule of liability: when someone commands another to act and harm results, the commanded person bears the loss. The rationale is straightforward—he is the direct agent, the mubashir. But fiqh is never monolithic; exceptions refine the rule. In this lesson, we explore the baseline and its first major qualification: if the commanded act benefits the commander, the relationship becomes agency, and the commander must indemnify the commanded person.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule
Locate the opening statement: the commanded person is the guarantor because he is the doer, not the commander. This is the prima facie position.
The rule is expressed in the negative: command does not add the act to the commander.
2
Understand the first application (contracts)
When a person is commanded to make a contract concerning himself, he alone is bound—even if the contract is disadvantageous to him. The commander does not become a party.
Contrast this with agency where the commander is bound by the agent's actions.
3
Recognize the coercion exception (contracts)
If coercion (ikrah) is present, the commanded person has the option (khiyar) to ratify or rescind the contract after the coercion ends. This is a nuance in contract law.
Coercion here is actual duress, not constructive.
4
Analyze the agency exception
When the commanded act is for the commander's benefit—like paying his debt, spending on him, or building his house—the commanded person is treated as an agent. He can recover what he paid or spent from the commander.
This is a major qualification: 'otherwise the command is in the nature of agency.'
5
Synthesize the provisional maxim
The passage suggests the rule should be: 'The doer is liable as long as the commander is not coercive or deceptive.' The agency exception operates parallel to this.
The full maxim emerges after considering all exceptions.

Source Text

- بخلاف ما لو أكرهه الآمر على العقد، فإنّ له الخيار في الإمضاء أو الفسخ بعد زوال الإكراه.

وذلك كما لو أمره بقضاء دين على الآمر، أو بالإنفاق عليه أو بناء داره مثلاً، فإنَّ المأمور عندئذ يكون كوكيل يرجع على الأمر بما دفع أو أنفق .
-
والإكراه الذي يضيف الفعل إلى الآمر قد يكون حكمياً تقديرياً كما لو كان الآمر سلطاناً فإن أمره إكراه ( ر ) قواعد الحمزاوي مسائل الغصب ص / ١٩٦).
- ومثل ذلك ما لو كان الأمر بالغاً عاقلاً والمأمور قاصراً صبياً أو مجنوناً، فإنَّ المأمور يضمن أولاً نتائج ما يفعل أو يتلف، ولكنه يرجع على الأمر، لأنَّ أمر الكبير العاقل للقاصر في حكم الإكراه.
الفصل (۸۱) شرح قواعد المجلة وتصنيفها
بخلاف ما لو كان الأمر صبياً مثله فإنَّ أمره غير معتبر، والضمان يستقر على المأمور.
- ومثل الإكراه في الحكم التغرير كما لو قال له : احفر لي في هذا الجدار، أو في جداري هذا بياء) المتكلم، وكان الجدار لغيره والمأمور لا يعلم، فإنه يضمن ويرجع على الأمر.
بخلاف مالو قال له حفر في هذا الجدار» فإنه لا تغرير فيه، والضمان على المأمور إلا أن يكون الأمر ساكناً في الدار، فيكون عندئذ تغريراً أيضاً، لأن سكناه قرينة توهم المأمور أنَّ الدار للآمر .
وعلى هذا كان ينبغي أن يقال في القاعدة: «ما لم يكن الأمر مجبراً أو مغوراً».
وفي كل موطن يضاف فيه الفعل إلى الآمر فإنَّ الخصومة القضائية في الضمان تكون مع المأمور أي تقام الدعوى من المتضرر على المأمور لا على الآمر فيضمن المأمور أولاً لأنه مباشر ، ثم يرجع على الآمر . (من شرح والدي رحمه الله نقلاً عن رسالة التحرير في ضمان الآمر والمأمور والأجير للعلامة الشيخ محمود الحمزاوي) (۱)
Chapter Ten: The Universal Legal Maxims of Fiqh. In general, if an animal falls or a crime is committed and the commanded person does that, the commanded person is the responsible guarantor, because he is the doer, not the commander. - And if he orders another with a contract pertaining to the commanded person himself, the commanded contracting party is the one bound by his contract, even if the contract is against his interest. - Contrary to when the commander coerces him into the contract, then he has the option to ratify or rescind after the coercion ends. Whenever the act is attributed to the commander because he is a coercer (in the active form), responsibility for the coerced person (in the active form, detailed in the discussion of coercion from fiqh books, according to whether the coercion is overwhelming or not, and whether it is coercion over a verbal action or an act) is established against him. A summary has preceded in the discussion of coercion among defects of consent in the theory of contracts, and in the commentary of the rules (see: f 4/34 and 81, 32). And the furu' of fiqh indicate that the rule is restricted to when the commanded act is not for the benefit of the commander; otherwise the command is in the nature of agency, where the commanded person takes the place of the commander within the limits of the command, and his action is binding on him. This is like if he orders him to pay a debt on the commander's behalf, or to spend on him, or to build his house, for example. Then the commanded person is like an agent who may claim from the commander what he paid or spent. - And coercion that attributes the act to the commander may be constructive and presumptive, as when the commander is a sultan, for his command is coercion (see Hamzawi's rules, questions of usurpation, p. 196). - And similar is when the commander is an adult of sound mind and the commanded is a minor, child, or insane. The commanded person first guarantees the results of what he does or destroys, but then he may recover from the commander, because the command of a mature, rational person to a minor is in the nature of coercion. Chapter 81: Commentary on the Rules of the Majalla and Their Classification. Contrary to when the commander is also a minor, for his command is not considered, and the liability remains on the commanded. - And similar to coercion in ruling is deception, as when he says to him: 'Dig for me in this wall' or 'in this wall of mine' (with the ya' of the speaker), and the wall belonged to another and the commanded person does not know. Then he guarantees and recovers from the commander. Contrary to when he says 'Dig in this wall' – then there is no deception, and the liability is on the commanded, unless the commander is a resident in the house, for then it is also deception, because his residence is an indication that misleads the commanded person into thinking the house belongs to the commander. Based on this, it should be said regarding the rule: 'As long as the commander is not coercive or deceptive.' And in every instance where the act is attributed to the commander, judicial litigation regarding liability is with the commanded person. That is, the lawsuit is filed by the injured party against the commanded person, not against the commander. So the commanded person guarantees first because he is direct, then he recovers from the commander. (From my father's commentary, may Allah have mercy on him, quoting from the treatise 'Al-Tahrir fi Daman al-Amir wa al-Ma'mur wa al-Ajir' by the scholar Sheikh Mahmud al-Hamzawi) (1).
Source pages p. 979

Commentary
This passage is a detailed commentary on the rule that a command does not transfer liability from the commanded person to the commander. It begins by stating the general principle: if someone commands another to do something and damage results, the commanded person is liable because he is the direct agent. Several furu' (applications) follow. First, if a person is commanded to contract for himself, he is bound. But if coerced, he has a right of rescission. Second, the rule is qualified when the act benefits the commander—then it becomes agency, and the commanded person can seek reimbursement. Third, constructive coercion occurs when the commander is a sultan (ruler) or an adult commanding a minor. Fourth, a minor's command is void, so liability stays on the doer. Fifth, deception (taghreer) also attributes the act to the commander, as when someone says 'dig in my wall' but the wall belongs to another; if the commander lives in the house, even saying 'this wall' implies deception. The passage concludes by suggesting that the maxim should be phrased as 'the doer is liable unless the commander coerces or deceives.' Procedurally, the victim sues the direct doer, who then seeks recourse against the commander. This entire discussion shows how Hanafi jurists refine general maxims by identifying exceptions and conditions.

Key Terms
إكراه (ikrah): Coercion that attributes the act to the commander, whether actual (mulji' or ghayr mulji') or constructive (hukmi).
مباشر (mubashir): The direct doer of an action that causes harm, contrasted with the mutasabbib (indirect cause).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an exception to the rule that the commanded person is liable?
A) The commanded act is unlawful.
B) The commanded act benefits the commander.
C) The commanded person is an adult.
D) The commanded act is performed negligently.

Key Takeaway
Command alone keeps liability on the doer; only when the act benefits the commander does liability shift to the commander via agency.

Coercion and Deception as Exceptions to Liability
Explaining coercion and deception as exceptions that shift liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the types of coercion (actual, constructive, by sultan, by adult to minor) and deception that transfer liability from the commanded person to the commander, and apply the procedural rule that the victim sues the direct doer first.

Core Idea
Coercion and deception shift liability: the commander becomes the responsible party.

The default rule—command does not transfer liability—gives way in two critical situations: coercion (ikrah) and deception (taghreer). This lesson unpacks the nuances of these exceptions. We learn that a sultan's command is always coercive, an adult's command to a minor is tantamount to coercion, and even a homeowner's presence can create deception. Crucially, the victim always sues the commanded person first, who then recovers from the commander.

Guided Path
1
Define constructive coercion
Coercion need not be physical. The passage gives two examples: a sultan's command (presumed coercive) and an adult's command to a minor (presumed coercive due to authority imbalance).
This is called ikrah hukmi (constructive coercion).
2
Contrast with non-coercive commands
If the commander is a minor, his command is invalid, and the commanded person (if adult) remains liable. If the commander is adult but the commanded person is adult, no constructive coercion unless actual duress exists.
Liability remains on the doer.
3
Understand deception (taghreer)
Deception occurs when the commander leads the commanded person to believe he has authority over the property. Example: saying 'dig in my wall' when the wall belongs to another. Even saying 'dig in this wall' while residing in the house implies deception.
Deception is analogous to coercion in its effect on liability.
4
Formulate the refined maxim
Based on the exceptions, the passage suggests the maxim: 'The doer is liable unless the commander is coercive or deceptive.' This is a dabt (restrictive precision).
The maxim synthesizes all the furuat.
5
Apply the procedural rule
In all cases where liability is attributed to the commander, the victim must still sue the commanded person (mubashir) first. The commanded person pays and then seeks recourse from the commander.
This is a practical litigation principle.
