The Direct Doer is Liable Even if Unintentional
Stating a Hanafi legal maxim on direct liability

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mubashir damin wa in lam yata'ammad' to cases of direct causation and distinguish it from the rule on indirect causation.

Core Idea
Direct causation creates strict liability regardless of intent.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi legal maxim: the direct doer (mubashir) is liable for damage even if he did not intend it. The maxim establishes strict liability for direct acts, contrasting with indirect causation where transgression may be required. We will define the key term, examine the ruling, and see its practical application through examples.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Read the Arabic text and its translation. Note that the maxim applies to all types of direct damage (itlaf).
The maxim is general; exceptions exist for coercion or command.
2
Define 'mubashir' precisely
Identify the definition given in the text: 'the one who produces the effect by his action' – the direct actor in a harmful act against another.
Contrast with 'mutasabbib' (indirect cause) which appears in related maxims.
3
Apply the maxim to a concrete case
Work through the example: a person slips and falls onto another's property, causing damage. According to the maxim, is he liable?
The answer is yes, even though the fall was accidental – intent is irrelevant.
4
Identify exceptions and related maxims
Consider when the maxim does not apply, e.g., when the actor is compelled (mukrah) or acting under coercion. Relate to the maxim 'ما لم يكن الأمر مجبراً أو مغوراً'.
These exceptions are not in this passage but are essential for complete understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۸۹/۸۱ - القاعدة التاسعة والعشرون : المباشر ضامن وإن لم يتعمَّد (م/ ٩٢).
المباشر هو الذي يحصل الأثر بفعله والمراد به في القاعدة من يباشر عملاً مُضراً بغيره .
فمن باشر إتلافاً بأي طريقة كانت فهو ضامن سواء أكان عامداً أو مخطئاً .
فلو زلقت رجل إنسان فسقط على شيء لغيره فأتلفه أو أضره، أو
89/81 - The twenty-ninth maxim: The direct doer is liable even if he did not intend (M/92). The direct doer is the one who produces the effect by his action, and what is meant in this maxim is the one who directly performs a harmful act against another. So whoever directly causes damage in any way is liable, whether intentional or mistaken. So if a person's foot slips and he falls on something belonging to another and destroys or harms it, or
Source pages p. 980

Commentary
This passage presents a fundamental Hanafi legal maxim: 'Al-mubashir damin wa in lam yata'ammad' (The direct doer is liable even if unintentional). It defines 'mubashir' as one whose action directly produces a harmful effect on another. The ruling is unconditional: the direct perpetrator of damage (itlaf) is liable whether he acted deliberately (amadan) or mistakenly (mukhti'an). The example given is the classic case of a person's foot slipping and falling onto another's property, causing damage—the falling person is liable even though there was no intent. This maxim is the foundation of the general principle that direct causation creates strict liability, and it is qualified only by exceptions such as the rule on command and coercion seen earlier. In Hanafi fiqh, 'mubashir' is contrasted with 'mutasabbib' (indirect cause), who may have lesser or secondary liability.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubashir): One whose action directly causes a harmful effect, as defined in the text: 'the one who produces the effect by his action and directly performs a harmful act against another.'

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim, what is the liability of a person who accidentally falls onto another's property and breaks it?
A) No liability, because there was no intent.
B) No liability, because it was an accident.
C) He is liable as the direct cause of the damage.
D) He is liable only if he was negligent.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-mubashir damin wa in lam yata'ammad' establishes strict liability for direct causation, making intent irrelevant for liability.

The Direct Doer is Liable Even if Not Intentional
شرح قاعدة المباشر ضامن مع أمثلة واستثناء

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-mubashir damin' to cases of direct causation and identify the exception when the commander is a compeller or deceiver.

Core Idea
The direct doer is strictly liable for harm, even if unintentional, unless acting under compulsion or deceit.

This lesson introduces a foundational Hanafi tort maxim: the direct doer (mubashir) is liable regardless of intent. We will examine its application through concrete examples, such as slipping or a blacksmith's spark, and then explore the critical exception when the act is ordered under coercion or deception, shifting liability to the commander.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim
Recite the rule: 'al-mubashir damin wa in lam yata'ammad' (the direct doer is liable even if not intentional).
This is Article 92 of the Majalla.
2
Define direct doer
A mubashir is someone who directly causes harm by his own action, whether intentional or mistaken.
The text says 'man yubashiru 'amalan mudirran bi-ghayrihi'.
3
Apply to examples
Work through the given examples: slipping and breaking a vessel, a spark from a blacksmith burning clothing, a child overturning and breaking an item—all are liable.
These are direct acts of causation.
4
Identify the dabt (restriction)
Note the exception: 'ma lam yakun al-amru mujbaran aw mughawwiran' (unless the commander was a compeller or deceiver). The rule is not absolute.
The exception shifts liability to the commanding party.
5
Analyze the deceit scenario
Understand taghrir (deceit) as equivalent to coercion in ruling. If a commander deceives the doer, the doer still pays but recovers from the commander.
Example: commanding someone to dig in a wall that actually belongs to a third party.

وعلى هذا كان ينبغي أن يقال في القاعدة: «ما لم يكن الأمر مجبراً أو مغوراً».
وفي كل موطن يضاف فيه الفعل إلى الآمر فإنَّ الخصومة القضائية في الضمان تكون مع المأمور أي تقام الدعوى من المتضرر على المأمور لا على الآمر فيضمن المأمور أولاً لأنه مباشر ، ثم يرجع على الآمر . (من شرح والدي رحمه الله نقلاً عن رسالة التحرير في ضمان الآمر والمأمور والأجير للعلامة الشيخ محمود الحمزاوي) (۱)
·
۸۹/۸۱ - القاعدة التاسعة والعشرون : المباشر ضامن وإن لم يتعمَّد
(م/ ٩٢).
المباشر هو الذي يحصل الأثر بفعله والمراد به في القاعدة من يباشر
عملاً مُضراً بغيره .
مخطئاً :
·
فمن باشر إتلافاً بأي طريقة كانت فهو ضامن سواء أكان عامداً أو
فلو زلقت رجل إنسان فسقط على شيء لغيره فأتلفه أو أضره، أو
طارت شرارة من دكان حداد فأحرقت ثوب شخص، أو انقلب طفل على آنية شخص فكسرها، كان كل أولئك ضامنين ما أتلفوا أو أفسدوا (ر: ف ١٣/٥٩ و ٥/٦١).
۹۰/۱ - القاعدة المتممة للثلاثين : المتسبب لا يضمن إلا بالتعمد
(م/ ۹۳).
المتسبب في حادثة هو الذي يفعل ما يؤدي إليها، ولا يباشرها
مباشرة والنظر الشرعي في التسبب لضرر الغير أنه :
أ - إذا انفرد التسبب في الميدان كان من موجبات الضمان بشرط أن يكون المتسبب متعدياً.
-
مثال ذلك ما لو حفر شخص حفرة في الطريق العام بدون إذن ولي الأمر، أو بإذنه ولكنه أهمل بعض القيود، كما لو أذن له بالحفر على أن يضع حواجز حول الحفرة فلم يضعها فوقع فيها حيوان أو شخص أعمى. فإنَّ المتسبب، وهو الحافر، يضمن ضرر الأنفس والأموال.
وكذا لو قطع شخص حبل قنديل معلق فسقط، أو شق زقّ سمن فسال، أو فتح باب قفص أو اصطبل حتى فرّ الطائر أو الدابة، فإنه يضمن في كل ذلك .
ب - فإذا لم يكن المتسب معتدياً كمن حفر حفرة في أرضه فدخل حيوان لجاره وسقط فيها، أو حفر حفرة في الطريق العام بإذن ولي الأمر وحوط حولها، فسقط فيها شخص أو حيوان، فلا ضمان عليه ر: شروح المجلة للعلامة علي حيدر وغيره م/۹۲ - ۹۳) .
يستخلص مما تقدم في هذه القاعدة والتي قبلها أن التعدي هو الأساس الملحوظ للتضمين في حالتي المباشرة والتسبب على السواء، وأن التعدي يكون :
١٠٤٦
Contrary to the case where the commander was a minor, for example, his command is not considered, and the liability falls on the commanded. Similarly, deceit is like coercion in ruling, as when he said to him: 'Dig for me in this wall, or in my this wall' with the pronoun 'my', and the wall belonged to another and the commanded did not know, then he is liable and he recovers from the commander. Contrary to if he said to him: 'Dig in this wall', then there is no deceit in it, and the liability is on the commanded unless the commander is a resident in the house, then at that time it is also deceit, because his residence is an indication that misleads the commanded that the house belongs to the commander. On this basis, it should be said in the rule: 'As long as the commander is not a compeller or a deceiver.' And in every situation where the action is attributed to the commander, the judicial litigation regarding liability is with the commanded, i.e., the lawsuit is brought by the injured party against the commanded, not the commander; so the commanded is liable first because he is the direct doer, then he recovers from the commander. (From the commentary of my father, may Allah have mercy on him, quoting from the treatise 'Al-Tahrir fi Daman al-Amir wa al-Ma'mur wa al-Ajir' by the scholar Sheikh Mahmud al-Hamzawi). 89/81 - The twenty-ninth rule: The direct doer is liable even if not intentional (Article 92). The direct doer is the one by whose action the effect occurs, and what is meant in the rule is whoever directly performs a harmful act to another. So whoever directly causes destruction in any way is liable, whether intentional or mistaken. If a person's foot slips and falls on something belonging to another and damages or harms it, or a spark flies from a blacksmith's shop and burns a person's garment, or a child overturns onto another's vessel and breaks it, all of these are liable for what they destroyed or corrupted (ref: Fatawa 13/59 and 5/61). 90/1 - The complementary rule to the thirtieth: The indirect causer is not liable except with intent (Article 93). The indirect causer in an incident is the one who does what leads to it, without directly doing it. The legal view on causing harm to others is: A) If indirect causation is the sole factor in the situation, it is a cause of liability provided that the causer is transgressing. Example: If a person digs a pit in the public road without permission of the authority, or with permission but neglected some conditions, such as if he was permitted to dig on condition that he place barriers around the pit and he did not place them, then an animal or a blind person falls into it, the indirect causer, namely the digger, is liable for harm to persons and property. Similarly, if someone cuts the rope of a hanging lantern and it falls, or slits a skin of ghee and it pours, or opens the door of a cage or stable so that the bird or animal escapes, then he is liable in all that. B) But if the indirect causer is not transgressing, such as someone who digs a pit in his own land and a neighbor's animal enters and falls into it, or digs a pit in the public road with permission of the authority and surrounds it, then a person or animal falls into it, there is no liability on him (ref: Commentaries on the Majalla by Allama Ali Haidar and others, Articles 92-93). It is deduced from what has preceded in this rule and the one before it that transgression is the observed basis for liability in both direct and indirect causation alike, and that transgression is: 1046.
Source pages p. 980

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-Amm by Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa presents two fundamental maxims from the Majalla al-Ahkam al-Adliyya. The first, 'al-mubashir damin wa in lam yata'ammad' (the direct doer is liable even if not intentional), establishes strict liability for direct causation. The author explains that the direct doer is liable regardless of intent or negligence, with examples like slipping, sparks causing fire, or a child breaking a vessel. However, this rule has an important exception: when the act is done under compulsion (ikrah) or deceit (taghrir) by a commander, the liability shifts to the commander, and the direct doer may recover from him. The second maxim, 'al-mutasabbib la yadman illa bil-ta'ammud' (the indirect causer is not liable unless with intent), limits liability for indirect causation to cases where the actor was transgressing (muta'addi). The text provides examples: digging a pit in the road without proper barriers, cutting a lantern rope, etc., where transgression exists. Conversely, if there is no transgression, like digging in one's own land, no liability arises. The author concludes that transgression is the common element for liability in both direct and indirect causation, though the burden of proof differs. This passage is a classic Hanafi treatment of tort liability, emphasizing the role of intention and transgression.

Key Terms
المباشر (al-mubāshir): من يباشر العمل المضر بنفسه
الضمان (al-ḍamān): التعويض المالي عن الضرر
التغرير (al-taghrīr): خداع المأمور بإيهامه أن الفعل مباح
الإكراه (al-ikrāh): إجبار المأمور على الفعل بتهديد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim 'al-mubashir damin wa in lam yata'ammad', who is liable when a blacksmith's spark accidentally burns someone's clothing?
A) The blacksmith is liable as the direct doer.
B) No one is liable because it was accidental.
C) The person whose clothing was burned is responsible.
D) The municipality where the shop is located is liable.

Key Takeaway
The direct doer of harm is strictly liable regardless of intent, unless the act was done under compulsion or deceit by a commander.

The Indirect Cause: Liability Only with Transgression
Rule explanation with examples and exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rule 'المتسبب لا يضمن إلا بالتعمد' to new cases by identifying whether the indirect cause was transgressive, and state the liability accordingly.

Core Idea
An indirect cause is only liable if he transgressed — intent means transgression in this rule.

This lesson unpacks a key principle from the Majalla: an indirect cause (mutasabbib) is not liable for harm unless there was intent. But the text equates 'intent' with transgression (ta'addi) — acting beyond what is legally permitted. Through examples, you'll learn to distinguish when the indirect cause is responsible and when he is not, a crucial skill for muftis handling tort cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the indirect cause
Determine who performed the action that led indirectly to harm (e.g., digging a hole, cutting a rope, opening a cage).
The indirect cause does not directly inflict harm but sets the stage.
2
Check for transgression (ta'addi)
Ask: Did the person act beyond what is legally permitted? Transgression includes acting without permission or with permission but violating conditions.
Digging in public road without permission = transgressive; digging with permission and proper barriers = not transgressive.
3
Apply the rule
If transgressive, the indirect cause is liable for all resulting harm. If not transgressive, no liability.
Liability applies if the indirect cause is the sole effective factor and transgressive.
4
Consider exceptions
Acts on one's own property without transgression or authorized acts with due care are exceptions — no liability.
The complementary rule 'الضرر يزال' governs these exceptions.
5
Conclude liability
Combine the above steps to decide whether the indirect cause must compensate for harm.
The ruling is clear: liability hinges on transgression, not subjective intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القاعدة المتممة للثلاثين : المتسبب لا يضمن إلا بالتعمد (م/ ۹۳).
المتسبب في حادثة هو الذي يفعل ما يؤدي إليها، ولا يباشرها مباشرة والنظر الشرعي في التسبب لضرر الغير أنه :
أ - إذا انفرد التسبب في الميدان كان من موجبات الضمان بشرط أن يكون المتسبب متعدياً.
-
مثال ذلك ما لو حفر شخص حفرة في الطريق العام بدون إذن ولي الأمر، أو بإذنه ولكنه أهمل بعض القيود، كما لو أذن له بالحفر على أن يضع حواجز حول الحفرة فلم يضعها فوقع فيها حيوان أو شخص أعمى. فإنَّ المتسبب، وهو الحافر، يضمن ضرر الأنفس والأموال.
وكذا لو قطع شخص حبل قنديل معلق فسقط، أو شق زقّ سمن فسال، أو فتح باب قفص أو اصطبل حتى فرّ الطائر أو الدابة، فإنه يضمن في كل ذلك .
ب - فإذا لم يكن المتسبب معتدياً كمن حفر حفرة في أرضه فدخل حيوان لجاره وسقط فيها، أو حفر حفرة في الطريق العام بإذن ولي الأمر وحوط حولها، فسقط فيها شخص أو حيوان، فلا ضمان عليه (ر: شروح المجلة للعلامة علي حيدر وغيره م/۹۲ - ۹۳) .
The completing thirtieth rule: The indirect cause is not liable except with intent (Majalla article 93). The indirect cause in an incident is one who does what leads to it without directly committing it. The legal view on causing harm to others is: (a) If the causation stands alone in the field, it is a basis for liability provided the indirect cause is transgressive. Example: someone digs a hole in the public road without permission from the authorities, or with permission but neglects some conditions, such as being permitted to dig on condition that he places barriers around the hole but fails to do so, and an animal or a blind person falls into it. Then the indirect cause, the digger, is liable for harm to persons and property. Similarly, if someone cuts the rope of a hanging lamp so it falls, or slits a skin of samn so it spills, or opens the door of a cage or stable so the bird or animal escapes, he is liable in all that. (b) If the indirect cause is not transgressive, like someone digging a hole in his own land and a neighbor's animal enters and falls into it, or digs a hole in the public road with permission from the authorities and fences it properly, and a person or animal falls into it, then there is no liability upon him (see commentaries on the Majalla by Ali Haydar and others, articles 92-93).
Source pages p. 981

Commentary
This passage presents the rule that an indirect cause (mutasabbib) is not liable for harm except when there is intent. However, the author immediately clarifies that the key condition is transgression (ta'addi), not literal intent. The rule is from the Majalla (article 93) and is an important principle in Hanafi tort law. The text then divides the treatment into two scenarios: (a) when the indirect cause is the sole factor and is transgressive, liability applies; (b) when the indirect cause is not transgressive, there is no liability. Several examples illustrate the rule: digging a hole unlawfully in the public road, cutting a lamp rope, etc. The examples show that the indirect cause is held responsible even if he did not directly inflict harm. The distinction between transgressive and non-transgressive behavior is crucial: if the indirect cause acts within his rights and with proper precautions, he is not liable. This reflects the Hanafi emphasis on legal authorization and proper conduct. The term 'intent' (ta'ammud) in the rule is understood here as synonymous with transgression (ta'addi), not subjective intent to cause harm.

Key Terms
متسبب (mutasabbib): One who does an act that leads to harm indirectly, without direct commission of the harmful event
تعمد (ta'ammud): In this rule, it means transgression (ta'addi) rather than subjective intent
تعدي (ta'addi): Acting beyond what is legally permitted, either by exceeding authorized limits or violating others' rights

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule 'المتسبب لا يضمن إلا بالتعمد', when is an indirect cause liable for harm?
A) Only if he intended the harm
B) Only if he was transgressive (muta'addi)
C) Whenever his action leads to harm
D) Only if he acted without permission

Key Takeaway
The indirect cause is liable only when transgressive; acting within rights or with proper authorization removes liability.

Refinement: Transgression as the True Basis of Liability
Critical refinement of the rule on indirect causation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi rule 'al-mutassabbib la yadman illa bi al-ta'ammud' actually refers to transgression (ta'addi) and apply this refinement to distinguish between direct and indirect causation liability.

Core Idea
In Hanafi liability, transgression (ta'addi) not intent (ta'ammud) is the operative criterion for indirect causation.

In this passage, Mustafa al-Zarqa performs a critical refinement of a well-known Hanafi rule. He argues that the term 'ta'ammud' in the rule 'al-mutassabbib la yadman illa bi al-ta'ammud' has been misunderstood. Instead of subjective intent, it should be interpreted as 'ta'addi' (transgression). This shift moves the focus from the actor's mental state to the objective legal wrong. Understanding this refinement is crucial for correctly applying liability in cases of indirect causation, such as accidents caused by negligence or unauthorized acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the rule being refined
State the rule: 'al-mutassabbib la yadman illa bi al-ta'ammud' (the indirect cause is not liable unless with ta'ammud). Note that the conventional understanding equates ta'ammud with intent.
This rule is from the Majalla.
2
Distinguish direct and indirect causation
Direct causation (mubashara) involves immediate harm; indirect causation (tasabbub) involves harm through an intermediary. Liability in both requires transgression (ta'addi).
Refer to the two types described in the passage.
3
Recognize the misleading usage of 'ta'ammud'
The author argues that 'ta'ammud' in this rule is inapt because it suggests subjective intent, whereas the actual criterion is objective transgression. Note that transgression includes exceeding permitted limits.
The author claims no commentator before him noted this.
4
Apply the refined concept to the mad person example
The example of a mad person shouting and causing harm: he is liable despite lacking intention because he committed transgression (exceeding permitted conduct). This shows liability without intent.
This illustration confirms that transgression, not intent, is decisive.
5
Understand the implication for fatwa
When issuing fatwa, the mufti should focus on whether the indirect cause exceeded legal boundaries, not on whether he intended harm. This aligns with the Hanafi emphasis on objective wrong.
This refinement affects many modern scenarios, e.g., traffic accidents.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يستخلص مما تقدم في هذه القاعدة والتي قبلها أن التعدي هو الأساس الملحوظ للتضمين في حالتي المباشرة والتسبب على السواء، وأن التعدي يكون :
١ - إما بتجاوز الفاعل على الشخص المضرور أو على حقوقه رأساً كما في حالة المباشرة.
٢ - وإما بتجاوزه الحدود المأذون بها شرعاً حتى تفضي إلى ضرر الغير، كما في حالة التسبب.
ومتى وجد التعدي لا ينظر بعد ذلك إلى التعمد والقصد، لأن حقوق الغير مضمونة شرعاً في حالتي العمد والخطأ، بل حتى في حالة الاضطرار المبيح للمحظورات كما تقدم (ر: ف ٣٥/٨١).
فالفروع الفقهية تفيد أن كلاً من المباشرة والتسبب لضرر الغير موجب للضمان متى وجد التعدي بمعناه المشروح سواء قصد الفاعل الفعل أو الضرر قصداً معتبراً أم لم يقصد.
ولذا لو صاح المجنون بدابة أحد حتى جفلت وأضرت بمال أو نفس كان ضامناً في ماله وإن لم يستحق عقوبة، مع أنه في ذلك متسبب وليس مباشراً، ولا يعتبر له قصد إلى فعل أو ضرر.
يتضح من ذلك أن التعبير بلفظ «التعمد» الوارد في قاعدة «المتسبب» هذه إنما المراد به معنى التعدي لا معنى القصد وهو تعبير غير سديد لاسيما في قاعدة لأنه موهم ولم أر من نبه على ذلك من الشراح.
It is deduced from what has preceded in this rule and the one before it that transgression is the observed basis for liability in both direct and indirect causation alike, and that transgression is either: (1) by the perpetrator's overriding the injured person or his rights directly, as in direct causation, or (2) by his exceeding the limits permitted by law, resulting in harm to another, as in indirect causation. Whenever transgression is found, one does not then consider intent and purpose, because the rights of others are guaranteed legally in both cases of intentional and unintentional harm, even in cases of necessity that permit prohibited things, as previously mentioned (cf. vol. 81, p. 35). So the positive law branches indicate that both direct and indirect causation of harm to another are grounds for liability whenever transgression exists in its explained sense, whether the actor intended the act or the harm with a valid intention or not. For this reason, if a mad person shouts at someone's animal causing it to startle and cause harm to property or person, he is liable from his wealth even if he is not subject to punishment, since in that he is an indirect cause and not direct, and he is not considered to have intention for the act or harm. It becomes clear from this that the expression 'intent' (ta'ammud) in this rule of the indirect cause refers to the meaning of transgression (ta'addi) and not the meaning of purpose, and it is an inapt expression, especially in a rule, because it is misleading, and I have not seen any commentator draw attention to this.
Source pages p. 981

Commentary
This passage is a critical refinement of the previous rule. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the real basis for liability in both direct and indirect causation is transgression (ta'addi), not intent (ta'ammud). Transgression occurs either by directly violating a person's rights (in direct causation) or by exceeding the boundaries of what is permitted by law (in indirect causation). Once transgression is established, intent or lack thereof is irrelevant; even unintentional harm and harm caused under necessity can lead to liability if transgression is present. The example of a mad person shouting and causing an animal to harm illustrates that liability can arise without any valid intent. The author then critiques the wording of the rule 'المتسبب لا يضمن إلا بالتعمد', noting that the term 'تعمد' is misleading because it implies subjective intent, whereas the actual criterion is transgression. He claims this is a point not previously noted by commentators. This refinement is important for understanding Hanafi tort law: the focus is on objective legal wrong rather than subjective mental state.

Key Terms
تعدي (ta'addi): Exceeding legal boundaries, whether by directly violating rights or by overstepping authorized limits
تعمد (ta'ammud): In the text, used misleadingly to mean transgression, not subjective intent
مباشرة (mubashara): Direct causation of harm, where the actor directly violates the victim's rights
تسبب (tasabbub): Indirect causation, where harm results from exceeding permitted limits
ضمان (daman): Legal liability to compensate for harm

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the term 'تعمد' in the rule 'المتسبب لا يضمن إلا بالتعمد' considered inapt?
A) Because intent is irrelevant in indirect causation
B) Because it refers to transgression, not subjective intent
C) Because the rule should be about direct causation
D) Because mad persons are not liable

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi liability for indirect causation, 'ta'ammud' must be understood as transgression (ta'addi), not subjective intent, so the key question is whether the actor exceeded legal boundaries, not whether he intended harm.

6 min
When Direct and Indirect Cause Meet: Attribution to the Direct Cause
Stating the rule for joint causation and its definition

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi legal maxim 'إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب يضاف الحكم إلى المباشر' to identify liability in joint causation scenarios and state the exception for non-responsible intervening agents.

Core Idea
When a direct and indirect cause meet, liability falls on the direct cause—unless the direct cause lacks legal responsibility.

This lesson examines the thirty-first rule from the Majalla: when both a direct and an indirect cause contribute to an incident, the ruling is attributed to the direct cause. The passage defines the 'meeting' of causes as the intervention of a voluntary act of another person between the indirect cause's action and the harm. This rule is central to Hanafi tort liability, clarifying that the indirect cause is exonerated when a responsible agent directly brings about the harm.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim verbatim
Read the Arabic maxim: 'إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب يضاف الحكم إلى المباشر'. Understand that it governs joint causation.
This is rule 91/11 from the Majalla (article 90).
2
Define 'direct cause' (mubashir) and 'indirect cause' (mutasabbib)
In Hanafi terminology, a direct cause is one who personally performs the harmful act without any intermediary. An indirect cause is one who sets the stage for harm but does not directly inflict it.
The distinction is crucial for attributing liability.
3
Explain the condition for the rule to apply
The rule applies only when a voluntary, deliberate act of a third party intervenes between the indirect cause's action and the harm. That third party becomes the direct cause.
If the intervening act is involuntary or by a non-responsible person, the rule may not apply.
4
Identify the consequence: liability shifts to the direct cause
Because the direct cause's act is 'more closely connected' (ألصق), the harmful outcome is attributed to that person, and the indirect cause is exonerated.
This is the default ruling under this maxim.
5
Note the exception: non-responsible direct cause
If the intervening person is not legally responsible (e.g., insane, coerced, minor), the indirect cause may still be liable because the intervening act does not break the causal chain.
The exception is governed by the maxim 'رفع القلم عن المجنون' (the pen is lifted from the insane).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ج - وأما إذا لم ينفرد التسبب بل اجتمع في الحادثة متسبب ومباشر فهذا موضوع القاعدة التالية :
۹۱/۱۱ - القاعدة الحادية والثلاثون: إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب يضاف الحكم إلى المباشر (م ۹۰ ) .
واجتماع المتسبب والمباشر في حادثة يكون بأن يتخلل بين عمل المتسبب وحدوث الحادثة فعل شخص آخر مختار .
فهذا الشخص عندئذ يكون مباشراً، فيضاف الفعل إليه لأنه ألصق به
(c) As for when the causation is not solitary but both an indirect cause and a direct cause come together in the incident, this is the subject of the following rule: Article 91/11 - The thirty-first rule: When the direct and indirect cause meet, the ruling is attributed to the direct cause (Majalla article 90). The meeting of indirect and direct cause in an incident occurs when there intervenes between the action of the indirect cause and the occurrence of the incident a voluntary action of another person. That person then becomes the direct cause, so the act is attributed to him because it is more closely connected to him.
Source pages p. 982

Commentary
This passage presents the rule for cases where both a direct cause (mubashir) and an indirect cause (mutasabbib) are present in an incident. According to this rule, liability is attributed to the direct cause, not the indirect one. The explanation defines their meeting: there must be an intervening voluntary act of a third person between the indirect cause's action and the harmful event. That intervening person becomes the direct cause, and the harmful outcome is attributed to him because his act is more directly linked to the harm. This rule is from the Majalla (article 90). It means that if an indirect cause sets the stage, but a free-willed person directly brings about the harm, the indirect cause is exonerated. This principle is applied in Hanafi tort law, for example, if someone leaves a dangerous object on the road and another person deliberately pushes it causing injury, the latter is liable. The rule emphasizes that the direct intervention of a responsible agent breaks the causal chain.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubashir): One who directly commits the harmful act without intermediary
اجتماع المباشر والمتسبب (ijtima' al-mubashir wa al-mutasabbib): The concurrence of a direct and indirect cause in the same incident, with the direct cause's act intervening

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the rule 'إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب يضاف الحكم إلى المباشر', when does an indirect cause escape liability?
A) When he did not intend harm
B) When a voluntary direct cause intervenes
C) When the indirect cause is a mad person
D) When the harm is unintentional

Key Takeaway
The direct cause bears liability if he voluntarily intervenes; the indirect cause is exonerated, unless the direct cause lacks legal responsibility.

Rule 31: Direct vs. Indirect Causation in Liability
Explaining the 31st rule of the Majalla with furuat

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the 31st rule of the Majalla to differentiate between direct and indirect causation in liability cases, and identify the istithna' involving a trustee.

Core Idea
Liability attaches to the direct cause, not the indirect, unless the indirect cause is a trustee betraying a duty.

This lesson unpacks a foundational maxim of Islamic tort law: when both a direct and an indirect cause contribute to harm, the direct cause bears liability. The rule is simple, but its application requires careful parsing of causation chains. Through concrete examples, you'll learn how to identify the 'direct' actor and when an exception applies due to a special duty of care.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Write out the maxim in Arabic and English: 'When the direct cause and the indirect cause come together, the ruling is attributed to the direct cause.'
This is Article 90 of the Majalla.
2
Define 'Direct' and 'Indirect' Cause
A direct cause (mubāshir) acts without an intervening free agent; an indirect cause (mutasabbib) sets a chain that includes a free agent's action. Identify which party is which in each scenario.
The intervening free agent is key to distinguishing them.
3
Apply the Rule to the First Furu'
In the digger-thrower case: the digger is indirect, the thrower is direct. Explain why the thrower is liable despite the digger's initial wrong.
The thrower's voluntary act severs the causal link to the digger.
4
Apply the Rule to the Second Furu'
In the thief-director case: the director is indirect, the thief is direct. The thief is liable, not the director.
Contrast with a case where the director is a trustee — the exception.
5
Identify the Istithna' (Exception)
When the indirect cause is a trustee (wadī‘) or someone under a contractual duty of care, that duty transforms indirect causation into direct liability. Analyze the trustee example.
The trustee's betrayal/negligence makes him jointly liable.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۱/۱۱ - القاعدة الحادية والثلاثون: إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب يضاف الحكم إلى المباشر (م ۹۰ ) . واجتماع المتسبب والمباشر في حادثة يكون بأن يتخلل بين عمل المتسبب وحدوث الحادثة فعل شخص آخر مختار . فهذا الشخص عندئذ يكون مباشراً، فيضاف الفعل إليه لأنه ألصق به من المتسبب السابق ويكون هو الضامن للضرر ولو كان المتسبب أيضاً معتدياً، لوجود من هو أولى بتحمل التبعة، وهو المباشر. وعلى هذا لو حفر شخص في الطريق العام دون إذن، فجاء شخص آخر وألقى في الحفرة حيواناً لغيره كان هذا الملقي هو الضامن دون الحافر. بخلاف ما لو سقط الحيوان من نفسه فإنَّ الحافر يضمن لانفراد التسبب . - وكذا لو دل شخص لصاً على مال غيره فسرقه، فالضمان على اللص لا على الدال . بخلاف ما لو دل الوديع على مكان الوديعة التي عنده لصاً فسرقها، فإنَّ الوديع يضمن هنا لأنه متعهد بالحفظ، وهذه الدلالة خيانة أو تقصير، فتوجب عليه الضمان ولصاحب الوديعة تضمين اللص أيضاً إن عرف لأنه مباشر ضامن على كل حال .
The Thirty-First Rule: When the direct cause and the indirect cause come together, the ruling is attributed to the direct cause (Art. 90). The coming together of the indirect cause and the direct cause in an incident is by there being intervening between the action of the indirect cause and the occurrence of the incident an action of another free person. This person is then a direct cause, so the action is attributed to him because he is more closely connected to it than the prior indirect cause, and he is the guarantor of the harm even if the indirect cause was also transgressing, due to the existence of someone more deserving of bearing liability, namely the direct cause. Based on this: If a person digs in the public road without permission, then another person comes and throws someone else's animal into the hole, this thrower is the guarantor, not the digger. Contrary to the case where the animal falls by itself, then the digger is guarantor because of the sole indirect causation. Similarly, if a person directs a thief to someone else's property and he steals it, the liability is on the thief, not the director. Contrary to the case where the trustee (wadī‘) directs a thief to the location of the deposit in his keeping and he steals it, then the trustee is guarantor here because he is charged with preservation, and this direction is betrayal or negligence, so it obligates him to guarantee, and the owner of the deposit may also hold the thief liable if known, because he is a direct cause and is guarantor in any case.
Source pages p. 982

Commentary
This lesson is a qawida (legal maxim) with its furuat (applications). The text presents the Thirty-First Rule from the Majalla (Article 90), which governs cases where a direct cause (mubāshir) and an indirect cause (mutasabbib) both contribute to harm. The rule states that liability is assigned to the direct cause, as it is the more immediate actor. The text first defines the scenario: the indirect cause performs an initial action, but then a free-willed person intervenes directly, causing the harm. That intervening person is the direct cause and bears liability, even if the indirect cause was also at fault. Three furuat illustrate this: (1) Digger vs. thrower: A person digs a hole; another throws an animal in. The thrower is liable. Exception: if the animal falls by itself, the digger is liable because he is the sole indirect cause. (2) Directing a thief: One points out property to a thief; the thief steals. Liability is on the thief, not the director. (3) Trustee directing a thief: If a trustee (who is contractually obligated to safeguard) directs a thief to the deposit, the trustee is liable because his direction constitutes betrayal/negligence, and the owner may also pursue the thief as a direct cause.

Key Terms
مباشر (mubāshir): The person who directly performs the act that causes harm, without an intervening free-willed agent.
متسبب (mutasabbib): The person who sets in motion a chain of events that leads to harm, with a free-willed actor intervening.
تعدي (ta‘addī): Transgression beyond what is permitted, whether intentional or unintentional, that causes harm to another.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the 31st rule, if a person digs a hole in the public road without permission, and another person later pushes a friend's bicycle into the hole, who is liable for the damage?
A) The digger, because he created the hazard.
B) The person who pushed the bicycle, because he is the direct cause.
C) Both are equally liable.
D) Neither, because the road is public.

Key Takeaway
Liability in Hanafi tort law follows causal immediacy: the direct actor bears the loss, unless the indirect actor had a special duty that elevates his role.

The Thirty-Second Rule: No Liability for Spontaneous Animal Harm
Explaining the 32nd rule of the Majalla on animal liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the Hanafi maxim on animal harm, explain its textual basis, and apply it with its exception in a fatwa scenario.

Core Idea
An animal's spontaneous harm is exempt from liability, but owner negligence nullifies the exemption.

This lesson introduces the thirty-second legal maxim of the Majalla, rooted in a prophetic hadith. The rule states that harm caused by an animal acting on its own accord does not incur liability on its owner. However, this exemption is conditional; if the owner was negligent, liability attaches. Understanding this maxim is essential for issuing correct fatwas in tort cases involving animals.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its Arabic wording
Memorize the exact wording: 'جناية العجماء جبار'. This is the textual anchor for the rule.
The maxim is from Majalla Article 94.
2
Identify the textual proof (dalīl)
Recognize the hadith 'العجماء جرحها جبار' narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, and others. The hadith is the primary source.
The hadith is sahih and mutawatir in meaning.
3
Define key terms: العجماء and جبار
العجماء means any dumb animal; جبار means the harm is null (no liability). Comprehend the legal implications.
The term جبار here is a technical legal term, not general negligence.
4
Distinguish spontaneous vs. culpable harm
The rule applies only when the animal acts on its own (e.g., breaks tether, kicks without provocation). If the owner was negligent or directly caused the harm, the rule does not apply.
Examples: animal running off vs. owner setting it loose.
5
Recognize the istithnā' (exception)
The exemption is lifted when the owner's fault (ta‘addī or tafrīṭ) is present, e.g., failing to restrain the animal in a dangerous area.
This exception is based on other qawā‘id like 'al-ḍarar yuzāl'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۲/۸/۱ - القاعدة الثانية والثلاثون: جناية العجماء جبار» (م/ ٩٤). هذه القاعدة حديث نبوي لفظه: العجماء جرحها جبار أخرجه البخاري ومسلم ومالك وأحمد في مسنده، وأصحاب السنن الأربعة عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه . والعجماء البهيمة. وجرحها معناه هنا ما يصدر عنها من ضرر . ومعنى كونه «جباراً» (بضم الجيم أنه هدر لا مؤاخذة فيه ولا ضمان. ويلحظ في هذا الموضوع ما يلي: أ - المراد بالقاعدة ما تفعله البهائم من تلقاء نفسها. وذلك كما لو قطعت رباطها وشردت أو جفلت، أو نفحت برجلها، فأضرَّت أحداً، فلا ضمان على صاحبها.
The Thirty-Second Rule: 'The injury caused by a dumb animal is a loss' (Art. 94). This rule is a prophetic hadith: 'The dumb animal: its wound is a loss' narrated by al-Bukhari, Muslim, Malik, Ahmad in his Musnad, and the authors of the four Sunan from Abu Hurayra (may Allah be pleased with him). 'Al-Ajma'' means the animal. 'Jarh' (wound) here means the harm that emanates from it. The meaning of its being 'jubār' (with damma on the jim) is that it is null (hadar), with no liability and no guarantee. In this subject, the following is noted: (a) The rule refers to what animals do of their own accord. For example, if it breaks its tether and runs off or startles, or kicks with its leg, and harms someone, there is no liability on its owner.
Source pages p. 983

Commentary
This lesson is a qawida (legal maxim) supported by a dalīl naqlī (textual proof). The text presents the Thirty-Second Rule from the Majalla (Article 94), which originates from a well-established hadith reported in the major hadith collections. The rule states that harm caused by an animal (al-‘ajmā’) acting on its own is 'jubār' – i.e., no liability (damān) falls on the owner. The text clarifies that 'al-‘ajmā' means the animal, and 'jarh' (wound) here refers broadly to any harm it causes. The rule applies only when the animal acts of its own accord, without any human intervention or negligence. Examples include the animal breaking its tether and causing damage, or kicking or startling and injuring someone. In such cases, the owner is not liable. This rule is an exception to general principles of liability (damān) due to the lack of human fault.

Key Terms
العجماء (al-‘ajmā’): Literally 'the dumb one', referring to any animal (bahīmah) that cannot speak.
جبار (jubār): Legally: a loss for which no compensation is due; the harm is considered null (hadar).
جرحها (jarḥuhā): Wound; in this context, any harm or damage caused by the animal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the thirty-second rule, in which situation is the owner NOT liable?
A) The owner left a gate open and the horse ran onto the highway causing an accident.
B) The owner's dog, while leashed, suddenly lunges at a passerby and bites him.
C) The owner's cat, while sleeping, scratches a child who accidentally steps on its tail.
D) The owner deliberately set his bull loose in a field to frighten trespassers.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'جناية العجماء جبار' exempts spontaneous animal harm from liability unless owner negligence intervenes.

The Maxim of Direct vs. Indirect Cause in Liability
Explains the Hanafi rule on direct vs. indirect causation with examples and an exception.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi qāʿida 'When the direct and indirect cause meet, liability is attributed to the direct actor' to concrete tort cases, and identify the exception for trustees.

Core Idea
When direct and indirect cause meet, the direct actor bears liability, even if the indirect cause was also at fault.

This lesson presents a key Hanafi maxim governing tort liability. Understanding when a person is directly or indirectly responsible for harm is essential for a mufti. The passage provides clear examples and a critical exception. By mastering this rule, you will be able to resolve common disputes in damages.

Guided Path
1
State the Maxim
Identify the qāʿida: إذا اجتمع المباشر والمتسبب يضاف الحكم إلى المباشر. This means when the direct actor and indirect cause are both present, the legal ruling (liability) is assigned to the direct actor.
The maxim is a minor rule (sughra) within the broader maxims system.
2
Understand the Reasoning
The direct actor is preferred because his act is the immediate cause of harm. He is closer to the harm and thus more deserving of bearing the consequence. This ensures clarity in attribution.
The Hanafi jurists prioritize proximity over mere causation.
3
Apply to Furuat
Work through each case: (1) Pit digger vs. thrower – thrower liable. (2) Director of thief vs. thief – thief liable. (3) Depositary directing thief – depositary liable due to breach of trust, but thief also liable as direct actor.
The exception for the depositary shows that the maxim is overridden when the indirect cause has a fiduciary duty.
4
Identify the Exception
The depositary (trustee) becomes liable because his direction constitutes betrayal (khiyānah). The governing maxim here is 'الأمانة تقتضي الضمان بالخيانة' (trust imposes liability for breach).
This exception does not negate the main maxim; it operates side by side, making the indirect cause also liable.
5
Derive the Fatwa Hierarchy
This maxim is well-established in Hanafi fatwa works. The mufta bihi is that the direct actor is liable; the indirect cause is not unless he is a trustee. The exception is considered a specific override.
The hierarchy: general rule → exception for trustees.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
من
المتسبب السابق ويكون هو الضامن للضرر ولو كان المتسبب أيضاً معتدياً، لوجود من هو أولى بتحمل التبعة، وهو المباشر.
وعلى هذا لو حفر شخص في الطريق العام دون إذن، فجاء شخص آخر وألقى في الحفرة حيواناً لغيره كان هذا الملقي هو الضامن دون الحافر.
التسبب .
بخلاف ما لو سقط الحيوان من نفسه فإنَّ الحافر يضمن لانفراده بالتسبب.
- وكذا لو دل شخص لصاً على مال غيره فسرقه، فالضمان على اللص لا على الدال .
بخلاف ما لو دل الوديع على مكان الوديعة التي عنده لصاً فسرقها، فإنَّ الوديع يضمن هنا لأنه متعهد بالحفظ، وهذه الدلالة خيانة أو تقصير، فتوجب عليه الضمان ولصاحب الوديعة تضمين اللص أيضاً إن عرف لأنه مباشر ضامن على كل حال .
Chapter Ten: The Universal Maxims in Fiqh. From the preceding indirect cause, he becomes the guarantor of the harm even if the indirect cause was also a transgressor, because there exists one who is more deserving of bearing the consequence, and that is the direct actor. Based on this: if a person digs in a public road without permission, then another person comes and throws someone else's animal into the pit, this thrower is the guarantor, not the digger. Indirect causation. Contrary to the case where the animal falls by itself, then the diger guarantees because he alone is the indirect cause. Likewise, if a person directs a thief to someone else's property and he steals it, the guarantee is on the thief, not the director. Contrary to the case where the depositary directs the thief to the location of the deposit in his possession and he steals it, then the depositary guarantees here because he is entrusted with preservation, and this direction is betrayal or negligence, so it imposes guarantee on him, and the owner of the deposit may also hold the thief liable if known, because he is a direct actor and guarantor in any case.
Source pages p. 983

Commentary
This passage presents the Hanafi rule on causation (tasbīb) and direct action (mubāshara). When a direct actor and an indirect cause combine, the liability falls on the direct actor, even if the indirect cause was also at fault. The reasoning is that the direct actor is closer to the harm and thus more deserving of bearing the consequence. The passage provides three applications: (1) A digger of a pit and a person who throws an animal into it: the thrower (direct actor) is liable. (2) If the animal falls by itself, the digger becomes liable because he alone is the cause. (3) A person who directs a thief to steal: the thief (direct actor) is liable, not the director. An exception is when the director is a depositary who breaches trust; then he is liable along with the thief. This aligns with the Hanafi principle of preferring the direct actor over the indirect cause.

Key Terms
المباشر (al-mubāshir): The person who directly causes the harm by his physical act.
المتسبب (al-mutasabbib): The person who indirectly causes the harm through a chain of causation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Based on the passage, if a person digs a pit in a public road without permission, and another person throws an animal into it, who is liable?
A) The digger only.
B) The thrower only.
C) Both equally.
D) Neither because the road is public.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim 'when direct and indirect cause meet, liability is on the direct actor' provides a clear rule for torts, with the exception that a trustee who breaches trust becomes liable as well.

The Maxim 'Jinaayat al-'Ajmaa' Jubar'
Presents and explains the hadith-based maxim that harm caused by an animal without human action incurs no liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'Jinaayat al-'Ajmaa' Jubar' to determine liability for animal-caused harm and identify the exception when human action triggers liability.

Core Idea
An animal's injury is without liability unless triggered by human action.

This lesson explores the thirty-second maxim of the Majalla, derived from a prophetic hadith. It establishes that harm caused by an animal acting on its own incurs no liability on the owner. However, the maxim is not absolute; when a human act—such as riding or startling—induces the animal's injury, the human becomes liable as a direct or indirect cause. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly applying the maxim in tort law and issuing fatwas.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
State the maxim 'جناية العجماء جبار' and note that it is a prophetic hadith narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, Malik, Ahmad, and others.
The hadith is the primary textual evidence (dalil naqli).
2
Define the key terms
Define 'al-ajma'u' as the animal (bahima) and 'jubar' as null/disregarded (hader) meaning no liability.
The term 'jubar' (with damm on the jim) is a technical term in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Distinguish between animal-alone and human-involved scenarios
Apply the maxim to cases where the animal acts of its own accord (e.g., breaks tether, kicks, cat kills bird). Then explore the exception: if a human action (riding, startling) causes the animal's injurious act, the human is liable.
The operator is a direct actor (mubashir) or indirect cause (mutasabbib).
4
Apply the maxim to novel cases
Test your understanding by applying the maxim to new scenarios, ensuring you correctly identify whether human involvement exists.
Use the furuat from the lesson as patterns.
5
Understand the inter-madhhab difference
Note that some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) hold the owner liable if the animal is known to be dangerous, using qiyas, whereas the Hanafi position relies on the explicit hadith.
The thamarah al-khilaf is practical: in Hanafi, no payment; in Shafi'i, diyat may be due.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
۹۲/۸/۱ - القاعدة الثانية والثلاثون: جناية العجماء جبار» (م/ ٩٤).
هذه القاعدة حديث نبوي لفظه: العجماء جرحها جبار أخرجه البخاري ومسلم ومالك وأحمد في مسنده، وأصحاب السنن الأربعة عن أبي هريرة رضي الله عنه .
والعجماء البهيمة. وجرحها معناه هنا ما يصدر عنها من ضرر . ومعنى كونه «جباراً» (بضم الجيم أنه هدر لا مؤاخذة فيه ولا ضمان. ويلحظ في هذا الموضوع ما يلي:
أ - المراد بالقاعدة ما تفعله البهائم من تلقاء نفسها.
وذلك كما لو قطعت رباطها وشردت أو جفلت، أو نفحت برجلها، فأضرَّت أحداً، فلا ضمان على صاحبها.
- وكذلك لو اغتالت هرة شخص طائر غيره، أو ربط شخصان دابتيهما مكان مأذون بالربط فيه فأتلفت إحداهما الأخرى، فلا ضمان على أحد. ب - أما لو كانت جناية العجماء منبعثة عن فعل إنسان، كما لو كان الشخص راكباً للدابة ولو في أرضه فداست شيئاً للغير، فإنه ضامن، لأنه يعتبر مباشراً.
وكذا لو أجفلها شخص فضربت أحداً فإنه يضمن جنايتها لأنه متسبب بإجفالها . ر باب جناية البهيمة والجناية عليها في كتاب الديات في الدر المختار وحاشيته ٣٨٧/٥).
92/8/1 - The thirty-second maxim: 'The injury of an animal is null' (M/94). This maxim is a prophetic hadith, its wording: 'The injury of an animal is null' narrated by Bukhari, Muslim, Malik, Ahmad in his Musnad, and the authors of the four Sunan from Abu Huraira, may God be pleased with him. 'Al-ajma'u' means the animal. 'Jarhuha' here means harm that issues from it. The meaning of 'jubār' (with damm on the jim) is that it is disregarded, no accountability and no guarantee. The following is noted in this subject: A - The maxim refers to what animals do of their own accord. For example, if it breaks its tether and runs away or is startled, or kicks with its leg and harms someone, there is no guarantee on its owner. Also, if a cat kills someone's bird, or two persons tie their animals in a place permitted for tying and one damages the other, there is no guarantee on anyone. B - However, if the injury of the animal arises from a human act, such as when a person is riding the animal even on his own land and it tramples someone else's property, then he is liable because he is considered a direct actor. Likewise, if someone startles it and it strikes someone, he is liable for its injury because he is an indirect cause by startling it. See chapter on injury of animals and injury upon them in the book of blood money in al-Durr al-Mukhtar and its gloss, 5/387.
Source pages p. 983

Commentary
This passage introduces the thirty-second maxim from the Majalla, which is a prophetic hadith: the injury caused by an animal is without liability (jubār). The maxim applies when the animal acts on its own (e.g., breaks loose, kicks, or startles). In such cases, the owner is not liable. The passage provides two applications: (1) a cat killing a bird, and (2) two animals tied in a permitted place damaging each other. However, if the animal's action is due to a human act, such as riding it or causing it to startle, the human is liable—either as a direct actor (rider) or as an indirect cause (one who startles). This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh: the maxim only exempts liability when the animal is the sole cause without human intervention.

Key Terms
العجماء (al-‘ajmā’): The animal; specifically any non-human animal that causes harm.
جبار (jubār): Null, disregarded; no liability or accountability.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'جناية العجماء جبار', when is an animal's owner liable for harm caused by the animal?
A) Whenever the animal causes harm.
B) Only if the animal was previously known to be dangerous.
C) When the harm arises from a human act, such as riding or startling the animal.
D) The owner is never liable.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim exempts owners from liability for animal-caused harm unless a human action triggers the injury.

Special Authority is Stronger than General Authority: The Maxim and Its Exceptions
Presents the maxim that specific authority overrides general authority and lists exceptions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-wilaya al-khassa aqwa min al-wilaya al-amma' to determine which authority prevails in guardianship cases and identify the two key exceptions where the judge's general authority overrides specific authority.

Core Idea
Special authority (guardian) beats general authority (judge), except when public interest demands the judge's intervention.

This lesson unpacks the thirty-third maxim from the Majalla: 'al-wilaya al-khassa aqwa min al-wilaya al-amma' (special authority is stronger than general authority). This maxim is fundamental in Hanafi guardianship and trusteeship law. It establishes a hierarchy: a specific guardian (e.g., a father, testamentary guardian, or waqf trustee) has priority over the general guardian (the qadi) in managing the ward's property or the endowment. However, the maxim is not absolute; there are exceptions rooted in public interest. You will learn how to apply the maxim and when to invoke its exceptions.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and define its terms
Read the maxim: 'الولاية الخاصة أقوى من الولاية العامة'. Understand that 'wilaya khassa' refers to authority given by sharia or appointment to a specific person over a specific ward or property (e.g., father over child, trustee over waqf). 'Wilaya amma' is the general authority of the judge over all wards and public assets.
The maxim is a legal presumption. The burden is on the general authority to justify intervention.
2
Apply the maxim to the standard cases
From the text: the judge cannot deal with minor's property if a guardian exists; the judge cannot manage waqf if a trustee exists. These are direct applications.
3
Identify the exceptions from the text
Two exceptions: (1) judge can lease waqf property for long term when renovation needed, trustee cannot; (2) judge can lend minor's money when needed, father/guardian cannot. Both are based on public interest (maslaha).
Exceptions are limited and require necessity.
4
Understand the rationale (ta'lil)
The reason for the exceptions is that preserving waqf and orphan property is a public right (haqq 'amm). The judge, as general guardian, has the duty to ensure proper management and can override specific guardians when they are unable or exceed limits.
The judge also has power to hold guardians accountable and dismiss them.
5
Practice applying the maxim in fatwa
Given a scenario, determine if the specific guardian's authority holds or if the judge can intervene. Use the maxim as default, then check if any exception applies based on public interest and necessity.
Always consider the hierarchy: specific authority > general authority, unless public interest requires otherwise.

Source Text

93/1 - The thirty-third maxim: 'Special authority is stronger than general authority' (M/59). Thus, the judge does not have the right to dispose of the property of a minor or an endowment if there is a guardian or trustee. This maxim has already been discussed in the theory of capacity and guardianship (see: 9/66). Exceptions: An exception to the maxim is that the judge has the right to lease the real property of the endowment for a long period when there is a need for renovation, and the trustee does not have that. Likewise, the judge has the right to lend the property of a minor, and the father or guardian does not have that (see: 6/62). The reason for this exception is that preserving the property of endowments and orphans is a public right. Exceeding the permitted limits for guardians, executors, and trustees reverts to the estimation of the general guardian. Therefore, the judge has the right to hold them accountable and dismiss them when necessary.
Source pages p. 984

Commentary
This passage explains the thirty-third maxim from the Majalla: specific authority (e.g., a testamentary guardian for a minor or a trustee of a waqf) has priority over general authority (the judge or public authority). Therefore, the judge cannot interfere in the management of the minor's property or the endowment as long as the specific guardian or trustee is acting properly. However, there are two exceptions: (1) the judge may lease waqf property for long terms when renovation is necessary, even if the trustee cannot; (2) the judge may borrow money for the minor's property when needed, even if the father or guardian cannot. The rationale for these exceptions is the public interest in preserving the assets. The judge, as the general guardian (wali ‘amm), has the ultimate authority to oversee and, if necessary, replace the specific guardian.

Key Terms
الولاية الخاصة (al-wilāya al-khāṣṣa): Specific authority granted to a person (e.g., father, guardian, trustee) over a particular ward or property.
الولاية العامة (al-wilāya al-‘āmma): General authority of the judge (qadi) or public guardian over all wards and public interests.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) No, because the trustee's authority overrides the judge's.
B) Yes, always, because the judge is the general guardian.
C) Yes, but only when renovation is needed and the trustee cannot do so.
D) No, leasing waqf property is never allowed.

Key Takeaway
Special authority prevails over general authority unless public interest requires an exception, and the judge as general guardian has the duty to intervene when specific guardians fail.

The Thirty-Fifth Maxim: The Translator's Statement is Proof
Defining the maxim and its operative condition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Thirty-Fifth Maxim, define al-mutarjim, identify the judicial scenario, and apply the condition of 'adalah for accepting the translator's testimony.

Core Idea
The translator's word is binding proof in court, but only if he is upright ('adl) and trustworthy.

The maxim 'يقبل قول المترجم مطلقاً' (The statement of the translator is accepted absolutely) is one of the legal qawaid governing procedure and evidence in the Hanafi madhhab. It addresses situations where parties or witnesses speak a language different from the judge's. While the maxim seems unconditional, the author clarifies that acceptance hinges on the translator's 'adalah (uprightness). In this lesson, we will dissect the definition, the scenario, and the essential condition that makes the translator's statement a hujjah (proof).

Guided Path
1
Identify the Maxim and Its Scope
Read the maxim 'يقبل قول المترجم مطلقاً' and note that 'مطلقاً' suggests apparent unconditionality. The commentary immediately restricts this by introducing conditions.
The maxim is legal, not absolute in application.
2
Define al-Mutarjim (Translator)
The text defines al-mutarjim as 'الذي يفسر لغة بأخرى' – the one who explains one language to another. Recognize that this role is also called al-turjuman.
This is a lexical definition; the legal definition is tied to the context of judicial proceedings.
3
Identify the Judicial Scenario
The scenario is when one or both litigants, or some witnesses, do not speak the judge's language. The judge relies on a translator to convey the speech. Understand that this is a practical necessity for justice.
The translator acts as an intermediary for accurate communication.
4
Analyze the Condition of Acceptance
The text states two conditions are required, but only one is given: the translator must be 'adl (upright) and not fasiq (corrupt), because a fasiq is not trustworthy. The second condition is implied but missing in the OCR; it is likely that the translator must be reliable (amin) in transmission.
The condition of 'adalah mirrors that of witnesses in Hanafi fiqh.
5
Derive the Ruling from the Maxim
Conclusion: The translator's statement is a hujjah (proof) in court if he meets the condition of 'adalah. A fasiq translator is not accepted because his word is not trusted.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

The 35th Maxim: Accepting the Translator's Statement Absolutely
Explains the 35th maxim of the Majalla on accepting a translator's statement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'يقبل قول المترجم مطلقاً' to identify when a translator's statement is accepted in legal proceedings and when the exception for hudud applies.

Core Idea
A translator's statement is accepted absolutely in all cases except hudud, where extra caution requires a male translator.

This lesson unpacks the Majalla's 35th maxim on the acceptance of a translator's statement in legal proceedings. While the default rule is broad acceptance in all types of claims and evidence, the Hanafi jurists carved out an important exception for hudud crimes. Understanding this maxim and its exception is crucial for a mufti or judge when navigating multilingual litigation.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its scope
Identify the Arabic text of the qawida and note that it governs the acceptance of a translator's statement in court procedure.
The maxim is 'يقبل قول المترجم مطلقاً'.
2
List the conditions for the translator's acceptance
Extract the two conditions from the passage: the translator must be just (adl) and must have sufficient knowledge of both languages to avoid error.
Multiple translators are not required; one suffices.
3
Explain the meaning of 'absolutely' in the maxim
Understand that 'مطلقاً' means the translator's statement is accepted in all types of lawsuits and evidence, whether the translator is male or female.
This is the general rule.
4
Identify the exception for hudud
Recognize that jurists excepted hudud crimes, requiring the translator to be male as an extra precaution.
The exception is based on the principle 'الحدود تدرأ بالشبهات'.
5
Apply the maxim to a hypothetical furu'
Given a scenario (e.g., a theft case with a female translator), decide whether the translator's statement is accepted and justify using the maxim and exception.
This tests practical application.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

المترجم والترجمان هو الذي يفسر لغة بأخرى.
فإذا كان أحد المتداعيين أو كلاهما أو الشهود أو بعضهم، لغتهم غير لغة الحاكم يعتمد الحاكم ترجماناً ينقل إليه الكلام.
فهذا الترجمان مؤتمن وقوله حجة بشرط أن تتوافر فيه صفتان:
١ - أن يكون عدلاً غير فاسق لأن الفاسق غير مؤتمن .
الواجد [؟] .
۲ - وأن يكون عالماً باللغتين علماً كافياً يؤمن معه الخطأ والخلط ولا يشترط تعدد التراجمة كنصاب الشهادة، بل يكتفي بالمترجم
ومعنى الإطلاق الوارد في القاعدة أنه يقبل قول المترجم في أنواع الدعاوى والبينات، رجلاً كان أو امرأة.
وقد استثنى الفقهاء الجرائم الموجبة لعقوبات الحدود، فاشترطوا في المترجم في دعاويها وإثباتها أن يكون رجلاً، زيادة في الاحتياط.
95/81 - The thirty-fifth maxim: 'The statement of the translator is accepted absolutely.'
The translator (mutarjim/turjuman) is one who interprets one language into another.
If one of the litigants or both, or some of the witnesses, have a language other than that of the judge, the judge relies on a translator to convey the speech.
This translator is trusted and his statement is authoritative, provided he possesses two qualities:
1. He must be just (adl), not immoral, because an immoral person is not trustworthy.
2. He must be sufficiently knowledgeable in both languages to the extent that error and confusion are avoided. Multiple translators are not required like the required number of witnesses; one translator suffices.
The meaning of 'absolutely' in the maxim is that the translator's statement is accepted in all types of claims and evidence, whether male or female.
However, jurists except crimes that entail hudud punishments, for which they require the translator in their claims and proof to be male, as an extra precaution.
Source pages p. 986

Commentary
This passage presents the thirty-fifth maxim from the Majalla regarding the acceptance of a translator's statement in legal proceedings. The author defines a translator as one who renders speech from one language to another. When a judge cannot understand a party or witness due to language differences, he appoints a translator to convey the speech. The translator is considered trustworthy, and his statement is accepted as evidence provided he meets two conditions: he must be just (adl) and not immoral, because an immoral person is not reliable; and he must have sufficient knowledge of both languages to avoid errors. Unlike witnesses, multiple translators are not required; a single translator suffices. The maxim's word 'absolutely' means the translator's statement is accepted in all types of lawsuits and evidence, whether the translator is male or female. However, an exception is made for crimes that carry hudud punishments, where the translator must be male for extra caution. This reflects the Hanafi approach of strictness in hudud cases.

Key Terms
المترجم (al-mutarjim): The person who interprets speech from one language to another for the judge.
الحدود (al-hudud): Fixed punishments prescribed by Shariah for specific crimes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the 35th maxim, in which type of cases is a male translator required?
A) All cases involving witnesses
B) Only hudud crimes
C) Cases where the litigants are women
D) Cases involving financial transactions

Key Takeaway
The translator's statement is accepted absolutely except in hudud, where only a male translator suffices.

Qa'idah: A Person is Bound by His Admission and Its Applications
Explanation of qa'idah with furu' and evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qa'idah 'al-mar'u mu'akhadh bi-iqrar' to cases of unexplained wealth in public office and identify the conditions for a binding admission.

Core Idea
Your admission binds you; silence in the face of inexplicable wealth is an implicit admission of wrongdoing.

This lesson unpacks the thirty-seventh qa'idah from al-Majalla: 'A person is held accountable by his admission.' We will examine its scope, its evidence from the Quran, and its powerful application to the wealth of public officials—a principle that has been used since the time of Umar ibn al-Khattab and continues relevance today in illicit enrichment laws. By the end, you will see how this qa'idah operates in both classical fiqh and contemporary governance.

Guided Path
1
Identify the qa'idah and its plain meaning
State the maxim in Arabic and English. Understand that it means a person's own declaration against himself is legally binding, provided he is competent.
The qa'idah is from al-Majalla article 79.
2
Find the textual evidence (dalil)
Locate the Quranic evidence cited: verse 2:282 (ayat al-mudayanah). The debtor is commanded to dictate, which is an act of admission. Explain how this demonstrates the principle.
Al-Sarakhsi's al-Mabsut is referenced for further details.
3
Analyze the furu' (application) to public officials
The passage gives a key branch: if an employee shows unexplained wealth, it is evidence of betrayal. Their inability to explain is treated as an implicit admission of illicit gain. Study the ruling that follows: dismissal and confiscation.
This is based on the maxim 'al-mar'u mu'akhadh bi-iqrar' and the sub-rule 'min ayna laka hadha?'
4
Examine the historical precedent of Umar
Umar ibn al-Khattab's practice: he recorded employees' wealth at appointment and confiscated any surplus without known source. This shows the early application of the principle in Islamic governance.
The anecdotes illustrate the strength of the rule: Umar's statement 'water and clay are my trustees'.
5
Connect to modern fatwa and law
The passage discusses the 1958 Illicit Gain Law in Syria/Egypt. As a mufti or legal scholar, you would apply the same qa'idah to contemporary cases: employees must declare assets, and unexplained wealth triggers liability.
This is an example of how classical qawaid inform modern legislation.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية الفقه
وذلك إذا كان عاقلاً كامل الأهلية، لأنَّ المفروض أنه أعلم من غيره بما فعل من أسباب الالتزام وبما عليه من حقوق وأن له ولاية على إلزام نفسه بما يشاء من طريق الإنشاء، بالعقود وغيرها من الأسباب المنشئة فله بل عليه شرعاً، أن يكشف الحجاب عن الالتزامات المستقرة عليه، لأن كتمان حقوق الناس وهضمها جرام.
ومن
الأدلة النصية الدالة على هذه القاعدة قوله تعالى في آية المداينة
من سورة البقرة بعد إرشاده إلى توثيق المداينة بالكتابة :
وليملي الَّذِي عَلَيْهِ الْحَقُّ وَلْيَتَّقِ اللهَ رَبَّهُ .
فقد أمر المدين بالإملال - أي الإملاء - واعتبر ذلك توثيقاً للدين. وهذا معنى المؤاخذة بالإقرار (ر : المبسوط للسرخسي في أول كتاب الإقرار. وهناك نصوص من السنة كثيرة.

Chapter (81): Explanation of the Majalla rules and their classification. Where do you get this? Among the important branches of this rule in general administrative works: government workers, tax collectors of the treasury, trustees of endowments and their scribes and the like, if signs of wealth appear on them and they build buildings without knowing the source of their wealth, that is evidence of their betrayal and bribery. So it is permissible to dismiss them and confiscate their wealth, unless they prove a source for it. (1) 97/81 - The thirty-seventh rule: A person is held accountable by his admission (Majalla article 79). (1) Umar ibn al-Khattab, may Allah be pleased with him, did that. When he appointed an employee, he counted his wealth in a written record. Then if he found with him a surplus without a source, he confiscated it or shared it with him according to the strength of suspicion and placed it in the treasury. Umar passed by a building being built with stone and plaster, and said: Whose is this? They mentioned an employee of his in Bahrain. He said: The dirhams refuse except to show their necks, and he shared his wealth with him. And he used to say: For every traitor I have two trustees: water and clay. And he confiscated from al-Harith ibn Wahb al-Laythi, and said to him: What about the young she-camels and slaves you sold for one hundred dinars? [al-qlas (with kasr qaf) plural of 'qlus' (with fatha qaf), it is the young she-camel.] He said: I went out with my own expense and traded with it. He said: By Allah, we did not send you for trade. Go away! He said: By Allah, I will not work for you after this. Umar said: By Allah, I will not employ you after this. (Ref: Sirat Umar ibn al-Khattab by the two judge brothers Ali and Naji al-Tantawi 1/231-233, al-Isaba 4/220, and Uyun al-Akhbar by Ibn Qutayba 1/53). This is what contemporaries today call the rule: 'Where do you get this?' They demand the enactment of laws to permit it, after many rulers and employees have become newly wealthy without a known source except betrayal and exploitation of the job. These legal principles and rulings provided the required legislative treatment. Indeed, after the union between Egypt and Syria, the 'Illicit Gain Law' was enacted in 1958 CE, requiring every employee and future employee to submit a statement inventorying all wealth owned by him, his wife, or his children. But it was applied for a period and then neglected. The tenth chapter: The overarching juristic rules. And that is if (the person) is sane and fully competent, because it is assumed that he knows better than others about the obligations he incurred and the rights he owes, and that he has authority to bind himself as he wishes through creation (of obligations) by contracts and other creative means. So he may, indeed he must, according to Sharia, lift the veil from the stable obligations upon him, because concealing the rights of people and wasting them is a crime. Among the textual evidences for this rule is the saying of the Almighty in the verse of debt from Surat al-Baqarah, after guiding to documentation of debt by writing: 'And let the one who owes dictate, and let him fear Allah, his Lord.' So He commanded the debtor to dictate (i.e., imlaa'), and considered that a documentation of the debt. And this is the meaning of being held to account by admission. (Ref: al-Mabsut of al-Sarakhsi at the beginning of the book of admission. And there are many texts from the Sunnah.) This, and applications of this rule have already been presented on many sufficient occasions.
Source pages p. 988

Commentary
This is a lesson on the thirty-seventh qa'idah (legal maxim) from al-Majalla: 'A person is held accountable by his admission.' The passage type is a qa'idah with its furuat (applications). The author begins by introducing an important branch of this rule: government employees who show wealth without a known source – their wealth becomes evidence of treachery, and they may be dismissed and their wealth confiscated unless they prove a legitimate source. This is a practical application of the qa'idah: their silence or inability to explain wealth is treated as an implicit admission of unlawful gain. The author then discusses the historical precedent of Caliph Umar ibn al-Khattab, who would record employees' wealth and confiscate any unexplained surplus. Umar's practice illustrates the principle of 'min ayna laka hadha?' (where did you get this?), a forerunner to modern illicit enrichment laws. The author notes that such principles were codified in the 1958 Syrian-Egyptian Illicit Gain Law, though it was later neglected. The qa'idah itself requires that the person be sane and fully competent, because only such a person has full authority over their obligations. The Quranic evidence is from the debt verse (2:282), where the debtor is commanded to dictate the terms, which is akin to making an admission. Thus, the qa'idah is firmly rooted in the primary sources. The author concludes that sufficient applications have already been presented.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrar): A declaration by a person acknowledging a right or obligation against himself
مؤاخذة (mu'akhadhah): Holding someone accountable or liable based on their statement
كسب غير مشروع (kasb ghayr mashru'): Illicit gain; wealth acquired through illegal means such as bribery or embezzlement

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

D) 'And establish the prayer and give the zakat' (2:43)

Key Takeaway
The qa'idah 'al-mar'u mu'akhadh bi-iqrar' binds a sane, competent person to his own admission, and silence when wealth is unexplained becomes an implicit admission of unlawful gain.
