The Thirty-Eighth Maxim: Established by Proof is Like Established by Sight
Explaining a major procedural maxim with its evidence, consequences, and derived sub-maxim

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the maxim and its three consequences, apply them to judicial scenarios, and distinguish between the transitive effect of evidence and the restrictive effect of confession.

Core Idea
What is proven by evidence in court is treated as certain, even if reality may differ, because the judge rules on the apparent.

This lesson explores a foundational procedural maxim in Hanafi jurisprudence. It explains how judicial proof (bayyina) is given the weight of certainty for legal purposes, and outlines its practical consequences. You will also learn the related sub-maxim that evidence is transitive while confession is restrictive.

Guided Path
1
Understand the maxim and its scope
Read the maxim 'What is established by proof is like what is established by sight.' Note that 'proof' here means judicial evidence (bayyina). The maxim applies in court: once evidence is accepted, the fact is treated as certain, ignoring possibilities of error or falsehood.
The maxim does not claim infallibility but sets a procedural rule for finality.
2
Identify the three legal consequences
The passage lists three consequences: (1) denial after proof is not accepted, (2) no contrary claim after judgment without a new cause, (3) the proof extends to all parties sharing the same cause (e.g., joint debtors). Commit these to memory.
3
Analyze the justification
The judge's duty is to rule on what appears before him, not to uncover ultimate truth. The hadith of the Prophet (ﷺ) confirms this: he judged based on arguments presented. This justifies treating evidence as if it were personal observation.
Reference: Hadith in Fath al-Qadir.
4
Distinguish bayyina and iqrar
The derived sub-maxim (Majalla article 78) states: evidence is a transitive proof, confession is restrictive. Evidence benefits all relevant parties; confession only binds the confessor. Work through the example of joint debtors.
Example: If one joint debtor confesses, he alone is liable unless the other confirms.
5
Apply to a scenario
Take a case where a judge rules based on two witnesses. Apply the three consequences. Then consider if later the witnesses are proven liars. The istithna' (exception) says the judgment may be overturned if definitive falsehood emerges, governed by the maxim 'certainty is not removed by doubt.'

Chapter Ten: The General Legal Maxims. And that is if he is rational and fully competent, because it is assumed that he knows more than others about the causes of obligation he has performed and the rights upon him, and that he has authority to obligate himself as he wishes through creation (insha') by contracts and other creative causes. So it is upon him, legally speaking, to lift the veil from the stable obligations upon him, because hiding people's rights and diminishing them is a crime. Among the textual evidences indicating this rule is His statement, the Exalted, in the verse of debt from Surah Al-Baqarah, after guiding to documenting the debt by writing: "And let the one upon whom is the right dictate, and let him fear Allah, his Lord." So He commanded the debtor to dictate - i.e., to recite - and considered that as documentation of the debt. And this is the meaning of being held to account by confession. (See: Al-Mabsut by Al-Sarakhsi at the beginning of the book of confession. And there are many texts from the Sunnah.) This rule's applications have been presented on many sufficient occasions. 98/81 - The Thirty-eighth Maxim: What is established by proof is like what is established by sight. By proof is meant the judicial evidences that establish, which are called al-bayyinat. That is, what is established before the judge in the court by evidence, of events or their legal consequences, is considered a real matter as if it were sensed and seen by sight. So he judges based on this establishment even if there is a possibility of its contrary due to some reasons, such as the witnesses being liars disguised as righteous, or the existence of an exculpatory reason in reality that no one has discovered, and similar possibilities. That is because all these possibilities remain in the realm of imagination relative to apparent evidence, and it has been presented that imagination has no consideration. And that the task of judging is to build on what appears and is established before him, and the judge is not tasked with discovering the true realities, for this is beyond his capacity. And the saying of the Messenger (peace be upon him) has been presented: "Indeed you bring disputes to me, and some of you may be more eloquent in their argument than others, so I judge in his favor according to what I hear." etc... (See: Fath al-Qadir 3/2). And the consequences of the rule "what is established by proof is like what is established by sight" are three: 1- That denial from the defendant is not accepted after that establishment. 2- And that after judgment, he is not heard to claim contrary to what was judged against him, except for a new reason (see: Al-Tariqa al-Wadiha ila al-Bayyina al-Rajiha by Al-Hamzawi, discussion of Tahaatur al-Bayyinat, p. 229). 3- And that the proof by evidence extends to those not judged against who are related parties united by the cause of obligation, so the matter is considered established with respect to them as well, as we will see. What branches out from it of the maxims: 99/81 - "Evidence is a transitive proof, and confession is a restrictive proof" (Majalla article 78). We said in the explanation of the previous maxim: Indeed evidence is the establishing judicial proof. And the meaning of confession being a restrictive proof has also been presented on many occasions, like one who confesses to a debt shared between him and another; his confession takes effect upon himself so he is held accountable in his wealth, and it does not extend to his partner unless he confirms him. (See: Fath al-Qadir 4/4, 1/80, 71/81, the footnote, and we will explain it soon as well). Indeed, what is established of rights by evidence before the judge is considered the real truth, rather it is considered as if seen by sight according to the previous maxim; and then it is used as proof against others besides the one judged against as well. 81/100 - The Thirty-ninth Maxim: Evidence is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath is to preserve the original state (Majalla article 77).
Source pages p. 989

Commentary
This passage presents two major procedural maxims and a derived sub-maxim from Hanafi jurisprudence, particularly relevant to the work of a mufti and qadi. The passage begins by explaining the legal basis for holding a person accountable based on their own confession (referring to the earlier context), citing Quranic verse 2:282 where the debtor is commanded to dictate the debt. This establishes the principle that a person's own admission is binding.

The thirty-eighth maxim is then formally stated: "What is established by proof is like what is established by sight." The author clarifies that 'proof' here means judicial evidence (al-bayyinat). Once evidence is presented before a judge and the matter is established, it is treated as certain, even if there is a possibility that the evidence is false or that an exculpatory fact exists unknown to the court. The justification is that the judge's duty is to rule based on what is apparent and proven before him; he is not required to uncover absolute truths. This is supported by the well-known hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) judges based on the arguments presented.

The maxim has three specific consequences:
1. After the evidence is established, the defendant cannot simply deny it.
2. After judgment, the losing party cannot bring a contrary claim unless there is a new cause of action.
3. The evidentiary finding binds not only the parties to the case but also others who share the same underlying cause (e.g., joint debtors).

The passage then introduces a derived sub-maxim (article 78 of the Majalla): "Evidence is a transitive proof, and confession is a restrictive proof." This means that evidence (bayyina) benefits all relevant parties, while a confession (iqrar) only binds the confessor. For example, if one of two joint debtors confesses to the debt, he alone is liable unless the other confirms it.

Finally, the thirty-ninth maxim is stated (article 77 of the Majalla): "Evidence is to prove contrary to the apparent, and the oath is to preserve the original state." This maxim governs the burden of proof: evidence is required when one claims something contrary to the default presumption (e.g., claiming a debt when the default is freedom from obligation), while the oath is taken by the defendant to maintain the original presumption.

Key Terms
البرهان (al-burhān): In this passage, it refers to judicial evidence (bayyina) that establishes a fact in court.
البينة (al-bayyinah): The legal evidence presented in court, typically testimony of two male witnesses or one male and two females, but can include other proofs.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): A confession by a party, binding only on the confessor and not on others.
اليمين (al-yamīn): Oath taken by the defendant to affirm the original state (e.g., denying a claim) when no evidence is presented.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the maxim 'What is established by proof is like established by sight', which of the following is NOT a consequence?
A) After evidence is established, the defendant cannot simply deny.
B) After judgment, no contrary claim is allowed without a new cause.
C) The evidence only binds the parties directly involved, not others.
D) The judge must treat the evidence as if he personally witnessed the event.

Key Takeaway
The maxim ensures procedural finality by treating judicial proof as certainty, but allows correction if definitive falsehood is later discovered.

Three Consequences of the Maxim: Proof Equals Eyewitness
Opening of a chapter on Majalla rules, stating a key qawida and its three furūʿ.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three procedural consequences of the qawida 'al-thābit bil-burhān ka al-thābit bil-ʿiyān' and apply them to judicial scenarios.

Core Idea
Judicial proof is treated as certain as sight—once established, it cannot be denied, reopened without new cause, and binds all who share the same cause.

This passage opens the section on the Majalla rules by recalling the prophetic hadith that judges rule by apparent evidence. It then establishes the key qawida that what is proven by legal proof is equivalent to what is seen with one's own eyes. From this, three concrete procedural results are derived that govern the finality and scope of judicial decisions. Understanding these results is essential for any mufti or judge applying Hanafi law in court.

Guided Path
1
Recall the foundational qawida
Identify the principle 'al-thābit bil-burhān ka al-thābit bil-ʿiyān' and understand its meaning: legal proof gives the same certainty as direct observation.
This is a wusta (intermediate) maxim governing judicial evidence.
2
Understand the hadith basis
The hadith shows that the Prophet (saw) judged based on what he heard, not on hidden realities. This supports that apparent proof is the basis for judicial decisions.
The hadith is a dalīl naqlī for the principle.
3
List the three results
From the source text, extract the three consequences: (1) defendant cannot deny after proof, (2) after judgment no contrary claim without new cause, (3) proof extends to all related parties sharing the same cause.
Each result is a furūʿ (branch) of the qawida.
4
Explain the logic of each result
Result 1 prevents futile denials after conclusive proof. Result 2 ensures finality unless new evidence arises. Result 3 avoids contradictory rulings on the same underlying issue.
These results are procedural, not substantive: they do not claim infallibility.
5
Apply to a hypothetical scenario
Consider a case where a court rules that X owes Y money based on witness testimony. Can X later deny the debt? Can his brother, who was a guarantor, be bound by the same ruling?
Answers: No to denial; yes, the brother is bound by result 3.

۲ - وأن لا يسمع منه بعد القضاء ادعاء بخلاف ما قضي عليه به، إلا بسبب جديد (ر ): الطريقة الواضحة إلى البينة الراجحة» للحمزاوي بحث تهاتر» البينات ص/٢٢٩).

Perhaps - Chapter (81) Explanation and Classification of the Majalla Rules. And the saying of the Messenger (peace and blessings be upon him) has preceded: 'You come to me with disputes, and some of you are more eloquent in argument than others, so I rule in his favor based on what I hear.' etc. And the results of what is established by proof being like what is established by eyewitness are three: 1 - That denial is not accepted from the defendant after that establishment. 2 - And that after judgment, no claim contrary to what was judged against him is heard from him, except due to a new cause. 3 - And that the proof by evidence extends to others besides the one against whom it was ruled, from among the related parties who are united by a single cause, so the matter is considered established with respect to them as well, as we will see.
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage opens the section on the rules of the Majalla. It first cites a hadith indicating that judicial decisions are based on apparent evidence, even if the reality may differ. It then states the fundamental principle that what is proven by evidence is treated as certain as if seen with one's own eyes. Three consequences follow: (1) once proof is established, the defendant cannot deny it; (2) after judgment, no contrary claim is entertained unless a new cause arises; (3) the proof applies to all related parties who share the same underlying cause, not just the litigants. These rules underscore the binding and transitive nature of judicial proof in Hanafi law.

Key Terms
ثابت بالبرهان (thābit bil-burhān): Established by legal proof (evidence) before a judge.
ثابت بالعيان (thābit bil-ʿiyān): Established by direct observation, i.e., seen with one's own eyes.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) The defendant must pay immediately.
B) The defendant cannot deny after proof.
C) The judge must seek more evidence.
D) The plaintiff may withdraw.

Key Takeaway
The qawida and its three results ensure that judicial proof, once established, is binding and final for the litigants and all related parties sharing the same cause, preventing repetitive litigation unless a genuinely new cause arises.

The Transitive Proof vs. the Limited Confession
Explaining the maxim with a furu' example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the qawida 'al-bayyinah hujja muta'addiya wa al-iqrar hujja qasira' to distinguish the binding effects of evidence versus confession in judicial proceedings.

Core Idea
Evidence binds all; confession only binds the confessor.

This lesson unpacks a key Hanafi maxim from the field of judiciary (qada). Understanding why evidence is 'transitive' (applies beyond parties) while confession is 'limited' (only binds the confessor) is essential for correct adjudication. The passage provides a concrete example and links the maxim to the complementary principle that what is established by proof is as certain as eyewitness.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and identify its two components
Write out the full maxim in Arabic and separate the two clauses: the part about evidence and the part about confession. Recognize that the maxim compares two types of proofs.
The maxim is a wusta (intermediate) qawida in the Hanafi system.
2
Analyze the meaning of 'transitive' vs. 'limited'
Consider why evidence is transitive: it establishes a fact that becomes binding on all parties and can be used against non-litigants. Confession is limited because it only creates liability for the person who admits.
Confession is a 'proof against oneself' (hujja 'ala nafsihi).
3
Apply the fuṟu' (derivative case) from the text
Take the example of a shared debt. Confession by one debtor does not affect the other debtor unless the latter confirms. This illustrates the limited nature of confession.
The same principle applies to other shared obligations.
4
Relate the maxim to the complementary qawida
The passage mentions 'al-thabit bil-burhan ka al-thabit bil-'iyan'. Understand how this reinforces the transitive nature of evidence by equating judicially proven facts to eyewitness observations.
These two maxims work together in Hanafi juristic methodology.
5
Summarize practical implications for a mufti
A mufti must always check whether the proof in a case is evidence (transitive) or confession (limited). This affects who is bound by the ruling.
Mistaking one for the other can lead to unjust outcomes.

99/81 - البينة حجة متعدية والإقرار حجة قاصرة» (م/۷۸).

قلنا في شرح القاعدة السابقة : إن البينة هي الدليل القضائي المثبت.

وقد تقدم أيضاً معنى كون الإقرار حجة قاصرة في مناسبات عديدة، كمن أقرّ بدين مشترك عليه وعلى غيره فإنَّ إقراره هذا ينفذ على نفسه فيؤاخذ به في ماله ولا يسري على رفيقه مالم يصدقه. (ر ف ٤/٤، ۱/۸۰، ۷۱/۸۱، الحاشية، وسنوضحه قريباً أيضاً).

إن ما يثبت من الحقوق بالبينة أمام القاضي يعتبر هو الحقيقة الواقعية، بل يعتبر كالمشاهد بالعيان بمقتضى القاعدة السابقة؛ وحينئذ يحتج به غير المقضي عليه أيضاً.
99/81 - The evidence is a transitive proof and the confession is a limited proof. We said in explaining the previous rule: Indeed the evidence is the judicial proof that establishes. And the meaning of confession being a limited proof has also preceded on many occasions, such as a person who confesses to a debt shared between him and another, then this confession of his is executed upon himself, so he is held accountable in his wealth, and it does not extend to his companion unless he confirms him. Indeed what is established of rights by evidence before the judge is considered the actual truth, rather it is considered as if seen by the eye according to the previous rule; and then it is used as proof against others besides the one against whom it was ruled.
Source pages p. 990

Commentary
This passage explains the maxim that evidence is transitive and confession is limited. The author defines evidence as the judicial proof that establishes facts. He then gives an example of a shared debt: if one person confesses to a debt that is jointly owed, it only binds himself and does not affect his co-debtor unless the latter confirms. This illustrates the limited nature of confession. The passage then reaffirms that what is established by evidence is treated as absolute truth, like eyewitness, and can be used against others not party to the case, showing the transitive nature of evidence.

Key Terms
بينة (bayyinah): Judicial proof, evidence presented in court, typically two male witnesses in Hanafi law, but includes other proofs.
إقرار (iqrār): Confession, admission of a right against oneself.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a person confesses to a debt that is shared between him and another, what effect does this confession have on the other person?
A) Both are bound equally.
B) Only the confessor is bound.
C) The confession is invalid.
D) The other person must be called as witness.

Key Takeaway
Evidence is a binding proof that extends to all, while confession only binds the person who confesses.

Evidence on the Claimant, Oath on the Denier: The Foundational Qa'ida
Explaining the hadith-based rule of burden of proof and the oath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the qa'ida 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' to identify the burden of proof in a given case and determine when the defendant must swear an oath.

Core Idea
The burden of proof always lies with the one who claims a departure from the original state; the denier is presumed truthful.

This lesson unpacks the famous prophetic rule that governs judicial proceedings: the claimant must bring evidence, and the denier confirms his denial by oath. Rooted in a hadith, this principle balances the presumption of innocence with the need for proof. We will explore its definition, its proof text, and its standard applications.

Guided Path
1
Define the claimant and denier
Identify who is mudda'i (claimant) and who is munkir (denier) based on the status quo. The mudda'i asserts a change from the original state; the munkir upholds it.
2
Locate the dalil
The rule is directly from a prophetic hadith. Though not quoted verbatim here, it is the primary textual evidence. Recognize that this is a dalil naqli.
Hadith narrated by al-Bayhaqi and others.
3
Apply the burden
The claimant must bring evidence (bayyina). If he cannot, the denier's statement is accepted, but he must confirm it with an oath.
The oath is a deterrent against false denial.
4
Note the reason for the oath
The oath is required to strengthen the denier's position because of the possibility of lying. It is a default rule.
5
Acknowledge the general principle and exceptions
The rule is general: 'whoever has the say is subject to an oath.' But there are limited exceptions, explored in the next lesson.
The exception includes cases where the denier could lawfully create the claimed state immediately.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
101/81 - البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» (م/٧٦).

هذه القاعدة بنصها لفظ حديث نبوي كما تقدم (ر: ف ۲/۱۰). إن المدعي هو الذي يزعم خلاف الظاهر، فعليه الإثبات بالبينة، كما أفادته القاعدة الأساسية السابقة. أما المُدَّعى عليه فإنه بإنكاره متمسك بالحالة الأصلية وهي براءة ذمته من المسؤولية فيجب قبول قوله إلى أن يثبت شغل ذمته بسبب طارىء. ولكن لاحتمال كذب المدعى عليه في الإنكار يوثق قوله باليمين إذا طلب المدعي تحليفه عند عجزه عن الإثبات. وهذا مبدأ عام أن من كان القول له فهو خاضع لليمين إلا في مستثنيات محدودة (١).

(۱) من هذه المستثنيات ما إذا رجع الواهب في هبته وطلب القضاء له باستردادها، فزعم الموهوب له هلاك الموهوب، فالقول له في الهلاك بلا يمين (ر: قواعد الحمزاوي في مسائل الهبة والدر المختار باب الرجوع في الهبة). والمبدأ الفقهي في هذا الاستثناء : أن كل من ادعى أمراً يملك إنشاءه في الحال فالقول له فيه بلا يمين. فالشخص الموهوب له هنا يملك استهلاك الموهوب في الحال دون مسؤولية ما دام ملكاً له قبل القضاء برده إلى الواهب فلا فائدة من تحليفه إذ لو كان كاذباً في زعم الهلاك لاستطاع استهلاكه الآن بلا ضمان.
101/81 - Evidence is upon the claimant and the oath is upon the one who denies. This rule with its text is the wording of a prophetic hadith as previously mentioned. Indeed the claimant is the one who claims contrary to the apparent, so upon him is the proof by evidence, as the previous basic rule indicated. As for the defendant, by his denial he is clinging to the original state, which is the innocence of his liability, so his statement must be accepted until the burden of his liability is proven due to an incidental cause. But due to the possibility of the defendant lying in his denial, his statement is strengthened by the oath if the claimant requests him to swear when he is unable to provide proof. And this is a general principle that whoever has the say is subject to an oath, except in limited exceptions (1). (1) Among these exceptions is when a donor revokes his gift and seeks a ruling for its return, and the donee claims the gift has perished; then the donee's statement about the perishing is accepted without an oath. The fiqh principle in this exception: that whoever claims a matter that he is able to bring about instantly, his statement is accepted without an oath. The donee here is able to consume the gift instantly without liability as long as it is his property before the ruling to return it to the donor, so there is no benefit in making him swear, because if he were lying in his claim of perishing, he could consume it now without liability.
Source pages p. 991

Commentary
This passage explains the famous hadith-based rule that the burden of proof lies on the claimant, and the oath is on the denier. The author defines the claimant as one who asserts a departure from the apparent norm, thus requiring evidence. The defendant, by denying, is presumed innocent until proven otherwise. However, to guard against false denial, the defendant is made to swear an oath if the plaintiff cannot prove his case. The passage then provides an exception: in cases of gift revocation, if the donee claims the gift has perished, his statement is accepted without an oath because he could lawfully consume it at any time, rendering the oath pointless. This exception is based on the principle that one who can immediately create the claimed state is not required to swear.

Key Terms
مدعي (muddaʿī): Claimant; the person who asserts a claim that contradicts the apparent state.
منكر (munkir): Denier; the one who denies the claim and upholds the original state.
بينة (bayyina): Evidence, proof; typically witnesses or documents.
يمين (yamin): Oath sworn by the defendant to deny the claim.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, what is the default burden of proof?
A) The defendant must prove his innocence
B) The claimant must provide evidence
C) Both parties must provide evidence equally
D) The judge decides without evidence

Key Takeaway
The foundation of judicial proof: the claimant must bring evidence, the denier's oath suffices unless an exception based on immediate possibility applies.

Exception to the Oath Requirement: When the Claimant Can Immediately Create the Claimed State
Explains the exception to the oath rule based on the principle of immediate possibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where the denier's statement is accepted without oath due to the principle of immediate possibility, and apply the exception in similar scenarios.

Core Idea
If the claimant could lawfully bring about the claimed state instantly, the oath is pointless and thus waived.

This lesson examines the exception to the general rule that the denier must swear an oath. The classic example is the donee who claims the gift perished after revocation: his word is taken without oath because he could consume it legitimately at any time. We explore the rationale and its application to analogous cases.

Guided Path
1
Recall the general rule
Evidence on the claimant, oath on the denier. The denier normally must swear to strengthen his denial.
2
Identify the specific exception
When a donor revokes a gift and the donee claims it has perished, the donee's statement is accepted without oath.
This is found in Hamzawi's Qawa'id and al-Durr al-Mukhtar.
3
Understand the underlying principle
The principle: 'Whoever claims a matter that he is able to bring about instantly, his statement is accepted without oath.' The donee can lawfully consume the gift at any time, so an oath would not prevent lying.
The rationale is that the oath's deterrent effect is absent.
4
Apply the principle to other scenarios
Consider cases like a lessee claiming return of the leased item, or a borrower claiming repayment. The test is whether the person has immediate lawful power to create the claimed state.
The exception is narrow; it does not apply to claims of past events beyond immediate control.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Burden of Proof on Claimant and Its Exception
Maxim with exception and furu'

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-bayyina 'ala al-mudda'i wa al-yamin 'ala man ankara' and identify the exception where the claimant's statement is accepted without oath when he has the power to create the claimed matter immediately.

Core Idea
The claimant must prove; the defendant swears—unless the defendant could have created the claimed fact instantly.

This lesson unpacks a foundational Hanafi legal maxim rooted in Prophetic hadith and codified in the Majalla. The maxim establishes who bears the burden of proof in disputes. We will also explore a critical exception: when the claimant's ability to create the claimed matter immediately renders the oath unnecessary. The example of a donee claiming the gift perished in a revocation case illustrates the exception's logic.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its source
Identify the Arabic maxim and note that it is from a hadith. The learner should memorize the maxim as a key procedural rule.
The maxim is Majalla Article 76.
2
Define the claimant and defendant
The claimant (mudda'i) asserts what contradicts the apparent state; the defendant (mudda'a 'alayh) denies and adheres to the original state of innocence. Analyze how this definition determines the allocation of proof and oath.
The defendant's statement is accepted because of the presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimmah).
3
Explain the rule: evidence on claimant, oath on defendant
The claimant must bring evidence (bayyinah) to shift the presumption. The defendant must swear an oath if the claimant requests it, due to the possibility of lying. Understand the rationale: to weigh competing claims fairly.
The oath is takhlif (swearing by Allah).
4
Identify the exception: power to create immediately
If the claimant claims something he could instantaneously create (e.g., donee claims gift perished), his statement is accepted without oath. Reason: the oath would be pointless because he could make the claim true now if lying.
The exception is based on rational inference, not explicit hadith.
5
Apply the exception to the gift revocation case
Donor revokes gift; donee claims it perished. Since donee can consume the gift at any time before judgment, his claim of perishing is accepted without oath. This furu' demonstrates the dabt (restriction) of the maxim.
Sources: Qawa'id al-Hamzawi, al-Durr al-Mukhtar.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
سنرى قريباً في الفصل التالي معنى الظاهر، وخلاف الظاهر والأصل.
ومعنى هذه القاعدة يتضح من القاعدة التالية المتفرعة عنها :
ما يتفرع عنها من القواعد
١٠١/٨١ - «البينة على المدعي واليمين على من أنكر» (م/٧٦).
هذه القاعدة بنصها لفظ حديث نبوي كما تقدم (ر: ف ۲/۱۰).
إن المدعي هو الذي يزعم خلاف الظاهر، فعليه الإثبات بالبينة، كما أفادته القاعدة الأساسية السابقة.
أما المُدَّعى عليه فإنه بإنكاره متمسك بالحالة الأصلية وهي براءة ذمته من المسؤولية فيجب قبول قوله إلى أن يثبت شغل ذمته بسبب طارىء. ولكن لاحتمال كذب المدعى عليه في الإنكار يوثق قوله باليمين إذا طلب المدعي تحليفه عند عجزه عن الإثبات.
وهذا مبدأ عام أن من كان القول له فهو خاضع لليمين إلا في مستثنيات محدودة (۱).
(۱) من هذه المستثنيات ما إذا رجع الواهب في هبته وطلب القضاء له باستردادها، فزعم الموهوب له هلاك الموهوب، فالقول له في الهلاك بلا يمين (ر: قواعد الحمزاوي في مسائل الهبة والدر المختار باب الرجوع في الهبة). والمبدأ الفقهي في هذا الاستثناء : أن كل من ادعى أمراً يملك إنشاءه في الحال فالقول له فيه بلا يمين. فالشخص الموهوب له هنا يملك استهلاك الموهوب في الحال دون مسؤولية ما دام ملكاً له قبل القضاء برده إلى الواهب فلا فائدة من تحليفه إذ لو كان كاذباً في زعم الهلاك لاستطاع استهلاكه الآن بلا ضمان.
Chapter Ten: The Overall Legal Maxims in Fiqh. We will soon see in the following chapter the meaning of apparent (al-zahir), non-apparent, and the origin (al-asl). The meaning of this maxim becomes clear from the following maxim derived from it: What branches from it from the maxims: 101/81 - 'The burden of proof is on the claimant, and the oath is on the one who denies' (Majalla Article 76). This maxim in its text is the wording of a prophetic hadith as mentioned earlier (see: 2/10). Indeed, the claimant is the one who claims contrary to the apparent, so upon him is proof through evidence, as indicated by the previous fundamental maxim. As for the defendant, by his denial he adheres to the original state, which is the innocence of his liability, so his statement must be accepted until the burden of his liability due to an incidental cause is proven. But due to the possibility of the defendant's lying in denial, his statement is fortified by the oath if the claimant requests him to swear when he is unable to provide proof. This is a general principle: whoever's statement is accepted is subject to the oath except in limited exceptions (1). (1) Among these exceptions is when the donor revokes his gift and seeks judgment for its return, and the donee claims the gift perished, then his statement about the perishing is accepted without oath (see: Qawa'id al-Hamzawi on gift issues and al-Durr al-Mukhtar, chapter on revocation of gift). The legal principle in this exception is: whoever claims something that he has the power to create immediately, his statement is accepted therein without oath. The donee here has the power to consume the gift immediately without responsibility as long as it is his property before judgment to return it to the donor, so there is no benefit in making him swear, because if he were lying in claiming perishing, he could consume it now without liability.
Source pages p. 991

Commentary
This passage explains a fundamental legal maxim derived from Hadith. The author clarifies that the claimant contests the apparent state and thus must bring evidence. The defendant adheres to the original innocence, so his word is accepted, but he must take an oath if the claimant requests it due to suspicion of lying. The text then provides a Hanafi exception: in a gift revocation case, the donee's claim of perishing is accepted without oath because he had the power to consume the gift immediately, rendering the oath pointless. This exception is based on a principle of Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence that proves a claim, typically testimony or documentation.
المدعي (al-mudda'i): The claimant who asserts something contrary to the apparent state.
اليمين (al-yameen): An oath sworn by the defendant to confirm his denial.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi legal maxim, under what condition is the defendant's statement accepted without an oath?
A) When the defendant admits liability.
B) When the defendant claims something he has the power to create immediately.
C) When the claimant fails to produce evidence.
D) When the defendant swears voluntarily.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi rule 'burden of proof on claimant, oath on denier' has a critical exception: when the claimant can immediately create the claimed matter, no oath is needed.

No Proof with Contradiction: The Maxim and Its Application in Testimony
Explanation of a supplementary maxim on contradiction in judicial evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi maxim on contradiction in testimony and apply it to distinguish between pre-judgment and post-judgment scenarios.

Core Idea
Contradiction before judgment destroys proof; contradiction after judgment destroys the judge's ruling but not the judgment.

This lesson unpacks a key supplementary maxim from the Majalla that governs judicial evidence. The maxim addresses a practical dilemma: when a witness's statements contradict each other, when does the contradiction nullify the case, and when does it not? The answer depends on timing, and the distinction protects court proceedings from manipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the maxim and its source
Find the exact wording of the maxim in Majalla Article 80. Note that it consists of two parts: 'No proof with contradiction' and 'but the judge's ruling is invalidated by it.'
The first part is the main rule; the second part qualifies its application to judgments.
2
Define 'contradiction' (tanaqud)
According to the text, contradiction means two statements that negate each other. In this maxim, it specifically refers to the witness's testimony that establishes the claim.
Not all contradictions are considered; only those in supporting testimony.
3
Distinguish the two temporal scenarios
The passage contrasts contradiction before judgment (qada) with contradiction after judgment. Identify the legal effect for each.
Before judgment: testimony collapses. After judgment: judgment stands, witnesses compensate.
4
Note the condition for valid retraction
The text adds that retraction (ruju') is only effective before the judge. Outside the judge's council, it is disregarded.
This prevents out-of-court retractions from undermining judicial finality.
5
Understand the rationale for protecting the judgment
The passage explains that the judgment is not invalidated because it is impossible to be certain that the later contradictory statement is correct. Allowing invalidation would enable bribery and manipulation.
This is a policy consideration based on protecting the integrity of the judiciary.

۱۰۲/۸۱ - القاعدة المتممة الأربعين: لا حجة مع التناقض، لكن يختل معه حكم الحاكم (م/ ٨٠) .
التناقض هو أن يصدر عن الشخص كلامان ينقض أحدهما الآخر.
وللتناقض بحث كبير وأحكام مفصلة وتطبيقات وتفريعات واسعة النطاق في مباحث الدعوى والشهادة والإقرار من الكتب الفقهية. والتناقض المقصود في هذه القاعدة هو تناقض الشاهد في شهادته المثبتة للدعوى.
فإذا وقع التناقض في شهادة الشاهد قبل القضاء بها، كما لو شهد في دعوى الدين مثلاً أنَّ الدين قرض ثم قال: إنه ثمن مبيع، انهدم الاحتجاج بشهادته وامتنع القضاء بها.

102/81 - The fortieth supplementary maxim: No proof with contradiction, but the judge's ruling is invalidated by it (Majalla Article 80). Contradiction is that two statements issue from a person that negate each other. Contradiction has extensive research, detailed rulings, and wide-ranging applications and subdivisions in the topics of litigation, testimony, and confession from the books of fiqh. The intended contradiction in this maxim is the contradiction of the witness in his testimony that establishes the claim. If contradiction occurs in the testimony of a witness before judgment is passed on it, for example, if he testifies in a debt case that the debt was a loan, then says it was the price of a sale, then the argument by his testimony collapses and judgment on it is prevented. As for if contradiction appears in the evidence after judgment is passed on it, for example if witnesses retract their testimony, or admit to something that falsifies it after judgment, then the judgment that occurred is not invalidated, but the witnesses are liable to the defendant for what was adjudged against him. It is required for the witness's retraction that it be before the judge; if he shows retraction outside the judge's council, his retraction is not considered, neither before nor after judgment (Majalla Article 1731). The judgment is not invalidated because it cannot be determined with certainty that the second statement of the witnesses that contradicts their first testimony is correct rather than the first. If invalidation were allowed, many judgments could be annulled by bribing witnesses. Thus it is from the principles that judgment is protected from annulment as much as possible. 103/81 - Ended the classification and explanation of the ninety-nine maxims that preface the Majalla. And these last maxims (35-40) and what branches from them are the maxims of evidence and distribution of proof and oath between the litigants with regard to 'the origin' and 'the apparent' and 'contrary to the apparent', and about confession and its limitations, and evidence and its transmission, and what...
Source pages p. 992

Commentary
This passage explains a supplementary maxim about contradiction in judicial evidence. The author defines contradiction and clarifies that the maxim applies primarily to witness testimony. Two scenarios are distinguished: (1) If a witness contradicts himself before judgment, his testimony is rejected and judgment is prevented. (2) If witnesses retract after judgment, the judgment stands but the witnesses must compensate the defendant. The reason for not invalidating is to protect the integrity of judgments against manipulation. The passage ends with a summary of the final group of maxims from the Majalla, which deal with evidence and burden of proof.

Key Terms
التناقض (al-tanaqud): Contradiction: two statements from a person that negate each other.
رجوع الشهود (ruju' al-shuhud): Retraction of testimony by witnesses after judgment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi maxim on contradiction, what happens if a witness contradicts his testimony after the judge has already passed judgment?
A) The judgment is nullified.
B) The judgment stands, but the witness is liable for damages.
C) The witness must be punished for perjury.
D) The case is reopened.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi maxim protects judicial finality: contradiction before judgment destroys the proof, but contradiction after judgment leaves the judgment intact while holding the witness liable.

Expanding the Maxim: Beyond Literal Haste to Bad Faith
Critique and expansion of a legal maxim with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the expanded maxim to cases of bad faith even without literal haste, and identify when the maxim is operative.

Core Idea
The maxim applies to all actions with bad faith, not just literal haste.

The author critiques the Majalla's narrow formulation which restricts the maxim to cases of hastening with bad intent. In reality, the maxim covers any action with evil intent, even if the means are lawful. This lesson examines the proposed expansion and four illustrative examples from Maliki fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Traditional Formulation
Identify the original maxim as stated in the Majalla and its limitation to cases of 'hastening with bad intent' only.
The author observes that this narrows the scope significantly.
2
Grasp the Author’s Broader Scope
Appreciate that the author proposes including any action based on bad faith or evil intent, even if the means are lawful, as long as the goal is a forbidden result.
This is a methodological expansion based on the maxim's underlying principle.
3
Analyze the Maliki Examples
Work through each of the four examples: (1) divorce with intent to harm, (2) intercepting traders, (3) mudaraba with bad faith, (4) debtor’s waqf. See how each applies the maxim to treat the wrongdoer contrary to his intent.
These examples illustrate the broad application beyond literal haste.
4
Apply to New Cases
Consider how the expanded maxim would apply to modern scenarios involving abuse of rights or bad faith, such as a debtor transferring assets to evade repayment.
The key is the presence of bad faith, not the speed of action.

Appendix to Chapter Eighty-One: A New Formulation with Examples for the Maxim 'Whoever Hastens a Thing...'
104/1 - In the previous chapter we presented the twelfth maxim 'Whoever hastens a thing before its time is punished by deprivation thereof' (vol. 62/81) and explained it briefly.
Two observations are noted about this maxim: First, its traditional formulation as issued in the Majalla narrows its scope to bad intent connected with hastening, while the reality of the maxim is much broader, as it includes all actions based on bad faith and intent even if the person uses a legitimate means, as long as his goal is to reach a forbidden result.
The second observation about the maxim is the scarcity of examples mentioned under it in the commentaries of the Majalla. For this reason we will provide a number of additional examples and then propose a better and more general formulation for this maxim.
105/81 - The first example: Imam Malik holds that if a man divorces his wife, then takes her back before her 'iddah expires, then divorces her without touching her, and his intent thereby is to harm her by prolonging the 'iddah, she does not begin a new 'iddah but rather builds upon what has passed of her 'iddah.
106/81 - The second example: Imam Malik holds that if one who is prohibited from intercepting traders (see vol. 8/35) does not desist but returns to it, he is treated contrary to his intent (his intent being to draw profit to himself without the market people). So the goods he intercepted are sold, and if profit is made, the market people (i.e., the other sellers) share in the profit; if loss occurs, it is on him alone as a discipline.
This ruling is based on Malik's view that the wisdom behind the prohibition of intercepting traders is to prevent harm to the other sellers in the market.
107/81 - The third example: Among the established fiqh rulings in the contract of mudaraba (see vol. 14/46) is that the capital is not guaranteed by the working partner; rather it is in his hands as a trust. If profit occurs, he shares it with the capital owner according to their agreement; if loss occurs without transgression or negligence, the loss is entirely on the capital owner, and the worker loses his effort. But if the worker violates the contract conditions by buying goods prohibited from purchase, he becomes a guarantor of the capital as in a loan, so he takes profit, bears loss, and must return the capital to its owner.
However, Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) stated in the Muwatta' (in the narration of al-Laithi, Dar al-Nafa'is, Beirut) that if the worker in a mudaraba buys prohibited goods, intending thereby to make the capital guaranteed upon himself so that he alone takes the profit without his partner – if he does this with this intent, the capital owner has the choice: either to enter into the goods as a partner according to his share of profit per the contract, or he can take his capital as a guarantee from the transgressing worker.
This is treating the bad-faith mudarib contrary to his intent as a discipline.
108/81 - The fourth example: We previously mentioned (vol. 6/15 f) the fatwa of Mulla Abu al-Su'ud which was issued as a sultanic order regarding the non-enforcement of a debtors' waqf in the amount (1) Ibn Farhun al-Maliki: Durrat al-Ghawwas fi Muhadarat al-Khawass, ed. Muhammad Abu al-Ajfan and Uthman Bateekh, Cairo: Dar al-Turath, 1980, p. 245, issue no. 417.
Source pages p. 994

Commentary
This passage is the author's critical analysis and expansion of the well-known legal maxim 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by deprivation thereof' (من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه عوقب بحرمانه). The author points out two shortcomings in the traditional formulation from the Majalla: first, it restricts the maxim to cases of hastening with bad intent, whereas the maxim actually applies to any action with bad faith or evil intent, even if the means are technically lawful; second, the commentaries provide too few illustrative examples. To remedy this, the author proposes a broader formulation and supplies four examples from Maliki fiqh. The examples demonstrate the principle of treating the wrongdoer contrary to his intent: (1) a husband who divorces and returns only to harm his wife by prolonging her 'iddah – her 'iddah is not renewed; (2) a person who intercepts traders despite prohibition – he is forced to share profit with market sellers and bears all loss; (3) a mudarib who violates conditions to become guarantor and monopolize profit – the capital owner may choose to share profit or demand guaranteed capital; (4) a debtor's waqf that is invalidated when intended to defraud creditors. Although the examples are from the Maliki school, the author uses them to illustrate the general maxim's broad scope irrespective of school affiliation, highlighting the ethical principle of preventing abuse of rights.

Key Terms
الاستعجال (al-isti'jal): Hastening; seeking something before its proper time
سوء النية (su' al-niyya): Bad faith; evil intent in a legal act
بنقيض قصده (binaqid qasdihi): Contrary to his intent; treating the wrongdoer opposite to his aim as a deterrent

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the main defect in the traditional formulation of the maxim according to the author?
A) It is too broad and covers harmless actions.
B) It restricts the maxim to cases with bad intent related to haste, ignoring other forms of bad faith.
C) It only applies to divorce cases.
D) It lacks any textual evidence from the Quran or Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'من استعجل الشيء قبل أوانه' should be interpreted broadly to cover all actions with bad faith, not just literal haste.

The Maxim of Treating Bad Intention Contrary to Its Aim: Analysis and Reformulation
Critical refinement of a legal maxim with examples

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the principle of treating a person with bad intention contrary to his aim, identify its applications in Maliki and Hanafi fiqh, and evaluate the proposed reformulation.

Core Idea
Bad intention turns a lawful act against itself: the actor is treated contrary to his aim.

This passage introduces a key legal maxim and challenges its narrow formulation. Through examples from Maliki and Hanafi jurisprudence, the author shows that the real operative factor is bad intention (sū’ al-niyya), not merely hastening a right. He then proposes a broader formulation that protects the objectives of Sharia from manipulation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the original maxim
First, note the maxim 'Whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it.' Understand its literal meaning and the assumed condition: that a person prematurely seeks a legal right before its due time.
2
Study the examples
Examine the four examples: intercepting caravans, mudarabah violation, debtor's waqf, and concealing defects. For each, identify the actor's bad intention and the legal countermeasure.
The countermeasure always foils the intended benefit and imposes the opposite consequence.
3
Identify the common element
The author argues that all examples share bad intention, not literal hastening. Recognize that this common element justifies a broader principle.
The old maxim's wording is narrower than its intended scope.
4
Understand the madhhab dispute
In the seller-defect example, majority schools (non-Hanafis) give the buyer the option of defect despite a stipulation of immunity. The Hanafi position differs. Note the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence).
This shows how the principle of treating bad intention is applied across schools, though with variation.
5
Grasp the reformulation
Read the proposed new formulations: 'The bad-intentioned person in a disposition is treated contrary to his intention' or 'Whoever intends by his disposition an impermissible purpose is treated contrary to his intention.' Understand how this captures the spirit of the examples.
The reformulation removes the temporal restriction and focuses on intent.

Chapter Ten: Comprehensive Legal Maxims
The two riders (see: F 8/35, it did not end but returned to it), he is treated contrary to his intention (and his intention is to attract profit for himself without the people of the market, so the goods he intercepted are sold; if they profit, he shares the profit with the people of the market (i.e., the remaining sellers), and if they lose, the loss is upon him alone as a discipline).

107/81 - The Third Example: Among the established fiqh rulings in the contract of mudarabah (see: F 14/46) is that the capital is not guaranteed by the agent (mudarib), but rather it is in his hand as a trust. If there is profit, he divides the profit with the owner of capital according to their condition; if there is loss without transgression or negligence, the loss is entirely upon the owner of capital, and the agent loses his effort. If the agent violates the conditions of the contract by buying goods that were prohibited from being bought, then he becomes a guarantor of the capital as in a loan; he gets his profit and bears his loss, and he must return the capital to its owner.
But Malik (may Allah have mercy on him) stated in al-Muwatta' (in the narration of al-Laithiy, Dar al-Nafa'is, Beirut) that the agent in qirad (mudarabah) if he buys what is prohibited of goods, intending thereby that the capital become guaranteed so that he alone takes the profit without his partner: then if he does that with this intention, the owner of capital has the choice: if he wishes, he enters into the goods bought as a partner according to his share of profit per the contract; and if he prefers, he takes his capital as guaranteed against the transgressing agent.
And this is treatment of the bad-intentioned mudarib contrary to his intention as a discipline for him.
108/81 - The Fourth Example: We mentioned previously (F 6/15 h): the fatwa of al-Mawla Abu al-Su'ud which was issued by a sultanic decree that the waqf of a debtor does not take effect to the extent necessary to pay off the debt from his wealth, absolutely, due to what some debtors resort to by endowing their wealth to smuggle it from the creditors. And this, as you see, is treatment of the bad-intentioned debtor contrary to his intention.
This is in addition to other examples like the seller concealing a defect in the sold item while knowing it, with his stipulation on the buyer that he is free from every defect that appears in the sold item. We have stated that according to the majority of jurists (other than the Hanafis), the option of defect is established for the buyer, and the bad-intentioned seller's deceitful intention is repelled back to him.
109/81 - Proposed New Formulation for this Maxim: It is noted in the four preceding examples that in none of them is there a hastening of something before its legal time as depicted by the mentioned maxim. Rather, these examples, along with what preceded them in paragraph 62 of this chapter, all share the presence of bad intention on the part of the acting person, and the punishment is to repudiate his intention back to him; so fiqh necessitated treating him contrary to what he intended, as protection for the legal rulings from being manipulated by the legally responsible person, turning them away from their objectives for which they were legislated, and causing harm thereby to others. This, in reality, is the spirit of the maxim whose formulation was deficient and narrower than its comprehensive legislative goal. In light of this observation and the examples and evidence, we propose for this maxim one of the following two formulas:
"The bad-intentioned person in a disposition is treated contrary to his intention" or "Whoever intends by his disposition an impermissible purpose is treated contrary to his intention."
Source pages p. 995

Commentary
This passage is from a modern introduction to fiqh discussing legal maxims. The author begins by referencing the existing maxim 'whoever hastens something before its time is punished by being deprived of it' and shows through four examples that the actual operative principle is broader: it is about counteracting bad faith (sū’ al-niyya) by treating the actor contrary to his intent. The first example from Maliki school concerns intercepting caravans (talakkī al-rukbān): a merchant who intercepts goods before they reach the market to get all profit is forced to share profit with other sellers and bear all loss alone. The third example discusses mudarabah: if the agent intentionally violates restrictions to become guarantor and take all profit, Malik gives the capital owner the option to share or reclaim capital. The fourth example, from Hanafi practical fatwa, invalidates a debtor's waqf intended to evade creditors. The author notes that Hanafis differ from the majority in the example of concealing a defect with a stipulation of immunity—the majority (non-Hanafis) grant the buyer the option of defect. After reviewing these, the author proposes that the maxim be reformulated to: 'The bad-intentioned person in a disposition is treated contrary to his intention' or 'Whoever intends by his disposition an impermissible purpose is treated contrary to his intention.' This shift from a narrow literal reading to a principle based on intent and maqasid is a characteristic of modern fiqh writing.

Key Terms
سوء النية (sū’ al-niyya): The intention to achieve an impermissible objective through a lawful form, such as profiting alone or evading debts.
يعامل بنقيض قصده (yu‘āmalu bi-naqīḍi qaṣdihi): The legal treatment that foils the actor's bad intention by applying the opposite consequence of what he aimed for.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the common element shared by all the examples in the passage that justifies application of the principle 'treated contrary to his intention'?
A) The presence of a contractual stipulation
B) The presence of bad intention (sū’ al-niyya)
C) The presence of a hurry to obtain profit
D) The presence of a loss to others

Key Takeaway
The maxim against hastening is actually a specific application of a broader principle: any disposition tainted by bad intention is foiled by treating the actor contrary to his aim.

The Original State (Al-Asl) as a Primary Preferrer
Defines al-asl and its sub-maxims in evidentiary methodology

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle of al-asl to determine presumptions in legal disputes, distinguishing between transient and original matters.

Core Idea
In legal uncertainty, the original state presumes non-existence for transient matters and existence for original matters.

The first primary preferrer in Hanafi evidentiary rules is al-asl (the original state). It functions as a default assumption that requires no specific proof. This lesson unpacks its definition, the sub-maxim distinguishing transient from original matters, and how it resolves disputes over contract validity, liability, and defects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the type of matter
Determine whether the disputed issue is transient (ʿāriḍa) or original (aṣliyya). Transient matters are those that may arise and disappear, like debt or defect; original matters are inherent states like health or existence.
This classification determines which presumption applies.
2
Apply the appropriate presumption
For transient matters, presume non-existence (e.g., no debt). For original matters, presume existence (e.g., a contract is valid if its existence is agreed).
The maxim is: 'الأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم، وفي الأمور الأصلية الوجود'.
3
Check for stronger evidence
Al-asl is a 'weak apparent' which can be overridden by a stronger proof (qawi). If such proof exists, the presumption is discarded.
This is why it is called a 'primary preferrer'—it tips the scale only in the absence of stronger evidence.
4
Apply to subsidiary rules
Sub-presumptions like baraʾat al-dhimma (innocence of liability) and freedom from defects stem from this maxim. Use them in relevant cases.
These are branches (shuʿab) of the same origin.

Source Text

فالحالة التي تسمى «الظاهر» هي في نظر الفقهاء نوعان: ۱ - ظاهر ضعيف لم يطرح معه احتمال خلافه كما سبق بيانه من أصل أو دلالة حال وهو المراد هنا بالمرجحات الابتدائية .
۲ - وظاهر قوي، وسيأتي الكلام عليه.
وأمثلة دلالة الحال كثيرة، وهي المعروفة بمسائل تحكيم الحال. وذلك كما لو زعم مستأجر الطاحون بعد انقضاء الإجارة أن الماء قد انقطع فتعطلت الرحى عن العمل وطلب إسقاط الأجرة، وزعم المؤجر عدم الانقطاع ولا بينة فحينئذ يلتجىء القضاء إلى تحكيم الحال لترجيح أحد الزعمين : فإذا كان الماء منقطعاً حين الخصومة يترجح زعم المستأجر بالنسبة إلى الماضي وإذا كان الماء جارياً حين الخصومة كان ظاهر الحال عدم الانقطاع بعد اتفاقهما على أنه كان جارياً عند العقد والتسليم، فيترجح زعم المؤجر ولا يسقط شيء من الأجرة حتى يثبت المستأجر الانقطاع (م/ (۱) (۱۷۷۶)
- وكذا لو أنفق الأب على نفسه من مال ابنه الذي عنده ثم اختلفا فزعم الأب أنه أنفق وهو معسر مستحق للنفقة، فلا ضمان عليه، وزعم الابن أنه أنفق وهو موسر.
(1) أما إذا اتفقا على انقطاع الماء واختلفا في مقدار مدته فالقول للمستأجر بيمينه في زعم زيادة مدة الانقطاع.
The Tenth Chapter: Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh. And these evidences are of two types: primary preferrers and establishers. The first type: primary preferrers. So the primary preferrers are two things: 1- the original state, and 2- the indication of the situation. Both are called the weak apparent, meaning that which has not reached a degree of apparentness where the possibility of its opposite is discarded. The clarification of that is as follows: Section 82 – The Original State: Linguistically, original state is the lower part of a thing and its foundation. And in the terminology of proof scholars: the original state is the general condition that is like an observed law initially, without need for a specific proof for it, rather it is considered accepted by itself. And some define original state as: that upon which others are built and it is not built upon other than itself. Examples of that in Sharia are many: including their statement: 'The original state in transient matters is non-existence, and in original matters is existence.' From the branches of this original state are many subsidiary origins, including that 'the original state is the innocence of the liability', and that the original state is the freedom of the sold item from defects and the like, as has preceded. And also their statement: The original state when there is dispute about a contract's invalidity and validity is that it is invalid, and when there is dispute about its corruption and validity is that it is considered valid. That is because the dispute about invalidity means contesting the existence of the contract and its non-existence, and non-existence is the previous accustomed [state]. As for the dispute about validity and corruption, its meaning is challenging the soundness of the contract from defects after acknowledging its existence and formation. And the attribute of soundness from defects is the natural original condition in existing things, as has been explained previously. Section (82) An analytical view in rules of evidence. And also their statement: The original state is attributing an event to its nearest time, and the original state is the continuation of what was as it was, and so forth, like many of the preceding rules considered as origins in Sharia, and among them are those that are derived from a more general origin and fall within its scope. Section 3/82 – The Indication of the Situation: As for the indication of the situation, it is the existing indication that points to something. So the condition called 'the apparent' is in the view of the jurists of two types: 1- A weak apparent with which the possibility of its opposite is not discarded, as has been explained before from either origin or indication of the situation, and this is what is meant here by primary preferrers. 2- A strong apparent, and discussion of it will come. Examples of indication of the situation are many, and they are known as issues of arbitrating the situation. Such as if the tenant of a mill after the end of the lease claims that water stopped and the millstone stopped working and demands dropping the rent, and the lessor claims that there was no stoppage and there is no evidence; then the court resorts to arbitrating the situation to prefer one of the two claims: if the water is stopped at the time of dispute, the tenant's claim about the past is preferred; if the water is flowing at the time of dispute, the apparent situation is that there was no stoppage after they agreed that it was flowing at the time of contract and delivery, so the lessor's claim is preferred and nothing of the rent is dropped unless the tenant proves the stoppage (Majalla article 1776). Also, if a father spends on himself from his son's money that he has, then they dispute: the father claims that he spent while being insolvent and entitled to maintenance, so there is no liability; the son claims that he spent while being wealthy. (1) If they agree on the stoppage of water and differ on its duration, the tenant's statement with his oath is accepted regarding the claim of longer stoppage duration.
Source pages p. 998

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm introduces the first type of evidentiary source: primary preferrers, which are two: al-asl (original state) and dalalat al-hal (indication of the situation). Both are termed 'weak apparent' because they are prima facie evidence that does not eliminate the possibility of a contrary proof. The author then defines al-asl: linguistically 'foundation', and in the terminology of proof scholars, a general condition accepted without specific proof. Examples: the maxim 'origin in transient matters is non-existence, in original matters existence' — this is the basis for the presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimma) and the presumption that a sold item is defect-free. Another application: in a dispute over contract invalidity (batal) the presumption is against existence; in a dispute over corruption versus validity (fasad), the presumption favors validity because existence is accepted and soundness is the natural state. The original state also includes attributing events to the nearest time and continuity of prior states. Next, dalalat al-hal is an indicator from the current situation. The author illustrates with a case from the Majalla: a mill tenant and lessor dispute whether water was cut off. The judge uses the observable situation at the time of litigation to decide: if water is currently cut off, the tenant's claim for past stoppage is preferred; if water is flowing, the lessor's claim prevails. A variation: if they agree water was cut but dispute duration, the tenant's claim is accepted with oath because he is denying liability for a longer period. These principles are fundamental in Hanafi evidentiary methodology for resolving factual uncertainties.

Key Terms
المرجحات الابتدائية (al-murajjihat al-ibtidaiyyah): Primary preferrers – initial evidentiary sources (original state and indication of the situation) that tip the scale in the absence of stronger evidence.
الأصل (al-asl): The general condition accepted without specific proof; a presumptive state.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a dispute over whether a contract is valid or corrupt (fasad), with both parties agreeing the contract was signed, what presumption does al-asl provide?
A) The contract is invalid because the original state is non-existence
B) The contract is valid because the original state for existing things is soundness
C) The contract is corrupt until proven otherwise
D) No presumption applies; evidence is required

Key Takeaway
Al-asl provides a default presumption: transient matters are presumed non-existent, original matters are presumed existent, shifting the burden of proof accordingly.

Furuat al-Qawaid — Derived Applications
intermediate
8 min
Indication of the Situation (Dalalat al-Hal) as a Primary Preferrer
Defines dalalat al-hal and provides examples from lease and maintenance disputes

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and apply the indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal) to resolve factual disputes when direct evidence is absent.

Core Idea
The current observable situation serves as an indicator to tip the scale in evidentiary conflicts.

The second primary preferrer is dalalat al-hal, the indication derived from the current observable situation. Like al-asl, it is a 'weak apparent' that can be overcome by stronger evidence. This lesson explains its definition and illustrates its application through the classic mill rental dispute and the father's spending case.

Guided Path
1
Identify the disputed fact
Determine the specific fact that requires evidentiary preference, e.g., whether water was cut off or whether a father was insolvent when spending.
The fact is often about a past event that cannot be directly proven.
2
Observe the current situation
Look at the present condition that is relevant to the disputed fact. For example, is water currently flowing or cut? Is the father currently wealthy or poor?
The situation must be observable and agreed upon by both parties.
3
Extrapolate from present to past
Assume that the present state reflects the likely past state due to the continuity of circumstances, unless proven otherwise.
This is based on the principle that conditions tend to persist (istishab).
4
Assign preference accordingly
The party whose claim aligns with the current situation receives the benefit of the doubt, shifting the burden to the opposing party.
If the current situation changes after the dispute, the analysis may differ; the timing of observation matters.

Source Text

(1) أما إذا اتفقا على انقطاع الماء واختلفا في مقدار مدته فالقول للمستأجر بيمينه في زعم زيادة مدة الانقطاع.
The Tenth Chapter: Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh. And these evidences are of two types: primary preferrers and establishers. The first type: primary preferrers. So the condition called 'the apparent' is in the view of the jurists of two types: 1- A weak apparent with which the possibility of its opposite is not discarded, as has been explained before from either origin or indication of the situation, and this is what is meant here by primary preferrers. 2- A strong apparent, and discussion of it will come. Examples of indication of the situation are many, and they are known as issues of arbitrating the situation. Such as if the tenant of a mill after the end of the lease claims that water stopped and the millstone stopped working and demands dropping the rent, and the lessor claims that there was no stoppage and there is no evidence; then the court resorts to arbitrating the situation to prefer one of the two claims: if the water is stopped at the time of dispute, the tenant's claim about the past is preferred; if the water is flowing at the time of dispute, the apparent situation is that there was no stoppage after they agreed that it was flowing at the time of contract and delivery, so the lessor's claim is preferred and nothing of the rent is dropped unless the tenant proves the stoppage (Majalla article 1776). Also, if a father spends on himself from his son's money that he has, then they dispute: the father claims that he spent while being insolvent and entitled to maintenance, so there is no liability; the son claims that he spent while being wealthy. (1) If they agree on the stoppage of water and differ on its duration, the tenant's statement with his oath is accepted regarding the claim of longer stoppage duration.
Source pages p. 998

Commentary
This passage from Al-Madkhal al-Fiqhi al-'Amm introduces the first type of evidentiary source: primary preferrers, which are two: al-asl (original state) and dalalat al-hal (indication of the situation). Both are termed 'weak apparent' because they are prima facie evidence that does not eliminate the possibility of a contrary proof. The author then defines al-asl: linguistically 'foundation', and in the terminology of proof scholars, a general condition accepted without specific proof. Examples: the maxim 'origin in transient matters is non-existence, in original matters existence' — this is the basis for the presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimma) and the presumption that a sold item is defect-free. Another application: in a dispute over contract invalidity (batal) the presumption is against existence; in a dispute over corruption versus validity (fasad), the presumption favors validity because existence is accepted and soundness is the natural state. The original state also includes attributing events to the nearest time and continuity of prior states. Next, dalalat al-hal is an indicator from the current situation. The author illustrates with a case from the Majalla: a mill tenant and lessor dispute whether water was cut off. The judge uses the observable situation at the time of litigation to decide: if water is currently cut off, the tenant's claim for past stoppage is preferred; if water is flowing, the lessor's claim prevails. A variation: if they agree water was cut but dispute duration, the tenant's claim is accepted with oath because he is denying liability for a longer period. These principles are fundamental in Hanafi evidentiary methodology for resolving factual uncertainties.

Key Terms
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): An indicator derived from the observable situation that points to a fact.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mill dispute, if water is flowing at the time of litigation, which party's claim is preferred?
A) The tenant's claim that water was cut off
B) The lessor's claim that water was not cut off
C) Both claims are rejected
D) The judge must request evidence from both

Key Takeaway
Dalalat al-hal allows a judge or mufti to use the current observable situation as a weak indicator to resolve factual disputes in the absence of direct evidence.

The Principle of Apparent Evidence: Defense, Not Entitlement
Explains types of apparent evidence and the maxim governing their use in initial preference.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify cases where weak apparent evidence serves as an initial preponderator and apply the principle that such evidence is valid for defense but not for establishing entitlement.

Core Idea
Weak apparent evidence preserves the status quo but cannot create rights: it is a shield, not a sword.

This passage from Mustafa al-Zarqa's analysis of evidentiary rules introduces a foundational Hanafi maxim: apparent evidence (al-amr al-zahir) suffices for defense but not for establishing a right. We will examine the two types of apparent evidence—weak and strong—and explore how weak apparent, derived from default principles or circumstantial indications, functions as an initial preponderator in judicial disputes. Practical examples from the mill tenant, father's spending, and the missing person case illustrate the maxim's operation and its limits.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish weak and strong apparent
Note that 'al-zahir' in fiqh has two types: weak (where possibility of opposite remains) and strong. The weak is what serves as initial preponderator; it includes both default principles (asl) and circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal).
2
Understand the function of weak apparent
The key maxim: 'al-amr al-zahir yasluhu li al-daf' la li al-istihqaq' — apparent evidence is valid for defense (repelling a claim) but not for entitlement (establishing a right). This means it can maintain the status quo but cannot be used to claim something from another.
3
Analyze the examples given
Work through the mill tenant case and the father's spending case. In each, identify what the apparent evidence (current state of water or father's wealth) indicates, and how it gives initial preponderance to one party's claim, subject to contrary proof.
4
Apply to the missing person (mafqud)
The missing person is presumed alive (istishab) to prevent heirs from taking his estate (defense), but this presumption cannot allow him to inherit from someone who dies after his disappearance (entitlement). His share is suspended — a clear application of the maxim.
5
Note the exceptions and boundaries
The text mentions exceptions exist in some furu' branches. Reflect that the maxim is not absolute; certain cases may override it. Identify the distinction between cases with circumstantial indicators (dalalat al-hal) and those without (e.g., simple debt claim), where default principles like 'original innocence' apply instead.

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the concept of 'indication of the circumstances' (dalalat al-hal) and its role as an initial preponderator in judicial disputes. The author, Mustafa Ahmad al-Zarqa, explains that the term 'apparent' (al-zahir) in juristic usage has two types: weak apparent (which does not eliminate the possibility of the opposite) and strong apparent. The weak apparent is what serves as an initial preponderator, and it may be derived either from a default principle (asl) or from circumstantial evidence (dalalat al-hal). Several examples illustrate this: the case of the mill tenant where the court assesses whether water is currently flowing to decide past claims, and the case of a father spending from his son's property where the father's current financial state is considered to determine whether he was entitled to spend. The author distinguishes such cases from those lacking circumstantial indicators, such as a simple debt claim, where default principles like original innocence and attributing events to the nearest time are used. The key principle enunciated is that 'apparent evidence is valid for defense, not for entitlement'—it can maintain a current legal status or repel a claim, but cannot itself establish a right against another. The application to the missing person (mafqud) is classic: the default is that he is alive (istishab), so his heirs cannot take his estate, but he cannot inherit from someone who died after his disappearance; his share is suspended. The author notes exceptions to this principle without detailing them.

Key Terms
دلالة الحال (dalālat al-ḥāl): The existing indicator that points to something; circumstantial evidence derived from the situation itself.
الظاهر الضعيف (al-ẓāhir al-ḍaʿīf): A type of apparent evidence where the possibility of its opposite is not eliminated; used as an initial preponderator.
المرجحات الابتدائية (al-murajjiḥāt al-ibtidāʾiyya): Initial preponderators that give weight to one claim over another in the absence of conclusive proof.
حجة الدفع (ḥujjat al-dafʿ): Evidence that is valid for repelling a claim or preserving the status quo, but not for establishing a right.
حجة الاستحقاق (ḥujjat al-istiḥqāq): Evidence that establishes a substantive right or claim against another party.

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) The opinion of a mill expert

Key Takeaway
Weak apparent evidence (including dalalat al-hal and default principles) serves to defend a legal status or repel claims, but cannot establish a new right; it is a shield, not a sword.
