The Maxim: Apparent Evidence for Defense, Not Entitlement
Explaining the legal maxim that الظاهر is a proof for defense but not for entitlement

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-amr al-zahir yaslahu hujjatan lil-daf' la lil-istihqaq' to distinguish cases where apparent evidence maintains the status quo versus where it establishes a claim, and identify the missing person case as a key application.

Core Idea
The apparent is enough to maintain the status quo but not to claim a right from another.

This passage introduces a foundational Hanafi legal maxim that governs the evidentiary weight of apparent indications (al-zahir) in judicial settings. It clarifies that such evidence can defend an existing legal state but cannot establish a new entitlement. The missing person case exemplifies the principle and its limits, while the maxim notes exceptions that further refine its application.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its components
Memorize the maxim in Arabic: 'الأمر الظاهر يصلح حجة للدفع لا للاستحقاق'. Understand that it distinguishes between two legal functions: daf' (defense) and istihqaq (entitlement).
This maxim is a sughra (minor) legal maxim in the Hanafi tradition.
2
Identify the two types of weak apparent evidence (al-zahir al-da'if)
The text mentions two sources: (1) an established principle (asl), like presumption of innocence; (2) an indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal), such as observed conduct. Both provide initial weight but not conclusive proof.
These are used as initial weighting tools (tarjih) when definitive proof is absent.
3
Apply the maxim to the missing person (al-mafqud)
The missing person is presumed alive based on the apparent (istishab). This prevents heirs from distributing his estate (defense of his presumed right). However, this presumption is insufficient for him to inherit from others (no entitlement), so his share is suspended.
The suspension protects both possibilities until death is confirmed.
4
Note the exceptions to the maxim
Some Hanafi jurists allowed entitlement based on apparent evidence in specific branches, as referenced by Ibn Nujaym. This shows the maxim is not absolute and must be applied with knowledge of its exceptions.
The exceptions are recorded in al-Ashbah and require careful study.
5
Connect the maxim to related principles
The maxim parallels 'Certainty is not removed by doubt' (اليقين لا يزول بالشك). Both protect the status quo until stronger evidence arises.
This connection reinforces the conservative approach of Hanafi evidence law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Chapter Ten: The Universal Legal Maxims in Fiqh. Then one resorts to judging the state of the father in wealth and poverty at the time of litigation, so the judge weighs based on that the claim of the one supported by the situation, as an initial weighting until the contrary is proven (see Radd al-Muhtar 2/685, end of Nafaqat). And the juristic consideration here is that in such matters there exist circumstantial indications that have a type of indication, so they are resorted to initially when definitive proof is absent. Unlike matters without such indications, like a claim of debt, where when evidence is lacking, one resorts to those principles for initial weighting, such as the principle of original innocence of liability, and attributing an incident to its nearest time, and others. 4/82 - These are the initial weighted preponderances by which the claim of one litigant is preferred over the other, namely: the weak apparent evidence (al-zahir al-da'if) in its two types, from a principle or an indication of the situation. - And it is established in fiqh that the apparent matter (al-amr al-zahir) is valid as a proof for defense, not for entitlement. So it is sufficient evidence to maintain an existing legal status and to repel someone who claims contrary to it without proof. But the apparent is not sufficient proof for entitlement against another. On this basis, rulings are derived from various chapters, including rulings on the missing person (al-mafqud). So the missing person is considered alive based on the apparent situation, which is the presumption of continuity of his previously certain life. That is because the principle is that what was remains as it was, as previously stated. This suffices to prevent his heirs from claiming his estate before his death is established, but it does not suffice for him to inherit from others if one of his relatives dies after his disappearance; rather, his inheritance share is suspended: if he returns alive he takes it, otherwise it is returned to the heirs who would have been entitled on the assumption that the missing person was dead at the time of his relative's death. However, this principle has exceptions in some jurisprudential branches where the jurists ruled for entitlement based on the indication of the apparent (see al-Ashbah by Ibn Nujaym 1/104).
Source pages p. 1000

Commentary
This passage from Chapter Ten on universal legal maxims explains the nature of initial presumptions (al-zahir) in legal evidence. The author first clarifies that when no definitive proof is available, a qadi may resort to circumstantial indications or legal principles (such as original innocence) as a preliminary weighting. The two types of weak apparent evidence are highlighted: one derived from an established principle (asl) and the other from an indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal). The core legal maxim is then stated: the apparent (al-amr al-zahir) is valid as a proof for defense (daf') but not for entitlement (istihqaq). This means it can maintain the status quo and repel claims, but cannot establish a right against another party. The missing person (mafqud) is presented as a key application: the presumption of continued life prevents his heirs from distributing his estate, but he cannot inherit from others; his share is suspended. The passage concludes by noting exceptions where some jurists ruled for entitlement based on apparent indications, as recorded in Ibn Nujaym's al-Ashbah.

Key Terms
الظاهر (al-zahir): The apparent or manifest indication that provides a preliminary weight in favor of one litigant, derived from either a legal principle (asl) or an indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal).
الدفع (al-daf'): Defense or repelling a claim, maintaining the existing legal status without establishing a new right.
الاستحقاق (al-istihqaq): Entitlement or right to claim ownership or inheritance from another party.
المفقود (al-mafqud): A person who has disappeared and whose life or death is unknown, subject to specific legal rules.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He inherits immediately because he is presumed alive.
B) He does not inherit at all; his share is given to the other heirs.
C) His inheritance share is suspended; he takes it if he returns alive, otherwise it is returned to the heirs who would have inherited if he were dead.
D) His share is distributed to the state until his return.

Key Takeaway
The maxim 'al-amr al-zahir yaslahu hujjatan lil-daf' la lil-istihqaq' protects existing legal states but cannot create new claims, as shown by the missing person's suspended inheritance.

Resolving Conflicting Evidence: Rules of Preference
Explaining rules for prioritizing conflicting evidence in judicial proceedings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules of preference (tarjih) to resolve conflicts between presumptions (usul) and indications of the situation (dalalat al-hal) in judicial proceedings.

Core Idea
When evidence conflicts, the presumption closest to the dispute's essence prevails; indications of the situation always override presumptions.

This lesson unpacks a key maxim from Hanafi usul al-iftāʾ: how a muftī or judge prioritizes conflicting pieces of evidence. You'll learn to distinguish between a legal presumption (asl) and an indication of the situation (dalālat al-ḥāl), and how the presumption of innocence (barāʾat al-dhimmah) often outweighs other presumptions because it speaks to the core of a dispute. Through concrete examples from deposits and maintenance, you'll see how this framework is applied in real fatwā practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of probative evidence
The passage lists four types: confession (iqrār), refusal to take an oath (nukūl), clear evidence (bayyinah) including testimony and conclusive circumstantial evidence, and strong apparent evidence (al-ẓāhir al-qawī). Recognize these as the building blocks.
The fourth type is treated as equivalent to conclusive circumstantial evidence.
2
Analyze conflict between two presumptions (aṣlān)
When two presumptions conflict—e.g., the presumption of innocence (non-liability) vs. the presumption that a contingent matter (like return of deposit) is absent—the presumption more connected to the dispute's essence is given weight. In the depositary example, barāʾat al-dhimmah prevails because the core is liability, not the physical return.
The rule 'al-amīn muṣaddaq' (trustee is believed) also supports the depositary.
3
Analyze conflict between a presumption and an indication of the situation (dalālat al-ḥāl)
The indication of the situation always overrides a presumption because it provides direct, observable clues that change the original state. Example: father's current wealth/poverty at litigation indicates his past condition, overriding the presumption of poverty.
This reflects the maxim 'al-ʿibrah li al-maqāṣid wa al-maʿānī'.
4
Apply the rule to the given furūʿ
Work through the two subsidiary cases: 1) Depositary vs. owner – preference to depositary due to essence. 2) Father vs. son – preference based on current wealth as indication. Trace the reasoning step-by-step.
Use the qawīda panel for the full application.

الفصل (۸۲) نظرة تحليلية في قواعد الإثبات

النوع الثاني : المثبتات

٥/٨٢ - أما المثبتات فأربعة أقسام متفاوتة الدرجات في القوة وهي:
۱ - الإقرار .
۲ - النكول : أي استنكاف الخصم عن حلف اليمين الموجهة عليه من القاضي .
٣ - البينة بأنواعها الثلاثة المتقدمة وهي البينة الشخصية (أي الشهادة)، والبينة الخطية، والقرينة القاطعة .
٤ - الظاهر القوي الذي سنتكلم عنه، وهو في قوة القرينة القاطعة .

إذا تبين ذلك نقول : قد تتعدد الأدلة المرجحة في القضية الواحدة، وتتعارض، فيكون بعضها شاهداً لأحد الخصمين وبعضها للآخر. فالنظر الفقهي عندئذ في الترجيح يقوم على التفصيل التالي الذي تدل عليه فروع الأحكام :

٦/٨٢ - تعارض الأدلة القضائية :

أولاً : إذا تعارضت المرجحات الابتدائية بعضها مع بعض، بأن كان أحد المتداعيين يستند إلى أحدها والآخر يستند إلى غيره ففي الترجيح تفصيل :

أ - فإن كان التعارض بين أصلين فإنه يرجح اعتبار الأصل الذي هو أكثر تعلقاً بموضوع النزاع وجوهره أو تؤيده قواعد أخرى في موضوع القضية . فمثلاً إذا تعارض أصل براءة الذمة مع غيره يرجح اعتبار براءة الذمة، لأنها أكثر قوة وثبوتاً.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه

مثال ذلك: ما لو ادعى الوديع ردَّ الوديعة، وأنكر المالك المودع ردها إليه ولا بينة فالقول الراجح قول الوديع، لأنَّ جوهر هذا النزاع يرمي إلى تضمين الوديع قيمة الوديعة بزعم أنه جحدها أو إلى عدم تضمينه بزعم ردها، وإن كان ظاهر النزاع منصباً على رد وديعة أو عدمه.

فبالنظر إلى جوهر النزاع، وباعتبار أنَّ العبرة للمقاصد والمعاني لا للألفاظ، يشهد للوديع أصل براءة الذمة. وبالنظر إلى الصورة الظاهرة يشهد للمودع أنّ الأصل عدم الرد لكونه عارضاً، والأصل في الأمور العارضة العدم. فيرجح اعتبار أصل براءة الذمة هنا، لأنه هو الذي يتعلق به جوهر النزاع، ولأنه تؤيده أيضاً قاعدة كون الأمين مصدقاً في قوله، حتى يثبت تعديه أو تقصيره .

وهذا يفسر لنا أن كثيراً من الفروع المستثناة من بعض القواعد المتقدمة إنما استثنيت منها لأنها أجدر بالتفريع على قاعدة أخرى ..

ب - وإن كان التعارض بين الأصل ودلالة الحال ترجح دلالة الحال دائماً لأنها قرائن وشواهد قائمة تنبىء بحدوث أمر يغير حالة الأصل. فيكون بمثابة دليل على كذب تمسك من يتمسك بذلك الأصل.

مثال ذلك: فرع اختلاف الأب والابن في يسار الأب وإعساره حين إنفاقه على نفسه من مال ابنه كما تقدم. فإنه إذا كان الأب حين الخصومة موسِراً كان هذا أمارة ظاهرة على صحة دعوى الابن ويكون القول له في أنَّ أباه كان موسراً، فيضمن الأب ما أنفق على نفسه من مال ابنه أن الأصل عدم اليسار. وإذا كان الأب حين الخصومة معسراً كان الأمر بالعكس، أي أنه يكون القول للأب عملاً بالأصل، لأن الغنى أمر عارض.
Chapter (82) Analytical View of Rules of Evidence. Second Type: Probative Evidence. 5/82 - The probative evidence are four categories of varying degrees of strength: 1 - Confession. 2 - Refusal: i.e., the opponent's abstention from taking the oath directed to him by the judge. 3 - Clear evidence (bayyinah) in its three types mentioned: personal evidence (i.e., testimony), written evidence, and conclusive circumstantial evidence. 4 - Strong apparent evidence (al-zahir al-qawi) which we will discuss, and it is as strong as conclusive circumstantial evidence. When this is clear, we say: There may be multiple preferential evidences in a single case, and they may conflict, so some may be in favor of one litigant and some in favor of the other. At that point, juristic consideration in preference is based on the following detail indicated by the subsidiary rulings: 6/82 - Conflict of Judicial Evidence. First: If the initial preferential factors conflict with each other, such that one litigant relies on one and the other relies on another, then in preference there is detail: A - If the conflict is between two presumptions (asl), then weight is given to the presumption that is more connected to the subject and essence of the dispute or is supported by other rules in the matter. For example, if the presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimmah) conflicts with another, the presumption of innocence is given weight because it is stronger and more established. Chapter Ten: Universal Rules of Jurisprudence. Example: If the depositary (wadi') claims to have returned the deposit, and the owner denies its return, and there is no evidence, then the preferred statement is that of the depositary, because the essence of this dispute aims at imposing liability on the depositary for the value of the deposit on the claim that he denied it, or at absolving him on the claim of return, even though the outward appearance of the dispute concerns the return or non-return of the deposit. So by looking at the essence of the dispute, and considering that consideration is given to objectives and meanings, not words, the presumption of innocence supports the depositary. And by looking at the outward appearance, the presumption of non-return (since it is contingent, and the presumption regarding contingent matters is non-existence) supports the owner. So the presumption of innocence is given weight here because it relates to the essence of the dispute, and also because it is supported by the rule that a trustee is believed in his statement until his transgression or negligence is proven. And this explains to us that many subsidiary cases excepted from some preceding rules are only excepted from them because they are more appropriate to be derived under another rule. B - And if the conflict is between a presumption (asl) and an indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal), the indication of the situation always prevails because it consists of existing clues and evidence that indicate the occurrence of something that changes the state of the presumption. So it serves as evidence of the falsity of the adherence of one who adheres to that presumption. Example: The subsidiary case of disagreement between the father and the son regarding the father's wealth and poverty when he spent on himself from his son's wealth, as previously mentioned. If the father at the time of litigation is wealthy, this is a manifest indication of the correctness of the son's claim, and the son's statement is taken that his father was wealthy, so the father is liable for what he spent on himself from his son's wealth, even though the presumption is absence of wealth. If the father at the time of litigation is poor, the opposite applies, i.e., the father's statement is taken acting upon the presumption, because wealth is a contingent matter.
Source pages p. 1001

Commentary
This passage provides an analytical overview of the rules of evidence (qawa'id al-ithbat) in Islamic jurisprudence, specifically focusing on the types of probative evidence (al-muthbitat) and the rules for resolving conflicts between them. The author, Mustafa al-Zarqa, first enumerates four types of probative evidence: 1) confession (iqrar), 2) refusal to take an oath (nukul), 3) clear evidence (bayyinah) including testimony, written evidence, and conclusive circumstantial evidence, and 4) strong apparent evidence (al-zahir al-qawi) which is treated as equivalent to conclusive circumstantial evidence. He then explains that when multiple preferential evidences conflict, juristic preference (tarjih) follows a detailed framework. The first scenario is conflict between two presumptions (aslan). Here, the presumption that is more closely connected to the subject and essence of the dispute is given weight, especially if supported by other rules. For example, the presumption of innocence (bara'at al-dhimmah) is considered stronger than other presumptions. The author illustrates this with the case of a depositary (wadi') who claims to have returned a deposit, denied by the owner, with no evidence. Although the outward appearance suggests non-return (since return is contingent), the essence of the dispute is about the depositary's liability. Therefore, the presumption of innocence in favor of the depositary prevails, reinforced by the rule that a trustee is believed (al-amin musaddaq) until proven otherwise. The second scenario is conflict between a presumption and an indication of the situation (dalalat al-hal). Here, the indication of the situation always prevails because it provides concrete clues that change the original state. For instance, in a dispute between father and son over the father's wealth at the time of spending from the son's wealth, the current financial status of the father at the time of litigation serves as a strong indication. If the father is currently wealthy, this indicates he was wealthy earlier; if he is poor, the presumption of poverty (since wealth is contingent) is followed. This demonstrates the practical application of the maxim 'consideration is given to objectives and meanings' (al-ibrah li al-maqasid wa al-ma'ani). The passage also notes that many exceptions to general rules are actually better placed under a different base rule, highlighting the interconnectedness of legal maxims.

Key Terms
أصل (asl): A legal presumption that a certain state continues to exist until proven otherwise, such as the presumption of innocence (براءة الذمة) or the presumption that a contingent matter is absent (الأصل في العوارض العدم).
دلالة الحال (dalalat al-hal): Indication of the situation: external circumstantial evidence that points to the occurrence of an event, such as a person's current wealth indicating past wealth.
براءة الذمة (bara'at al-dhimmah): The presumption of innocence or non-liability; the legal principle that a person's responsibility is free from obligation unless proven otherwise.
الأمين مصدق (al-amin musaddaq): The rule that a trustee (e.g., depositary, partner, agent) is believed in his statement regarding the performance of his duty, absent evidence of breach.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, when a depositary (وديع) claims to have returned a deposit and the owner denies it, with no evidence, whose statement is given preference and why?
A) The owner's, because the original state is non-return and that presumption prevails.
B) The depositary's, because the essence of the dispute is about liability and the presumption of innocence supports him, along with the rule that a trustee is believed.
C) Neither, because the two presumptions cancel each other out.
D) The judge decides based on the custom of the marketplace.

Key Takeaway
In judicial preference, the presumption most relevant to the dispute's essence prevails over mere outward presumptions, and any clear indication of the situation overrides all presumptions.

Rule of Acknowledgment vs. Evidence: Binding Scope
Analyzing the legal effects of acknowledgment versus evidence

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the maxim 'al-iqrar hujja qasira ala al-muqir, wa al-bayyinah hujja muta'addiya ila al-ghayr' to distinguish when an acknowledgment binds only the acknowledger and when evidence binds all parties, and identify the exceptional case of the father's acknowledgment of dowry receipt.

Core Idea
Acknowledgment binds only the one who makes it; evidence binds everyone because it reveals objective reality.

This lesson examines a foundational maxim in Hanafi evidence law: the difference between acknowledgment (iqrar) and evidence (bayyinah). While both are proofs, they have different scopes. Understanding this distinction is crucial for a mufti when advising in litigation cases. We will explore examples from sale and inheritance, and a notable exception involving the father's authority over his daughter's dowry.

Guided Path
1
State the maxim and its meaning
Write the maxim: الإقرار حجة قاصرة على المقر، والبينة حجة متعدية إلى الغير. Understand its literal meaning: acknowledgment is a proof limited to the one who makes it, while evidence is a proof that extends to others. Note the epistemological basis: acknowledgment is a voluntary admission, while evidence is assumed to uncover objective reality.
This maxim is a sughra (minor) maxim within the Hanafi legal maxims system.
2
Apply the maxim to the furuat (branches)
Work through the two main examples from the text: (1) Sale with a third-party claimant: if the buyer acknowledges the claimant's ownership, the acknowledgment does not bind the seller; if the claimant proves ownership by evidence, it binds the seller. (2) Debt against an estate: if an heir acknowledges the debt, only that heir's share is liable; if the creditor proves the debt by evidence against one heir, the entire estate is liable.
The key is to note the change in outcome when proof shifts from iqrar to bayyinah.
3
Identify the exception and its rationale
Locate the exception: a father's or grandfather's acknowledgment of receiving the dowry of his adult virgin daughter is binding on her, even if she later denies it. Understand the rationale: the father has customary authority to receive the dowry, and whoever has the right to receive has the right to acknowledge receipt. This is not a contradiction to the maxim but a specific application of the maxim that custom is authoritative (al-'ada muhakkama).
This exception does not weaken the general rule; it is based on a separate valid maxim.
4
Evaluate the mufta bihi position
Determine that the general maxim is universally accepted (mufta bihi) across the Hanafi school. The exception is also mufta bihi but limited to the specific context. Note the authority weight: the maxim is from the core Hanafi evidence theory, and the exception is recorded in the Majalla and authoritative Hanafi texts like al-Ahkam al-Shar'iyyah li al-Ahwal al-Shakhsiyyah by Qadri Pasha.
A mufti should cite the general rule first, then mention the exception when relevant.

الباب العاشر : القواعد الكلية في الفقه
لكنه لا يملك أن يقر على غيره ولا يسري إقراره على ذلك الغير
إلا بإنابة منه وتوكيل.
ب - أما البينة فإنها برهان يفرض فيه أن يكشف الواقع ويجلوه. والبرهان برهان بالنسبة إلى سائر الناس كما أن الواقع واقع بالنسبة إليهم جميعاً. فلا يمكن أن يعتبر الواقع موجوداً ثابتاً بالنسبة إلى شخص، ومعدوماً بالنسبة إلى آخر. فلذا كان الثابت بالبينة يحتج به على سائر الناس.
/۱۲/۸۲ - من أمثلة ذلك: أنَّ من ادعى ملكية شيء في يد آخر، وأثبت دعواه بالبينة يثبت ملكه حتى بالنسبة إلى من تلقى المدعى عليه الملك منه.
ويتفرع من ذلك أنه إذا اشترى إنسان شيئاً، ثم ظهر له مستحق يدعي أنه ملك له : فإذا صدقة المشتري المدعى عليه وأقر له يؤمر بتسليمه للمدعي وليس للمشتري عندئذ حق الرجوع على بائعه محتجاً بأن المبيع ظهر له مستحق وأن البائع قد باعه ما ليس ملكه. وذلك لأن إقرار المشتري للشخص المستحق هو حجة قاصرة على نفسه بمقتضى أن الإنسان مؤاخذ بإقراره، فلا يسري إقراره هذا على بائعه الأصلي، ولا يكون حجة ملزمة له.
لكن إذا أنكر المشتري المدعى عليه ملك مدعي الاستحقاق، فأقام المستحق البينة على ملكه للمبيع الذي يدعيه وحكم على المشتري بتسليمه إياه، تكون هذه البينة سارية على البائع وملزمة له ويكون للمشتري حينئذ حق الرجوع عليه بالثمن الذي دفعه إليه دون حاجة إلى إعادة إثبات استحقاق المبيع بمواجهته. ونظير ذلك أيضاً ما إذا ادعى غريم ديناً على التركة بحضور أحد الورثة، فإن أقرّ الوارث بالدين على المورث يؤاخذ بإقراره. ولكن يكون إقراره قاصراً على نفسه، بمعنى أن المدعي المُقر له يأخذ من حصة الوارث المقر لا من حصة بقية الورثة ؛ لأن إقرار رفيقهم لا يسري عليهم.
ولكن إذا أثبت الغريم الدائن حقه الذي على الميت ببينة أقامها على أحد الورثة، وقضي له استناداً إلى البينة فإنه يستوفي دينه كله من التركة كلها ولو استغرق حصص الورثة جميعاً لأن بينته سَرَت عليهم. ويستنتج من ذلك أن الوارث إذا أقرَّ للمدعي بالدين الذي يدعيه على التركة كان للمدعي أن يطلب التمهل في الحكم حتى يقيم البينة. فإذا أقامها يحكم له بها لا بالإقرار وذلك لكي تسري البينة على بقية الورثة غير المقرين، فلا يحتاج إلى دعوى جديدة عليهم يثبت فيها حقه (مع أن الإقرار بالنسبة إلى المقر يعتبر أقوى إلزاماً من البينة حتى قالوا : إنه إذا أقر الخصم في الدعوى بعدما أقيمت عليه البينة يقضى عليه بإقراره لا بالبينة).
۱۳/۸۲ - الاستثناء :
على أنه يستثنى من قاعدة قصور الإقرار ما ذكره الفقهاء من أن الفتاة البكر البالغة إذا أقرّ أبوها، أو أقرَّ جدها عند عدم الأب، بقبض مهرها حين تزويجها، فإن إقراره يسري عليها ويثبت به القبض إن أنكرته هي فيما بعد (ر: الأحوال الشخصية لقدري باشا م/ ٩٥).
وهذا مبني على أن للأب قبض مهر ابنته البالغة بحسب العرف والعادة، وأن من ملك حق القبض ملك الإقرار به، كما تقدم في نظرية العرف.
هذا ما أردنا كشفه من مبادئ نظام الترجيح والإثبات القضائي في الفقه الإسلامي بهذه النظرة التحليلية العارضة بمناسبة القواعد المتعلقة بالإثبات.
(من المقرر فقهاً أن أحد الورثة ينوب عن الباقين في كل ما يدعى به لحساب التركة أو عليها من حقوق كما في هذا المثال (ر: المجلة ١٦٤٢).
Chapter Ten: The Universal Rules in Fiqh. But he does not have the authority to acknowledge on behalf of others, nor does his acknowledgment bind that other, except by delegation and agency from him. B - As for evidence (bayyinah), it is a proof that is presumed to uncover and reveal reality. The proof is proof with respect to all people just as reality is reality with respect to all of them. So it is not possible to consider reality as existent and established with respect to one person and non-existent with respect to another. Hence, what is established by evidence is binding on all people. /12/82 - Examples of that: Whoever claims ownership of a thing in the possession of another and proves his claim by evidence, his ownership is established even with respect to the person from whom the defendant received the ownership. From that it branches: if a person buys a thing, then a claimant appears claiming it belongs to him: if the buyer (the defendant) affirms (saddaqa) him and acknowledges (aqarra) his claim, he is ordered to hand it over to the claimant, and the buyer then has no right to recourse against his seller, arguing that the sold item has a claimant and that the seller sold what was not his property. That is because the buyer's acknowledgment to the claimant is a proof limited to himself (hujja qasira 'ala nafsihi) based on the principle that a person is held accountable for his acknowledgment. So this acknowledgment does not extend to his original seller, nor is it a binding proof against him. But if the buyer (the defendant) denies the claimant's ownership, and the claimant presents evidence of his ownership of the claimed item and a judgment is given against the buyer to hand it over, then this evidence is binding on the seller and is effective against him. Then the buyer has the right to recourse against the seller for the price he paid, without needing to re-establish the claim of ownership against him. A similar example: if a creditor claims a debt against the estate in the presence of one of the heirs, and the heir acknowledges the debt against the deceased, he is held accountable by his acknowledgment. But his acknowledgment is limited to himself, meaning that the claimant takes from the share of the acknowledging heir, not from the shares of the other heirs, because the acknowledgment of their companion does not bind them. However, if the creditor proves his right against the deceased by evidence presented to one of the heirs, and judgment is given based on the evidence, then he collects his entire debt from the whole estate, even if it exhausts the shares of all heirs, because his evidence binds them. From this it is derived that if the heir acknowledges the claimed debt to the creditor, the creditor may request a delay in judgment until he presents evidence. If he presents it, judgment is given based on it, not on the acknowledgment, so that the evidence binds the other non-acknowledging heirs, and he does not need a new lawsuit against them to prove his right (even though acknowledgment is considered more binding than evidence for the acknowledger, to the extent that they said: if the litigant acknowledges after evidence has been presented against him, judgment is given based on his acknowledgment, not on the evidence). /13/82 - The Exception: However, from the rule of the limitation of acknowledgment, an exception is what the jurists mentioned: that if the father, or the grandfather in the absence of the father, acknowledges receipt of the dowry of an adult virgin daughter at the time of her marriage, his acknowledgment binds her and receipt is established thereby even if she later denies it. This is based on the fact that the father has the right to receive the dowry of his adult daughter according to custom and practice, and whoever has the right to receive has the right to acknowledge receipt, as previously discussed in the theory of custom. This is what we intended to uncover of the principles of the system of judicial preference and proof in Islamic fiqh through this brief analytical overview on the occasion of the rules related to proof. (It is established fiqh-wise that one heir represents the others in everything claimed for or against the estate as in this example (see: Majalla 1642).
Source pages p. 1008

Commentary
This passage is a discussion of a fundamental rule in evidence law: the distinction between acknowledgment (إقرار) and evidence (بينة). The author begins by stating that a person's acknowledgment is limited to himself and does not bind others unless he is their agent. In contrast, evidence is a proof that reveals objective reality, and therefore what is established by evidence is binding on all people, because reality is the same for everyone. The author then provides two examples. First, in a property dispute: if a buyer acknowledges a third party's claim to the purchased item, the acknowledgment only binds the buyer; the seller is not bound, and the buyer cannot reclaim the price from the seller. But if the third party proves ownership by evidence, that evidence binds the seller as well, and the buyer can recover the price. Second, in inheritance: if an heir acknowledges a debt against the estate, the acknowledgment only binds that heir's share; the other heirs are not affected. However, if the creditor proves the debt by evidence (even against just one heir), the debt is taken from the entire estate. The author notes a procedural point: a creditor who receives an acknowledgment from one heir may seek a delay to present evidence so that the evidence binds all heirs, avoiding multiple lawsuits. Finally, an exception is mentioned: a father's or grandfather's acknowledgment of receiving the dowry of his adult virgin daughter is binding on her, based on the father's customary authority to receive the dowry. This exception does not undermine the general rule but is based on specific custom.

Key Terms
إقرار (iqrār): A voluntary acknowledgment by a person of a right against himself; in this text, it is a proof that is binding only on the one who makes it, not on others.
بينة (bayyinah): Legal evidence (usually two witnesses or equivalent) that establishes the truth in a way that is objective and binding on all parties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In Hanafi law, if a creditor proves a debt by evidence against one heir, what is the effect?
A) The debt is taken only from that heir's share.
B) The debt is taken from the entire estate.
C) The heir's acknowledgment is required for the debt to be taken from the estate.
D) The creditor must sue each heir separately.

Key Takeaway
Acknowledgment is a proof limited to the one who makes it, while evidence binds all parties because it reveals objective reality; an exception exists for a father's acknowledgment of dowry receipt based on custom.
